THE RED PAGE.
The Years of Hamlet.
J. Le Gay Brereton writes—
Wilson Barrett has been favored with much
easy ridicule because he follows the tradition of
Garrick in representing Hamlet as a stripling.
But, as many critics have taken the trouble to
show, the question of the Prince’s age is not
settled by ready reference to the First Grave-
digger.
* ♦ ♦ «
Apart from his individual genius, Hamlet is a
typical adolescent. Until the deatli of his father
he has lived quietly and self-sufficiently. Then
he is suddenly brought into contact with wicked-
ness, and his ideals are rudely overthrown. His
faith deserts him and he loses heart, because, in
his inexperience, he confounds the general with
the particular. It is a strange and striking fact
to this young undergraduate that ‘ a man may
smile and smile and be a villain. 5 In horror at
his mother’s shame, he loses all faith in her sex,
and treats Ophelia with disrespect. All women
are alike—they delight him not—they are wanton
untrustworthy, shallow, and full of affectation ;
at the best, or worst, were breeders of sinners. He
feels a passionate tormenting joy in unmasking
hypocrisy. He expects to find baseness every-
where, and delights to discover it. Men of reflec-
tive character pass through this stage before they
have reached the age of 20, as a rule. Hamlet’s
swift changes of exaggerated feeling; his love of
bantering and mocking formalism and folly ; his
spasmodic, almost uncontrollable activity under
stress of excitement; his brooding upon abstract
questions of life and death ; his love of the good,
his ineffective reveries, his evasion of the duty
that lies nearest; his abortive tendency towards
suicide; are not these the characteristics'of a boy
of eighteen or nineteen ? Appeal to your own ex-
perience. By the time he has reached thirty, a
man has some kind of foothold on the earth, or
has found for himself wings and dwells among the
clouds ; unless he be an ignoramus or an ass.
• • » *
That Hamleb’s youthfulness is several times al-
luded to in the tragedy is significant, but may be
made too much of. Horatio calls him ‘young Ham-
let to distinguish him from theelder Hamlet whose
form has just passed from sight. And we need
not lay stress upon the fact that the * Ghost 5 ad-
dresses his dutiful son as ‘ noble youth, 5 and refers
to his “young blood. 5 But when Laertes speaks
of the Prince’s love as
A violet in the youth of primy nature,
Forward, not permanent,
we may pause to consider whether the love of the
thirty-year-old is remarkable for precociousness
and spring-time sweetness. And a few lines lower
down we hear that, in the opinion of Laertes, this
vernal trifier. has not yet attained, physically, his
full proportions. (I. 3. 10 sqq. Camb. Sh.). Polo-
nius follows with the advice : ‘ Believe so much in
him that he is young,’ &c. We learn, moreover,
* rV,- Btud y> n £ afc Wittenberg; and in the
Age of Elizabeth boys began studying at the Uni-
versity earlier than they now do. [Bacon com-
menced his college career at the ripe age of
12.] Further, if he is of age, why did he not
succeed to the throne on the death of his father,
since he is, as. Claudius tells us, a popular favorite?
Then, in Act 111., Sc. IV., does the Queen address
her son as though he were a man of 30 ? In the
earliest extant edition of the play she cries sharply;
How now, boy ! 5 In later editions this exclama-
tion is omitted. And now the difficulty does
not appear hopelessly insoluble.
* « * «
Hamlet 5 was Shakespeare’s life-work. Pro-
fessor MaoCallum, upon an impartial examination
of the evidence, is inclined to believe that the poet
may have first taken this subject for dramatio
treatment as early as 1589. We can hardly doubt
that he occasionally altered and added to his great
workuntilsome time after 1611. In the edition
of 1604 and its successors we have the evidence of
the Gravedigger that Hamlet was about 30 years
of age (Act V., Sc. 1., 11. 133-140, and 162-178).
™ ™ s evidence does not appear in the edition
of 1603. The inference is this : Shakespeare, who
seems to have pourtrayed himself more directly in
Hamlet than in any other of his creations, had
originally conceived his hero as a mere youth.
But as he grew older he kept modifying and
altering his masterpiece, and Hamlet developed
with him.. At last an inconsistency began to
manifest itself. With the temperament of 19
years Hamlet had the intellect of a more mature
age. Shakespeare—how many boots would leap
to crush a worm who dared say mistakenly?—
tried to readjust the balance by declaring the
infant prodigy 30, at the same time making other
accordant alterations in the text of the play. In
fine, Shakespeare says Hamlet is 30; dare we
oontradiot him ?
Australian Slang.
Writes “ She-Oak
Some examples of Australian slang, now for the most
part obsolete. ‘ Experiences of a Colonist Forty Years
■A.R O (J. Williams, Adelaide, 1880) has at p. 60, describing
a journey from Fort Phillip to S.A. in 1839, this—
i By this time we had eaten up all our provisions,
even to the last grain of salt, and we were compelled to
live upon what the men called staggering bob —that is,
newly-born calves This without salt, or bread, or any
was sorry food, and never satisfied our
(hunger. The craving longing for fat was most painful;
the moisture that oozed out of the young flesh when fried
was eagerly drunk, and although we were not starving
think we were suffering some of the pangs of starvation
[But isn’t * staggering hob ’ oookney ?]
* * * *
Barr6re and Leland’s ‘ Slang Dictionary,’ vol. i, p. 262
gives cove as Australian station slang for the master
overseer. The word had a great vogue, but is now (?
obsolete. ‘ Experiences of a Colonist ’ (quoted above
gives interesting examples of its use—
The party consisted of eleven men, one boy, an over
seer, and the master, or * cove ’ as he was called by hi
men.—p. 1.
He evidently did not take me for the ‘cove’ or h
would not have been quite so reckless about his ‘ cove’s
property.—p. 45.
(See also pp. 35, 65.) The same book refers (p. 57) to
an Australian use of watchbox —
Near the hurdles there was a s!range-looking affair—i
was not a palanquin, or a gondola, or a sedan chair, anc
yet it had something of the character of each of these
it was built of the ever-present bark, sapling and greer
hide. It rested on four short legs, and there were two
poles at each end which acted as handles for the purpose
of enabling it to be carried about from place to place
This was called the ‘ watchbox,’ in which slept the watch
men near the folds to protect the sheep from the ravages
of the wild dogs. At present the watchman was doing
what was called shifting the hurdles, and his two dogs
were asleep on the blankets in the box.
1 Folds ’ and ‘ watchboxes ’ —the great Australian woo
industry was primitive indeed in the days of * a Colonist ’
* » * a
Break enters into several slang expressions, the
familiar Australian break in (of horses or dogs) signifying
to train, to fit for practical service. But at p. 309 ol
Henry Melville’s ‘Australia and Prison Discipline
(Lond., 1851) I And, regarding a kangaroo-dog which eats
the game it hunts—
A good dog will never overeat, as it impedes his run-
ning. If any break in— that is, after killing, if they help
themselves to the fleah—they are generally shot; because
dogs that will do so will, as a matter of certainty, des-
troy sheep.
Is break in in this sense still used anywhere? Also,
whence comes banjef At p. 16 of J. C. F. Johnson’s
‘Christmas on Carrmga ’ (Adelaide, 1873) it is said—
Banjing is bush slang for sleeping or lying full length
under a tree.
But banje, I take it, had origin elsewhere than in the
bush? Johnson, at p. 21, gives flyblowed as an old bush
synonym for stumped, penniless. At p. 16 he has an in-
teresting definition of whaling—
Men when on the tramp through the Riverina country
often carry a piece of twine and a hook to catch cod or
blackfish. This is termed ‘ Murrumbidgee Whaling.’
» * * *
Melville, above quoted, says (p. 312)
When hard pressed in running by the dogs the mother
dings her joey,’ that is, with her fore paws lifts the joey
or young one out and casts it into or alongside a bush or
shelter of some kind. Here the little one nestles up and
secrets itself ; and if the mother escapes she returns and
takes charge of her little beloved.
The usual term nowadays is JUng or throw, I think ?
"Under Govt. House Patronage.”
Puff.— Yes, sir, puffing is of various sorts ; the prin-
cipal are—the Puff Direct, the Puff Preliminary, the Puff
Collusive, and the Puff Oblique, or Puff by Implication.
These all assume, as ciroumstanoes require, the variou
forms of Letter to the Editor, Occasional Anecdote, Im
partial Critique, Observation from Correspondent, i '
Advertisement from the Party. . .
Sneer.—And do you think there are any who are influ
enoed by this ?
Puff.— O lud, yes, sir! the number of those who unde
go the fatigue of judging for themselves is very sma 1
indeed. —The Critic.
A notable truth this, as Mr. J. D. Hennessey
author of ‘ A Lost Identity,’ has apparently dis-
covered. Bis Puff combines the Direct, the Pre
liminary, the Oblique, and the Puff by Govern
ment House—the last a new variety.—Witness
Sydney daily papers of April 6, 1898—
MR. J. D. HENNESSEY’S RECITAL.
By invitation a large number of ladies and gentleme
attended at the Y.M.C.A. Hall, Pitt-street, last night, t
hear a recital by Mr. J. D. Hennessey from his new nove ,
‘ A Lost Identity,’ which is on the eve of being issuet
from the press. Mr. Hennessey is already known as th
author of 1 The Dishonourable,’ ‘ Wynnum,’ and ‘An Aus
tralian Bush Track,’ and his name may be said to occupv
a prominent situation in the field of Australian writers
His latest work, ‘A Lost Identity,’ is a psychologiea
romance, and although the story opens in England, it i
transferred in the later chapters to Melbourne, and thei
to Sydney. Mr. Hennessey last night recited several o:
the most powerful and interesting chapters, or parts o
them, in an eminently successful manner. He possesses
a dramatic style of a power and order which is non
usually found associated with the purely thoughtful auc
unobtrusive writer of hooks, and especially books which
have a pronounced psychological bearing. Judged,
therefore, from the standard of dramatic criticism, the
recital was satisfactory in every w ay, and from the stand-
point of being a departure, as far'as Australian author-
ship is concerned, distinctly successful.
This entertainment was notified as “ Under the
Patronage of His Excellency Viscount Hamp-
den,” and it was explained that the Gov. re-
gretted being unable to be present. And the next
daily paper’s social news contained this item—
Among the guests invited to Mr. Hennessey’s recital
on Tuesday evening were Mr. and Mrs. Abbott, Mr. and
Mrs. Cartwright *sche, Dr. and Mrs. Bueknell, Mr. and
Mrs. Cow lishaw. the Postmaster-General, Mr. and Mrs.
Gould, Dr. and Miss Jar vie Hood, Miss MTlwraith Harper
Miss Bonny, Miss Essie Bavnton, Mr. Harrison, Mrs’
Hawley, Mr. and Mrs Barr Johnson, Dr. and Mrs. Mailler
Kendall, Major and Mrs. Lee, Miss Rose Scott, Dr. and
Mrs. Scott S&irving, Mr. and Mrs. Harrie Wood, etc.
Then, close on the heels of the Puff, came the book
itself—under the patronage of Govt. House.
* * * .
Well, and why should not an author puff his
wares like a grocer ? The most scrupulous writer—
though he may have qualms—will not interfere to
prevent his publisher’s announcement that the
Saturday Review considers Mr. So-and-So’s work
scrumptious, or that the Times hails in Mr. This-or-
That the genius of the century. Surely the offence
against Good Taste, if offence it be, is not lessened
by being done per deputy? Could one blame
Herbert Spencer if he had earlier turned the tic
of publishing-loss by assiduously declaring
hoardings and in the newspapers that his boo
contained the most valuable systein-of-seien
known to modern philosophy ? Or would no
George Meredith be entitled to public gratituc
if he had earlier emerged from obscurity by dint o
judicious self-praise ? And if it be right for a grea
author to puff himself, is it not right for a litt
one ? If it be right for a publisher to praise 1
author ah, here is Mr. Hennessey’s defenc
Author Hennessey does not puff himself ; he
puffed by Publisher Hennessey. For Mr. He
nessey is a man of many capacities. ]
combines author, printer, and publisher in 1
single person, with most creditable indust
and enterprise. Why reproach such an adtnirab:
example of well-directed energy—under the p
tronage of Govt. House ?
* * * #
For this reason only, that there still lingers
some out-of-the-way places a belief that Liter
ture is essentially an Art, not a Trade ; and th
in proportion as the artist is merged in the trade
man sis result is less worthy of intellige
esteem, less worthy to be called Literatur
Mr. J. D. Hennessey supplies one more illustr
tion of the justice of the belief. The boo
puffed with pulpit eloquence and newspap
paragraphs, under Govt. House patronag
is easily the worst book be has written. In I TI
Dishonourable,’ his first work, Mr. Hennesse'
showed that he had brains, command of languag
and considerable dramatic power and constructiv
faculty. One was glad to say that ‘Mr. Hennesse’
has produced one of the most promising Austr
lian books printed for a long time.’ Then
‘Wynnum’ and ‘An Australian Bush Track
Mr. Hennessey—never very far above the mob
fell flat to the mob’s level. He had apparent'
realised that the multitude of readers are most'
fools, and he fed them on flapdoodle. And i
“A Lost Identity ” he ranks himself definitely :
the order of literary jerry-builders, and—unde
Govt. House patronage—out-corellis Corelli he
self. It is possible, indeed, that his title-“
Lost Identity, by J. D. Hennessey ’’—hints at
darksome secret. The book turns on a quotatio
from Irenaeus—
‘Thereare three of which the perfect man consists, flesh
soul, spirit . . .’ Was it possible, I thought, for th
spirit of man to be divorced from his soul and body anc
to be reincarnated in another body ?
And the hero instances such divorcement i
his own person. Now, admirers of Mari
Corelli have complained that her most re
cent books show a falling-off in bathos—
the telling is less turgid; the personages ar
less utterly unlike anything in heaven o
earth or the waters under the earth, and they
even gabble less fluently than their predecessor
in ‘Wormwood’ or ‘Barabbas.’ Has the spin
of Marie Corelli been divorced from her sou
and body? One remembers with tremors Marie’
own talent for puffery—also under Govt. Hous
patronage : for is it not announced that her book
are the Prince of Wales’s favorites, that the
Duchess of Teok’s pug-dog is called ‘Ardath,
and so on to infinity of notoriety? And yet .
haw fervently would not one pray that this cup
might pass from Australia—under the patronage
of Govt. House ! *
* * * *
A Lost Identity ’ opens with a picture of
man dying in furnished lodgings to the tune of
Sydney Post-office clook. To him enter a child
not more than seven years old, but beautiful as
a dream, * A wealth of golden curls broke away
from the white hat, and rested in rich negligence
upon the snow-white dress in which the child was
clad’—and many worn-out catch-phrases break
away from the web of nonsense, and rest
in rich negligence upon the minor ornaments of
the penny-a-lining style. Says the beautiful-as-a
dream child : That s not my Grandpa Gunnery ’
—and exit. The man pops off to the chime of the
Post-office clock; but Mr. Hennessey is his exe-
cutor. “An old book-case” ... “a secret
drawer” ~ . and—ha ! our prophetic soul!—“a
closely-written manuscript’’—under the patronage
of Govt. House.
* * » ,
The rest of the story is explained by the olosely-
wntten manuscript. Mark Gunnery, an English-
man of fortune, marries Violet Freeman, who
deserts him under the influence of Horace de
Vere. As we know from the Family Herald,
the woman who can resist the influence of Horace
de Vere has not yet studied Paris fashions. A
bad tune follows for Mark Gunnery, and he relates
his agony in the fine rhetorical manner of the
evening papers—
I sat behind that epergne ["epergne” is good—one of
these little touches that show an author’s oulture] petri-
fied. My heart seemed suddenly turned to stone . I
closed the door between us, and, as it shut, the cold
hard, iron door of separation came crashing down be-
tween two human hearts; came down in its appointed
grooves; settled in its iron bed, to abide for ever.
Occasionally the spirit of Corelli gets in even
“chaster ” work—
The dying day sank slowly to its rest, and night spread
its shadowy wings above the breathless landscape, until
darkness had sealed the vision, and with the darkness the
calm, brave spirit of Squire Gunnery passed to rest.
Unluckily, Mr. Hennessey does not often give us
pure aesthetics like this. Only four pages later he
is down to the low, coarse level of
I must have trodden on a banana-skin, or something.
Why will he not realise that in literary matters,
as Stephen Phillips sings, his ‘simple doom is t(
bo beautiful ’ —under the patronage of Govt.
House ?
* • * »
Horace de Vere and Mrs. Gunnery come to Aus-
tralia conveniently ; and Mark Gunnery follows
them. In Melbourne, Mark meets Horace, who
gives him a galvanic shock, and changes him into a
hkeness of his grandfather. Then Mark chases
Horace to Queensland, and pursues him on horse-
back into a quicksand with what looks like the
weakest imitation of a famous scene in ‘Lorna
Doone ’ which could possibly be compassed. In
the quicksand Vere dies to slow music, with
atmospheric effects after the manner of the best
masters. But his evil deeds live after him. There
is extant in Sydney a young man who has
acquired Mark Gunnery’s body atid soul—his
spirit is still attached to the semblance-of-
his - grandfather. This young man behaves
m a dem’d unpleasant way—signing Mark
Gunnery’s signature, spending his cash, and
generally making himself even more objectionable
than a flesh-and-soul on the loose after the doc-
trine of Irenseus might be expected to do. But a
clever doctor joins the real Mark’s spirit to his
flesh-and-soul for one hour only, and Mark takes
revenge by killing a boon companion of the objec-
tionable young man and leaving him to stand the
racket. The body of the objectionable young
man, containing the flesh-and-soul of Mark Gun-
nery, is duly hanged by the neck till it is dead
for murdering the boon companion ; an(l at its
death the soul of Mark Gunnery flies back to the
semblance-of-his grandfather and is reunited with
the spirit. The shock is too much for the aged
body of the semblance-of-his-grandfather, which
dies incontinently to the sound of the Post-
office clock before - mentioned—and the story
closes. Such a farrago of nonsense one is rarely
privileged to encounter: it even gives new views
qf the astounding possibilities of the Corellian
imagination—under the patronage of Govt. House.
» * • *
Some gems of style and matter—
I used to wonder sometimes why he clung to me so
closely, and passed so much time in my company, for I
knew that the hatred between us was mutual. I believe
that there was some physicological cause, which even now
I only partially understand.—p. 82.
My hreast was torn by conflicting emotions.—p. 89.
(Good old “conflicting emotions”!)
I felt stung to madness, as when one sees a thief flaunt-
ing about in some valuable stolen garment.—p. 94.
Ecc., etc.—under the patronage of Govt. House.
* * * «
At the end Mr. Hennessey adds a significant
postscript to the “ closely-written manuscript
Whether a large p 'rtion of his story was the raving of
a madman ; or whether, on the other hand, it was a
glimpse into a hitherto unexplored avenue of the mys-
terious tripartite nature of man, time will show.
But probably Mr. Hennessey did not expect that

THE RED-TRESSED MAIDEN.
Red she is in a robe of sable,
Rosy with pictures and tales to tell,
She is a fairy and yet no fable,
Weaving the dreams that we love so well.
Out in the dark where the night-winds hurry
And the dead leaves carpet the silent bush,
She Hath a charm for the mind a-worry,
For the worn, white face a fresh young blush.
Tell her a story of some love laid in
The grave long since with a maiden white,
She will not taunt you, the Red-Tressed Maiden
. Dressed in her mantle of starless Night.
With fingers potent as rich wine chosen
From dusty cellars where years lie dead,
She melts the ice in the veins long frozen,
And the blood runs chainless, the heart grows
red.
Her ears have hearkened the joyous laughter,
Man-made, maid-lifted, through years and years
To frescoed dome and to smoky rafter,
And tears and tears and ceaseless tears.
Old as the world, and some say older,
Is she, and yet she is young and sweet,
She heard the story the Cave-Man told her,
When hearts were redder and ruder their beat.
No tale so trifling but she will listen :
The long day ended, the day’s toil done ;
Then wheresoever her great eyes glisten
An ancient battle is fought and won.
She is ready to hearken to some chance roamer,
With a lyre on his shoulder, a lilt on his tongue,
As she was of old to the blind-eyed Homer,
Who sang high strains when the world was
young.
On winter nights when the roads are cheerless
And West winds under a frosty moon,
She paints us Summer in colors peerless,
And the broad gold charm of a Tropic noon.
On summer evenings in sylvan places
(The picnic over, and stars in the skies),
She heightens the blush on sun-kissed faces,
And deepens the dream in dear young eyes.
And who is the Maiden ? When Night is about
you,
Bile high the dry leaves and the dead wood, and
so
Make a light for the darkness within and without
you . . .
And now do you see her —and now do you know ?
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HYMN TO EROS.
G Love ! that early found me,
Stay by me to the end ;
When gathering ills surround me,
Be thou my constant friend.
Though past Youth’s fervent fever
And Manhood’s ardour warm,
Dear charmer and deceiver,
. Still—still deceive and charm !
At five, thine arrows sought me,
And pierced my armour’s joint;
But then no pain they brought me,
Unpoisoned was their point.
The years brought arrows plenty,
And venomed barbs they bore,
With love at five-and-twenty,
And love at forty-four.
(A love that grew in secret,
And strengthened with the years,
Died in a wordless anger
And grief too deep for tears.
O, Woman ! Woman ! Woman !
False, frivolous and vain,
A blight on all things human
You’ve cast, since Eden’s stain !)
Yet, Love, though thou hast ruled me
For blessing less than bane,
And many a time hast fooled me,
I would be fooled again !
Now comic, and now tragic,
The parts thou bad’st me play ;
Yet ever, as by magic,
My doubts were charmed away.
Ah ! Many a false illusion
At Wisdom’s touch has flown ;
Of all my youth’s profusion
The years have spared but one !
I hug my gilded fetters,
I joy in Eros’ thrall;
Wealth, Fame, Religion, Letters—
O, Love ! Thou’rt more than all !
And now, if thou should’st leave me,
What comfort would be left ?
If Fate of thee bereave me,
I am indeed bereft.
What joy can Memory find me
In all his musty store ?
A hundred loves behind me
Are less than one before !
Experience near me hovers,
Beside him stands old Time ;
This sneers at foolish lovers,
That talks of Age’s rime.
Bah ! What though heads be hoary,
If hearts are soft and warm ?
O, fly not, Love ! Thy story
Is potent still to charm.
Bid some sweet face enamour,
Some soft lips court caress ;
Cast o’er my life a glamour
To glorify and bless ;
Still visit me—though rarely
Thine angel-wings sweep past;—
O, Love ! that found’st me early,
Desert me not at last !
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
IN RERUM NATURA.
Please do not say we swindled and defaulted.
Look you ! the salty seas our shores entwine ;
The very desert sand is soaked in brine —
Then who can help it, if our mines are salted ?
Q.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will re. urn all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those' sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit

will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 23, 1898.
Queensland’s New Premier.
Another curious shuffle of the political
cards takes place in Queensland, and Thomas
Joseph Byrnes is Premier of the province
where the banana grows. The people, who
have even less voice in the appointment of
their rulers than those of the other pro-
vinces, have hardly heard yet that Tomjoe
Byrnes is the new boss of Kanakaland. He
wasn’t appointed by the people or by the
people’s representatives in Parliament ;
Parliament wasn’t sitting, which was one
good reason why it couldn’t appoint Byrnes,
and the people were sitting—sitting down
and dreaming—wherefore Byrnes practically
appointed himself before they awoke. How
long the new Premier will hold his billet it
is difficult to imagine. The Continuous
Ministry, if it rested exclusively on the
suffrages of the people, would be drawn out
very thin by now, for after eight years of
office any Ministry grows wearisome. But
the people have comparatively little to do
with the making and unmaking of Govern-
ments in the North ; they don’t take
their Ministry seriously—nor their poli-
tics, nor their dismal capital down
on the river flats, where in flood time
it is necessary to shoo the large green frog
out of the second-floor windows. Nobody
really expects a Queensland politician to be
anything but a hot, tired humbug, an open
and undisguised grabber after anything he
can get, and a person who, in his political
capacity, is chronically for sale unfurnished.
And by the' combination of all these circum-
stances Thomas Joseph Byrnes is the new
Premier of Queensland.
* * * *
Byrnes is the third individual who has
led and bossed Bananaland’s Continuous
Ministry. It came into office in August,
1 '?AO, with Samuel Griffith as leading
article and Thomas MTlwraith as sub-
leader, and Byrnes away down in a humble
position at the tail as a species of small para-
graph. MTlwraith had been for years
Griffith’s bitterest enemy, and they had
habitually called each other things which it
would be ungentlemanly to mention. But
the public didn’t take their vituperation
seriously, nor did they take it seriously
themselves. As MTlwraith was elected in
the capacity of Griffith’s enemy, and as the
strong man of the Opposition, he naturally
threw the Opposition overboard and joined
Griffith in the true fashion of Kanakaland
politics. It was generally understood that
he left his party and his alleged principles
through a bitter, sordid, complicated row
over some private share-transactions, and
there were confused tales about how high
politicians were busy cleaning each other out,
and how various members of the patriotic
brotherhood spent their time mainly in
picking each other’s bones. Anyhow,
Queensland took it quite as a matter of
course that MTlwraith should change his
party and his principles, or whatever they
were called, over this mean, private shindy.
Then Griffith, having been elected as a
consistent supporter of white labor, naturally
became a vigorous supporter and advocate of
the cheap mongrel; and the country took
that philosophically also. And when his
Government practically fired Charles Lilley
out of the Chief Justiceship, and then added
£lOOO a year to the salary, and finally
Griffith appointed himself to the billet,
Kanakaland didn’t trouble about the matter
to any great extent.
Then Hugh Nelson, being leader of the
Opposition, quite naturally changed sides,
and dropped his old set of political princi-
ples, and became head of the Government
which he was elected to oppose. In this
matter he merely followed MTlwraith’s
lead, and, anyhow, nobody in Queensland
expects an Opposition leader to have prin-
ciples which he can’t abandon at the shortest
notice. Now Nelson, like Griffith, ap-
points himself to a good, permanent billet
as President of the Council, and Byrnes is
the last remnant of the old original Cabinet
of 1890. Of its seven members, Cowley is
provided for as Speaker, and Tozer, who
might have asked for the Premiership, takes
the comfortable billet of Agent-General
instead. The small man who was at
the dim, remote tail of the 1890
crowd takes the lead, and will, doubtless,
find a permanent billet later on. Unmack,
the slow and weary German whose one great
idea was that 10s a week was sufficient wage
for any white laborer, was too heavy and
protracted to get anything, and is back in
obscurity. Hodgkinson lost his pile and
dropped out, and is now in his old age seek-
ing to repair his fortunes in Westralia.
MTlwraith, after holding office at intervals
for 24 years, was dropped overboard the
other day ; he is old, absent, allegedly
penniless and said to be paralysed, and the
Q.N. Bank trouble had damaged his reputa-
tion so badly that it was easier and safer to
abjure him with horror than to find a billet
for him. At the best, there are not enough
good billets in Queensland to go all round,
and the necessity of taking over Nelson, so
as to keep the Opposition as short of leaders
as possible, made it difficult to find anything
good for all the original seven.
* * * *
Premier Byrnes has decided that he
won’t have a general election and ask the
people to confirm him in his position,
and, seeing how little the people matter in
Queensland, he is probably wise in abandon-
ing that empty formality. The Continuous
Ministry goes on its placid way from year to
year, and the retiring man (when he has
appointed himself to a good, permanent
billet) either appoints bis successor, or
leaves his colleagues to decide on a suc-
cessor among them. If there are two
men with equal claims one gets the Pre-
miership and the other gets a good billet.
The Ministry is in a position to make
these pleasant family arrangements without
consulting the people mainly because there
is practically no Opposition, and there lias
been practically none for the last seven
years. The principal reason why there is no
Opposition worth mentioning is that it has
become a recognised thing for the Govern-
ment to find a portfolio for the Opposition
leader when he shows signs of becoming
troublesome the only two strong men on
the side of the malcontents in recent
years were MTlwraith and Nelson,
and they were provided for in turn,
leaving the party practically headless!
Neither the Government nor the direct
Opposition has any distinct war-cry, or any
definite programme, or any political con-
science, or ally politics worth mentioning
therefore men change sides easily and with-
out fuss, and the country has no deep inte-
rest in either party. Queensland politics
were long ago squashed flat by the dead-
weight of the Q.N. Bank. For years the
Q.N. Bank has depended on the Government,
in an underhand sort of way, for assistance!
Therefore the Q.N. Bank had to respect the
Government’s friends and object to the
Government’s enemies. That institution
spreads all over Queensland, and the
prominent men among Queensland’s handful
of population are, or they were, almost all
either indebted to the bank and liable to be
sold up if they aggravated the dreadful con-
cern, or in want of assistance and liable not
to get it if the Q.N. and they were not on
speaking terms, or were shareholders of the
bank, and had to support the Govern-
ment which kept the bank afloat, and
thus staved off the evil day. The
great object of Queensland politics for
years past has been to keep quiet and do
nothing to seriously embarrass the men who
were keeping the Q.N.B. afloat—with the
public’s money.
* * * *
Therefore things get back to the old point
that, outside the Labor-party, which is
wrecked by bad leadership, there are practi-
cally no politics in Queensland. The Upper
House is a nominee Chamber. The Assem-
bly is elected on a franchise which is
whittled down very low by the simple pro-
cess of striking objectionable persons off
the roll —the striking-off process being
done by a court nominated by the
Government. It is difficult to find enough
strong men who are free from Q.N. Bank
influence to make an Opposition. The
necessity of not offending the bank, or em-
barrassing the Government which supported
it, led to the dropping of programmes and
ideas that might disturb the waters of poli-
tics and possibly make the great rotten
iinancial craft further strain its timbers.
The utter sameness of politics when they
were thus flattened out destroyed public in-
terest. A Ministerialist and an Oppositionist
became so much alike that the public hardly
knew or cared which was which. Being
just about the same, men changed sides with
such facility that there practically ceased to
be any real Opposition. The party system
is based on the assumption that there are
always two distinct parties. It goes badly
when there are two ; it goes worse when
there are three or more, as in New South
Wales ; it becomes absolutely rotten when
there is only one, as in Queensland, and it
is a party without a policy or a political
conscience, and with no future, and
a past which won’t bear speaking
about. If absolute manhood suffrage was
established in Queensland, and a new Assem-
bly was elected, and that Assembly was
entrusted with the election of a Ministry, it
might produce some startling results. But
as it is, Thomas Joseph Byrnes will pro-
bably, barring some unlooked-for cataclysm,
keep in office for a few years, and then
appoint himself to a pleasant life billet, and
nominate his successor when he retires, all
in the good old Kanakaland fashion.
The Defection of Lyne.
It is now little more than six weeks till the
Federal election, and N.S.W. is still the only
province where any organised anti-Federal
party has asserted itself. Melbourne Age,
after a week of hostility and another week
spent in a painful wobble between two opinions,
has practically dropped the subject. Ward,
of South Australia, and M‘Lean, Victoria’s
unpaid honorary gratis Minister without port-
folio, are hardly definite enough to be called an
opposition. But in New South Wales the
strange alliance between the Labor-party and the
most awful variety of Fat still continues. Why
Fat should be hostile is quite understandable.
Why the Labor-party should be bitterly wroth
at a Federal Constitution which provides that
both Houses are to be elected by the people,
members to be paid in each House, no property
qualification either for electors or members, no
plural vote to be allowed in the election of either
representatives or senators, and both Cham-
bers to be dissolvable, and which also includes a
moderately liberal form of Referendum, the Lord
only knows. But a strong suspicion prevails
that the Labor-party in Parliament—as distin-
guished from the laboring masses outside
began to freeze when not one of its nominees
succeeded in getting a place in the Convention,
and froze four feet thick when it sized up its
chances in the scramble for the Federal loaves
and fishes.
* * * *
Lyne, the dreary, plank-like man whose
leadership wrecks the chances of the Protec-
tionist party in N.S.W. Parliament, last week
joined himself to Want, and the Fat end of
the Foreign-trade crowd, and the Labor-politi-
cians, as another advocate of provincialism.
The reasons assigned by the Protectionist boss
evil-genius were mostly (as in the case of Labor-
member Hughes) the awful thing that isn’t|in
the Constitution at all, and the dreadful event
which never happened, and the fact which
might be but wasn’t, and the person that isn’t
there, and other myths. Lyne bitterly de-
nounced the Federal Senate, where each pro-
vince is equally represented, and where, conse-
quently, the small provinces carry more weight
than their population entitles them to, as being
painfully “un-British”:
We are taught that every unit of the British empire
shall be equal in v oting power, and that is a principle
which cannot be scattered to the wind.
Which is tolerably steep as regards an empire
in which about five-sixths of the people are not
allowed any vote at all. And this is only a
fair sample of Lyne’s general inaccuracy.
* * * *
The loudest wail of “William John’s”
oration—which is also the loudest wail of the
Provincial party in general—is that the Federal
union is a fixture for “ all time ” :
Once we step into this Federation we are there irre-
vocably. Let me impress upon these few gentlemen who
say “ No ” that they could not have read the Bill, because
it distinctly says that the compact is indissoluble. There
is only one way you can retrace your steps, and that is
at the point of the bayonet.
And The Bulletin, which has read the Bill,
also says “ no ” ; likewise “ bosh ” and “ rats !”
Nothing in this world is indissoluble. Nobody
is irrevocably anywhere— except in hell, and
whether anybody is irrevocably there even
theology knoweth not; it merely says
it knows, and takes up a collection by
way of changing the painful subject. The
Draft Constitution certainly says that the
union is to be indissoluble. But it
also says that the Constitution can be altered
in any way by a vote of both Houses, and by
the vote of a majority of the States and the
people. Therefore, if the people please, they
can amend the word “ indissoluble” out of the
Constitution at any time, just as they can
amend anything else. So far from the union
being irrevocable, this paper notices with
regret that it is a good deal easier to dissolve
the Commonwealth than to create it. To make
a union of the five provinces requires the assent
of ten separate Houses of Parliament —the
vote of five separate majorities in the five
separate provinces, and not only the con-
sent of a majority of the people voting, but of a
majority which must command at least 142,000
suffrages—Bo,ooo in N.S.W., 50,000 in Vic-
toria, and the rest in Tasmania and YV.A. r lo
break the union requires a majority of only
two Houses, the votes of three provinces instead
of five and a bare majority of the people voting
—no minimum whatever is prescribed. The
man who asserts that the proposed union is in-
dissoluble and irrevocable is either a weather-
prophet or a person who doesn’t know enough
lo put up his umbrella when it rains.
* * * *
Even if the union was indissoluble, which it
isn’t, and could only be sawn asunder by the
bayonet, which is drivel, it would leave things
just about where they are now. The union of
the Riverina (or any other place) to New South
Wales, of the Wimmera (or any other place) to
Victoria, the union of every separate fraction
of every province to the rest of the province, is
also “indissoluble.” According to the British
law now existing, it is decided that no part of
a province can secede, no matter how much its
interests require secession, without the consent
of the rest of the province, as expressed by a
Parliamentary majority in both Houses. And,
as no province ever wants to sacrifice any part
of its territory, there is, by Mr. Lyne’s reason-
ing, no way out here either except by the
bayonet. And this dreadful state of things is
existing all the time without William John
Lyne even noticing the alleged horror which
rages at his door.
* * * •
Like every other anti-Federal speaker up to
date, Lyne raised the old, exploded bogey of
Braddon’s amendment, and told the same old
taradiddle about it in almost the same words as
a dozen other scare-mongers who went before
him. Speaking of the Federal customs and
excise revenue :
All the Federal Parliament or Treasurer can take is £1
in every £4 ; that is to say, that £3 is to be returned to
the states. ... As far as we can reasonably estimate
it, the total expenditure after the first five years of this
Federation must come to £2,500,000 at the least. Now
the sum required to give a revenue sufficient to enable
the Federal Parliament to pay back to the States three-
quarters of the total collected will require a very large
tariff indeed. . . .1 have taken a lower sum,.
£2,250,000, and to raise that under this extraordinary
proposal you must have a revenue from customs and, excise
amounting to £9,000,000.
The Bulletin respectfully asks William
John Lyne if he can read ordinary large print,
for not one word of the above farrago of wild
inferences is in the Constitution Bill at all.
All that document says is that the Federal
Government may keep not more than a quarter
of the customs and excise revenue for its own
use and must return the rest —not that it is to
pay all expenses out of that quarter. And if
it really wants <£2,500,000 a year, or any other
impossible sum like that, it is at perfect liberty
to raise £2,449,000 by stamp duties, and
probate duties, and absentee taxes, and other
direct taxation, and make up the other
£1 by collecting £4 a year altogether in
customs and excise, and keeping £1 out of
the £4. The same baseless ghost is raised
unanimously by every anti-Federal orator
who goes out to influence the people by
telling them about the dreadful thing that isn’t
in the Bill. And The Bulletin has serious
thoughts of calling the next raiser of the weary
old spectre a quantity of rich bullock-driving
vocabulary, so that he can carry his tale into
the libel-court and read clause 87 of the Con-
stitution Bill to the Judge by way of evidence.
* * * •
Lyne’s other reasons appear to be various,
and most of them painfully ridiculous. He
complained that there is no power in the Con-
stitution to make the Federal Government (after
the first five years) apportion the expenditure
and the surplus fairly among the different parts
of its dominions. But when one considers that
there is not a word in the Constitution of any
one of the Seven Provinces to make the Pro-
vincial Governments apportion the expenditure
fairly among the different parts of their
dominions, and that every, or almost every,
other country on earth is in the same situation,
the presumption seems to be that things might
be a lot worse. Anyhow, as Lyne has never
tried to repair this alleged horror in his own
province his sincerity seems very doubtful.
He also wailed that “N.S.W. will have to
keep Tasmania afloat,” which, considering that
N.S.W, has had almost incessant deficits for
ten years or more, while Tasmania is enjoy-
ing a decent succession of more or less reliable
surpluses, sounds rather interesting. He
entered a lament concerning the Victorian
bounty on locally-produced coal—which bounty
isn’t in existence. Also Lyne is dead certain
that N.S.W. won’t federate “unless the
majority of the people are allowed to rule,
which, from a man who voted for the 80,000
minimum and thus squashed the principle of
majority rule in his own province when it
suited his own purposes better that the mino-
rity should have all the show, is very pathetic
indeed.
* * * *
The latest cry of the Cornstalk anti-Federal
party —which cry Lyne takes up with enthu-
siasm, is that the rejection of the present Com-
monwealth Fill really doesn’t matter, and that
it can’t by any possibility mean the abandon-
ment of Federation. If the Bill is ie,acted,
N.S. Wales is t o immediately open negot ations
for another convention, and start the movement
over again more vigorously than ever, and
William John Lyne promises to put his mas-
sive shoulder to the wheel, and shove like
Gehenna. But it is more than probable that
the other provinces would be too sick and dis-
gusted to listen to any more Cornstalk proposals
for years to come. It was at the request of
N.S. Wales, in Parkes’s time, that the first
Federal Conference was held, and a Constitu-
tion drafted ; and that movement fell through
because the Parkes Government broke its pro-
mises, and didn’t even take the trouble to lay


its own scheme before Parliament. It was
N.S. Wales that made the second overtures in
the Dibbs epoch. The third movement likewise
started at the urgent request of N.S. Wales.
Also, N.S. Wales was the only province of the
five which broke faith by raising (this after the
business was tvell under way) the minimum vote
required for the acceptance of the Constitution to
a vastly larger figure, in proportion to population,
than that demanded by any of its partners.
The provinces all fixed the minimum vote which
was to insure the acceptance of the Constitu-
tion at practically the same percentage of the
population—and N.S.W. alone departed from
the agreement. It is also the only province
where the Government is lending practically no
active assistance to the cause. If the 80,000
minimum blocks the way, and the apathy and
faithlessness of the N.S. W. Government wreck
the 1898 Federal movement as they did that of
1891, any appeal from N.S.W. to hold another
Federal Convention isn’t likely to meet with a
very cordial response for years to come. Even
the wild assertion of Lyne, Want and co., that
they have put their whole soul into the Federal
movement, and only want to wreck it so that
they may toil with the fury of an ancient
Israelite building a pyramid at the starting of
another one, reads a trifle thin.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Naval Agreement.
According to the daily press: “Some im-
portant resolutions were passed at the confer-
ence of Premiers which lately met at Parlia-
ment House, Melbourne, and among them a
resolution was carried agreeing to continue the
contribution towards the maintenance of the
Auxiliary Squadron in these waters.” This is
certainly “an important resolution,” and the
occasion is a fitting one to again call the atten-
tion of the people of Australia to the nature of
the “ agreement ” and the benefits they derive
from it. The vessels comprising the Austra-
lasian Auxiliary Squadron were built out of
funds provided by the British Govt., Austra-
lasia agreeing to pay interest at the rate of
five per cent., but not exceeding £35,000
per annum, on the cost of construction.
In addition, these provinces undertake to
pay an amount not exceeding £91,000 per
an. for maintenance of the vessels and crews,
and other charges. At the termination of the
agreement the vessels remain the property of
the British Government. In other words, we
simply rent the squadron, and pay charges
whilst it is supposed to be in our service, and,
at the termination of the agreement, Britain
gets its ships back. One of the conditions of
the agreement is that the ships shall not be em-
ployed beyond the limits of the Australian
station without the consent of the contributing
Governments, so that there may be some chance
that the defence which Australia pays for may
be there when it is wanted. But the vessels
are to be under “ the sole control and orders ”
of the naval commander-in-chief of the
station, and they are commanded by
British officers and manned by British sea-
men. It has often been pointed out by cer-
tain obnoxious persons that these latter cir-
cumstances render the prohibition of removal
from Australian waters a mere farce, because,
if Britain should find itself in a tight place in
any other part of the globe, the “ Australasian
Auxiliary Squadron ” would almost certainly
light out for the scene of action, and leave the
“ kawlinies ” to bustle along for themselves
in the meantime. These persons have been
generally regarded as wicked traducers of
Britain s reputation, and have been reminded
of “British honor” and other guarantees that
her solemn obligations would be kept. But
recently the Melbourne Argus was indiscreet
enough to publish a report of a discussion which
took place on the subject at the United Service
Institution in London, in the course of which
Admiral Bowden Smith gave away the
whole show. The admiral said that he
“did not attach so much seriousness as
Sir John and Admiral Colomb to the clause
limiting the operation of the fleet to colonial
waters. When the Premiers were at home he
had discussions with them all and had brought
away the conviction that they were not likely
to insist upon the observance of the clause even
if they pressed for its retention in the agree-
ment. “ Anyhow,” remarked the Admiral,
“ if war were declared in China and I felt it
necessary to do so, I should take the squadron
to Chinese waters in spite of all the Premiers
of Australasia.” In other words, one Admiral
anyhow doesn’t care a straw for Australia or
the naval agreements, when they conflict with
British interests. And as Australia only wants
the squadron in time of war, the Admiral’s
avowal that he would be prepared to take it
away in time of war is very unpleasant. Before
that agreement is renewed it would be advis-
able to learn how many more Admirals are of
tlie same way of thinking, and how far the
British naval bosses agree with them. The
mere joy of knowing that the squadron can
always be relied upon to be in Hobson’s Bay at
Cup time isn t sufficient equivalent for even the
moderate subsidy paid for its maintenance.
4.
Want and Four Wolves.
The confession of faith of N.S. W. ex-Attorney-
General Want was about the saddest contribu-
tion made to anti-Federal literature up to date.
Jack Want represents the old Cabbage-tree
Mob, that quaint faction which had its head-
quarters at the now-extinct Sydney Reform
Club, and was kept alive mainly by Jack
Robertson’s insane hatred of everything that
lay beyond his own narrow circle in Botany
Bay; and the C.T. Mob’s fixed idea is that every
Australian outside Botany Bay is a being with
horns and claws and a tail, and without soul or
conscience or honor or anything else worth
possessing. W ant’s cheerful denunciation of
all the lederal delegates outside N.S.W. as
wolves ’ was the keynote of his entire shriek,
and that part of it which didn’t consist of
allusions to wolves was mostly hysterics and
utterly unscrupulous misrepresentation—or else
incredible lack of education. Among other
things he told his hearers that Victoria had
‘ so rigged the coal contracts and bounties and
duties, that it is almost impossible for an ounce
from N.S. W. to go in there and compete with
them.” The reality is that Victoria levies no
duties at all on N.S. W. coal, and that it is,
and always has been, so far, the best customer
in the matter of coal that N.S. W. possesses, and
buys nearly 600,000 tons yearly. The next-best
customer, S. A., buys about 300,000 tons yearly.
As these facts can be ascertained by any one who
cares to look up Coghlan’s statistics, the
brassy assurance of ex - Attorney - General
Want’s misrepresentation is something almost
incredible. He warned his hearers over and
over again that the Federal Constitution was a
cast-iron bargain “settled for all time, forever
and aye, which you can never change.” Con-
sidering the elaborate provisions made in the
Bill to enable the people to change the Consti-
tution whenever they want to, this is about on
a par with the coal yarn. He explained how
the other provinces had stolen “our rivers,”
which, seeing that none of the rivers except the
Murray are to be under the control of the
Federal Government, is an assertion that can
hardly be described in polite language. Also,
he announced, on his own unsupported pro-
phecy, that after live years the other provinces
would seize N.S. W.’s share of the surplus and
divide it among them—that there would be war
—that the other provinces would be insolvent
- —that the whole scheme was a deep-laid plot
of the other provinces to plunder “us,” and
similar beer-froth and hysterics. In the mind
of Want and his friends, the fact that every
attempt at Federation up to date has been
commenced by N.S. W. requesting the other
provinces to unite with it is clear proof
that it is the other provinces which are
yearning all the time to unite with
N.S.W. for some base and sinister and un-
speakably gory purpose. Want sees a dark
conspiracy in the fact that N.S.W. will have
24 (or, rather, 25) out of the 64 members in
the Federal Lower House ; this is its fair quota,
but his idea is evidently that the only fair
thing is for N.S.W. to have a majority vote
“on its own,” and the four “ wolves ” should
do with a minority vote among them. And he
desires to know if N.S.W. is going to surrender
its freedom at the bidding of “ 80,000 fanatics ”
—this being an allusion to the famous arrange-
ment by which 79,999 votes for Federation are
to count for nothing, even if there is only one
lone provincial fanatic like King Cabbage-Tree
on the other side. Further, he explained how
the Constitution Bill left with the provinces,
N.S.W. included, until June the power of
granting bounties in support of their local in-
dustries, and how the “wolves” would in-
evitably take advantage of this brief interval
to grant bounties of all kinds on an enormous
scale, and thus blast the industries of N.S.W.
There are now only a few weeks left, and not
one province has granted a single new bounty
yet, or proposed granting one, or even
got its Parliament together so as to en-
deavor to grant one, so this assertion
reads like the most drunken Japanese
prophecy imaginable. Ex-A.-G. Want told his
hearers that, because of his provincial patriot-
ism, he stood before them “stripped of every-
thing but honor.” His billet, his cash, his
clothes, his porous-plaster, his return-ticket for
Japan, his portrait of the pretty brown lady he
saw there—all were gone. And he pledged his
honor about so many gory and awful and im-
possible things which the wolves in the other
provinces were going to do, that his honor is in
great danger of going along with the other
things. This is such a dreadful situation for
any man to be in at the beginning of winter
that The Bulletin feels almost sorry even
for Jack Want.

“ Terewth ” versus Truth.
It is the fate of all creeds to become Respect-
able or to perish. Evidently the Salvation
Army is rapidly becoming respectable, since
the respectable press begins to show a zealous
and Keristian desire to deliver it from evil by
studiously overlooking the “ Capting’s ” little
lapses from grace, and always being otherwise
engaged when a lassie is caught in the slips.
One of those “shocking cases” in which the
great dailies usually take a chastened and
melancholy pleasure, and from which it is their
common custom to draw long moral homilies
for Saturday morning’s sermons, was heard at
a recent Melb. Criminal sittings. The Judge
was Justice Hodges ; the defendant a Salvation
officer in an exceptionally responsible posi-
tion, and the crime was one for which the
domestic press has no name, but it was an
abominable and unnatural horror involving the
degradation of certain youths. Prisoner was
found guilty, and duly sentenced for his crime,
but next day’s papers were dumb about the
unspeakable iniquity of the convict ; there
were no virtuous diatribes about Harmy laxity,
or large, vague demands addressed to nobody
of any consequence, and calling for nothing in
particular, in the interests of cleanliness, religion
and common morality, such as are almost al-
ways printed when a scandal of the kind springs
from a secular institution. In fact, neither
Herald, Argus, nor Age printed one word
about the case, and all the “ ’orrible revela-
tions ” were practically wasted, from the
editorial point of view. The Salvationist first
excited suspicion by his frequent visits at night
to an establishment in the city, always accom-
panied by a young lad. A trap was laid, and
the suspect fell into it, and was caught in
the act by two constables and a third man.
The boy who appeared in the case was one of a
number held in the Salvation reformatory,
and other Salvation officers appeared in Court
as witnesses for the defence, to testify for the
prisoner, and enlarge on the beauty of his
character, and his devotion to duty and holy
living. It is not to be imagined that all the
great dailies shut their columns to the delectable
details of this interesting and instructive case
without a severe professional pang, but the
editorial soul suffered and was silent out of con-
sideration for public morality and the true
faith, both of which, according to newspaper
principles, are especially endangered by the
gutterings of a shining light. However, the
extraordinary unanimity of the press in this
particular instance points to Army influence of
a most strenuous kind. The publication of the
truth with the usual scare-heads must have led
to a serious falling-off in Army receipts, and
The Bulletin pictures Army authorities of
both sexes wrestling with editors in prayer to
add another to the long list of their sins of
omission.
♦
Justice Hood, passing sentence recently upon
three hardened Melb. larrikins, expressed
doubts as to the curative virtue of imprison-
ment in such cases. Imprisonment “ seemed
to him to be only a means of breeding up
criminals to be a terror to the community.”
The young terrors under discussion had been
more or less created by previous prison experi-
ences, thought Justice Hood. It was a pity
they had not been swept off the streets into the
army or navy, or some other school of discipline
before, or immediately after, they committed
their first crime. But seeing that the Law,
which had been wrong in its first treatment of
those youths, was exactly the same Law as the
Law he was now administering, his Honor sent
the three prisoners back for further doses, and
much bigger doses, of the same old bad treat-
ment. Punishment is prescribed according to
past results. If it was shockingly unsatisfac-
tory in its result, the patient gets a larger
bottle of the same medicine next time.
The vicar of a small English town, or village—
South Merstham—appears to have astounded
his parishioners by proposing to establish a
“ public house for the public good,” with capi-
tal supplied by himself and fellow syndicators.
Their interest on the investment is to be limited
to 3 or 4 per cent, “ all profits after this to go
towards objects of local benefit—public hall,
recreation-ground, baths and wash-houses,” &c.
The beer sold at the pub. to be guaranteed
pure, and the spirits at least five years old.
The license and property to be vested in
trustees, and the manager to draw a fixed salary
for running the place as a “ real public house ”
which “ anyone can use without being obliged
to order anything.” The parson’s scheme de-
parts from the Gothenburg System in the one
respect that is likely to commend it to local
capital whilst quieting the alarm of anti-
Socialists. It is doubtless workable at a fair
profit, notwithstanding the pure beer and five-
year-old whisky regulation—spirits in England
being retailed by measure, and not by the un-
certain quantity of a customer’s own helping
from the bottle. South Merstham seems likely
to be known hereafter as the starting-place of
a radical reform in the licensed victualling of
Great Britain, for there is no apparent reason
why the awful vested interests of the almighty
brewers and distillers should not be brought
into harmony with the parson’s innovation.
Meantime, Australia is painfully reminded of
its inability to start such a scheme anywhere.
Absolutely pure beer and aged whisky would
command no sale hereabouts in com petition
with tanglefoot and hellfire ; also, the licensing
laws (of Victoria, notably) are directly opposed
to any improvement in the quality of" the stuff
that now burns the coating off the public
stummick. Melbourne has two huge hotels
(owned by shai’eholders who would insist upon
the sale of reasonably respectable liquor) which
are denied a beer-and-spirit license in the
interests of the poisou trade. Whilst this
English parson is making for holiness in intoxi-
cants, the pietists of big Australian cities are
furious supporters of any licensing law “im-
provement” that protects the vilest old drink-
ing shanties against wholesome competition.
Ben Tillett’s passionate appeal to the workers
of Australasia :
I ask you to think seriously over these industrial pro-
blems ; I ask that, while yet there is a chance, you should
try and capture Parliament, try and use every municipal
power ; you should have your own press, you should have
every political and social organisation that will advance
your principles ; it’s no use grumbling ; to-day is not the
day for pessimistic vaporings ; to-day is the'day to act;
here —this precious hour, here—this precious'moment,
this is the time for the miracle-working ; it does not re-
quire so much brain as heart, but if the heart is put into
it the brain will follow. Let us realise then our possi-
bilities ; let us win the world for ourselves ; and any coun-
trj' which gives to the very humblest of the community
a share in the wealth of the community, a share in its
power of government, a voice in every expression of its
opinion, an opportunity of expressing judgment—that is
the country that will last, and the grandest Federation
of all is the federation of a union of hearts, is a federa-
tion of men and women bound together in the holy bonds
of friendship—of fighting friendship ; that does not care
what people say—that does not want to be extreme.
While a man dies, while a child wants bread, while a
woman is driven to dishonor, while a man is driven to
disgrace, while the cry of slavery goes up, while the
sight of a pinched, wan face presents itself to
one’s view in the street, while slums exist, then
you and I must fight, fight on, fight for our liberties, fight
for our rights, fight for the little children who will come
after us, and let us hand them on—every generation-
let us hand them on the accumulated additions of their
rights to manhood and womanhood, to earn their “ well-
done ” and the tears of fond memory when our ashes
shall be in the grave.
A scene of frenzied enthusiasm followed this
utterance at Melb. Trades Hall, the other
night. The throng was ready to do anything,
promise anything, pledge itself to anything;
but—how,long will it last ?
A week or two before the death, in battle, of
his cousin endowed H. H. Champion with
.£7OOO a year and reinstated him among the
Hinglish toffocracy he got a warm tribute of
praise from Ben Tillett. Evidently dapper
little Ben is a believer in the man who, of yore,
gave much time and money to the cause of
Socialism at ’Ome, and has stuck pretty closely
to his text since he came to earn his bread-and-
butter as a journalist in Melbourne. What
Tillett thinks of Champion is probably the
truth, or very near it. A Tory by birth, fed
in his boyhood with the haw-haw precepts of a
military circle, young man Champion be-
came a Radical through conviction—a gen-
tlemanly “ faddist,” advocating the cause of
Labor versus the tyranny of Cash. He
lacked the appearance of enthusiasm, the cheap
air of sincerity, the magnetic force to move
multitudes, and as a demagogue he was a
respectable failure in London, where well-
dressed and polished professors of Socialism
excite suspicion by their uncommonness. His
chance of achieving an Australian popularity
was lost when he uttered the opinion that the
workers here were lions led by asses. The ani-
mosity which Champion thereby excited m the
leading few has been spread among the many,
who might very well have taken hiß epigram
as a compliment. If his inheritance of “an
estate worth £7OOO a year” be a solid faot, the
brainy and cynical ex-agitator will— The Bul-
letin supposes—return at once to the land of
his proputty and set a decent example to the
average landlord. Prosperity is apt to change
the born toiler into a snob. ’E dunno where
’e are. Champion knows where he was 10
years ago, and is not now likely to fall a victim
to circumstances that make for snobbery. A
converted Tory rarely backslides, a converted
Democrat never.
Vic. Full Court has decided that Panton, P.M.,
is not disqualified for a place on the licensing
bench by the fact that he is “ biassed ” in favor
of the Australian wine industry. Judge Wil-
liams dissented from the ruling, but his Honor’s
remarks were not very impressive. Panton’s
alleged unfitness lay in the absurd supposition
that he was willing to grant wine-licenses
without regard for the character of the
applicants, merely in order to increase
the sale of Australian grape - juice. The
Full Court practically sat upon this supposition,
and ruled that Mr. Panton is not an unjudicial-
minded magistrate where the respectability of
a would-be licensee is concerned. In short, not
a crank. Panton shares in the popular opinion
that wine is good, and that Australia ought to
be a good wine-growing country. His opponents
are the “cranks,” as times go, because they
represent a small minority, which rails against
intoxicating liquor, and regards moderate-
drinkers of wine, beer, or spirits as instruments
of the Devil.
The British Royal Family is rapidly outgrow-
ing its means of living in luxurious idleness.
•The “almost penniless” condition of future-
queen Duchess of York’s father and brothers is
a notorious ease in point, and many more
similar absurdities will arise in the natural
course of things within the next 20 years. As
the family spreads, the system of national
grants is curtailed. British princes and prin-
cesses, presently becoming as cheap as German
serenities, will need to introduce their “ blue ”
blood into the families of successful soap-boilers
and sich. John Bull’s fat-headed reverence
for monarchial Government is not immortal.
At present he prays for “ other members of the
Royal Family ” every Sunday, because the line
was put into the Litany at a time when other
members were few, and, therefore, “swells.”
As soon as they get mixed up with the masses
J.B. will leave them out of his Litany, and once
his loyalty begins to break away from Church
of England religion he will soon let it go
altogether.
“Jack” Want, of N.S.W., in his famous
oration about the “ wolves ” :
When they thought the Bill was safe it was taken to
Melbourne, and the wolves began their game. The whole
body and the meaning of the Bill when it had been
before us was taken out (Howls of “Shame”). In the first
place, out went the Referendum. They knew they had
got enormous voting power in the Senate, and they knew
the Referendum was a check on it. But when they got !
down there they kicked the Referendum out.
This is a beautiful whoop if there was even a
shred of accuracy about it. But the real state
of the case is this : (1) The Referendum w'asn’t
in the orginal Bill at all; (2) The four “wolves”
(Victoria, S.A., Tasmania, and Westralia)
therefore didn’t kick it out. (3) What they
really did was this : Under the scheme as it
left Sydney, in case of deadlocks the Lower
House was to be dissolved first; the Senate,
where the small provinces have their main
strength, was to be exempt from the penalty of
dissolution unless the new Lower House brought
about a new deadlock. And instead of throw-
ing out an imaginary referendum which wasn’t
there, the small provinces gave up this privi-
lege, and agreed that their particular House, the
Senate, should be subject to dissolution on
exactly the same basis as the Representatives—
the Chamber where N.S.W. has the biggest
say. Therefore, as ex-Attorney-General Want,
in his dreadful ignorance and political in-
capacity, deliberately accused the small pro-
vinces of doing what they did not do, and
charged them with tearing large pieces out of
N.S.W., whereas they really made that pro-
vince a handsome concession, and as he brutally
and ungratefully kicked his benefactors and
libelled them as well, and seeing that he has
been “ stripped of everything but honor,” and
a man who has only that left should hang on to
it hard—well, The Bulletin is waiting to hear
Jack Want’s apology.
Allan M‘Lean, the unsalaried gentleman with-
out portfolio, has resigned from the Victorian
Cabinet, and this leaves Turner’s Ministry solid
for Federation. M'Lean’s reasons for being
the one lone provincialist are principally : (1)
That Victoria doesn’t get its fair share of re-
presentation in the Commonwealth Parliament;
(2) that the abolition of border duties will
damage Vic. industries ; (3) that the settlement
of the border railway difficulty will drag the
Riverina trade to Sydney ; (4) that the new
Victorian coal industry will be wrecked in the
interests of N.S.W. ; (5) that the power of
granting bounties has been unjustly taken
away from the States. The shriek of the pro-
vincialist on the northern side of the Murray,
on the other hand, is that N.S.W. is the only
province which suffers from lack of adequate
representation in the Federal Parliament;
that the Riverina trade is going to Melbourne*
or Adelaide, or Gehenna—principally Gehenna ;
and that the Vic. coal industry is getting all
the advantage, and the States’ power of grant-
ing bounties hasn’t been taken away near so
decisively as it should be. This sort of thing
makes the provincial gospel about as clear as
the original site of the Garden of Eden
Anyhow, Allan M‘Lean, though he is
alleged to carry a certain amount of
the Gippsland vote in the pocket of his
kilt, is no serious loss to the Ministry.
He was various things in tire Mitnro and
Shiels Cabinets, and it was a sort of inherited
tradition that the respectable oldjnatt should
have a show when Turner formed the Ministry
m 94. So he, with Vale, Pratt, David
Coutts, and Samuel Williamson, were all
made honorary Ministers with nothing to do-
just as fcsiMON Fraser, wholesale tailor Stuart
Peacock, and Cornelius Job Ham were en-
gaged as the unemployed and unpaid tail of
Munro’s Government.

St. George’s Hall Professor Kennedy.
An unfortunate horse accident befell
Miss Edith Motley on Sunday last, and
has seriously disarranged the plans of the
criterion management. The “ Marriage of Con-
venience had to be replaced on Monday night
by the “Tree of Knowledge,” the hole being
dug at short notice, and the brief shrub shoved in
hurriedly, with very little time to apply the bone-
dust of careful rehearsal around the roots. The
fact that under these painful circumstances the
Tree ’ was played admirably and without a
hitch says a great deal for the Cartwright co. At
the same time it is impossible to disguise the fact
that the herb tottered a good deal in the estimation
of a rather thin house. It is not comedy or drama ;
it isn’t exactly tragedy, nor is it exactly Tan-
gueray. It is clever and almost brilliant, and it
is true to nature, inasmuch as it is utterly un-
finished. Nature never really finishes anything.
* * * 9
There are seven leading characters among
the various branches of the “Tree,” and five of
them pass away unaccounted for. The tale con-
cerns a young man who once had a Mrs. Tan-
queray and is blighted by the remembrance of
her, and won’t marry the pretty girl who loves
him because the old episode is on his soul. His
Mrs. Tanqueray turns up as the wife of his best
friend, and when the friend and his father are
ruined she elopes with his next best friend, and the
young man marries the pretty girl after all. Story
Gofton, who was lately wasting his talents as a
giant in a pantomime, appears as a military
gentleman who is also in love with the girl Being
refused, he drifts through the back-door, and the
rest of his blighted life passes out of the story.
Ihe lady-part of the audience mostly falls in
love with Charles Thursby, who does forcible
work in a rather difficult character as the man
who marries the unreformed Tanqueray. When
he and his father are broke, and his Tanqueray
leaves him, the pair step out into obscurity. The
father has no very definite exit, and the
son is last seen asleep on a couch with
his mother’s jewellery balanced on his
stomach where the faithless wife left it when
she fled. The next best friend and the wife both
disappear. Presumably, she becomes old and poor
j battered and badly-painted,
and the rain and the wind beat on her tangled
back-hair and her frescoed eyebrow; but all these
things are left to the imagination. The story is
picturesque and cleverly told, but the public wants
a lugger and more glaring Tree with a larger veget-
i j- ** on more conspicuously. All the
leading people do their work in a finished and
artistic fashion. Miss Beatrice Lamb is hardly
youthful enough for the adventuress, but her idea
of the character is excellent. Miss Gethyn Dar-
ragh’s first Sydney appearance goes remarkably
well, and Cartwright does all that man can well
do for the blighted gentleman that used to have a
Tanqueray. But the brutal public always wants
to see half-a-ton of retribution fall publicly on
somebody, and to hear the bones squash, and
they don’t squash. It’s no good giving caviare
to people who eat with their knives.
** * *
The Bulletin desires to cordially recom-
mend Phantoms ’to all -lovers of a good, gory
shocker- one that shocks all through and keeps on
the straight path of bones and blood and Ker-rime
without deviation. The latest Sydney Lyceum
play commends itself to this paper’s soul. Barring
the fact that it goes off its head in the last
act and takes to doing comedy acts on an irrele-
vant bike, it is a consistent affair with an object in
vi©w, and it climbs over much dead body to arrive
there. There are two villains-no one villain
could drag all that load of sin right through four
a t the end of Act 11. the new villain
kills the old one, and thereafter personates him,
though considerably discommoded by the pale
green wraith of the old villain who will keep
appearing to him in his dreams as he sleeps in his
ill-gotten four-post bed, with the shiny pot-hat,
which was bought out of the wages of iniquity,
hung on the nail behind the door. The tale in-
cludes a safe-robbery; another robbery, combined
with murderous assault on a postman ; the seduc-
tion of a nice girl; the registration of her
child in the name of the heroine, whose repu-
tation is consequently knocked endways; the
seduction of another girl; a marriage, in
which the loathsome party in faded whiskers
borrows the hero’s name so that he has a very
tough charge of bigamy to answer; and the
shooting of the first villain, who drops down a
mine. The second villain goes down and robs the
body, and finding his old partner still alive, wal-
lops him fatally on the skull until he is dead. He
also lays a charge of murder against the hero;
swears false affidavits ; abducts the nice girl who
had the child ; tries to steal the heroine ; throws
another child over a cliff, and does evil too
frequent to enumerate.
* * • *
The path of the two partners is piled high
with bones, and the ill-gotten top-hat of the second
sinner is just visible above a sea of blood. Also,
in the silent night the ghosts of his evil past
gallop on him in squadrons and leave their foot-
marks all over his night-shirt. If it is worth
while to write a horror at all it is worth while to
write a big, artistic horror, and The Bulletin
rather likes “ Phantoms,” partly because there is
a certain amount of art in it, and partly because
of its unmitigated wickedness. It would like it
still better if Miss Therese Lindahl did her rather
clever study of a street-arab in her bare feet, in-
stead of disguising the character in white stockings
which make a thin pretence of being feet and aren’t.
An arab in stockings is an anomaly, and this
writer has no reason to suppose there is any-
thing the matter with Miss Lindahl’s feet that
they shouldn’t be exposed. Among the rest,
Charles Field, Stanford, John Forde, Diver,
Nunn, and Miss Inez Bensusan are all more or
less deserving. J. H. Nunn, who has been absent
from Sydney for some ten years past, does a very
clever streak of comedy as a base solicitor, and
koide, as a doctor who badly requires a three-
penny hair-cut, is another person who is re-
freshing to the soul. Diver and Stanford—
especially the former—bear the heat and
burden of the shriek with great ability. Miss
Bensusan, as the girl who has the child she
oicln t ought to, and who is abducted while in
the last stage of consumption, is bright and
natural, and looks consistently pretty, and repents
beautifully in a loose frock and a summer hat.
Miss Maud Williamson is reasonably good in her
acting capacity, and in anxious moments she lifts
up to a quite unnecessary elevation a voice whose
top-note has been wrecked by years of continuous
calling for help. The person who was disrespect-
fully mentioned in last drama may be passed over.
Another man has arrived and gone away again
since then who is a lot worse.

A matinee benefit for the good object of
raising funds to help old Lachlan M'Gowan
gently down the vale of life takes place at Her
Majesty son Friday. All the companies now in
bydney are assisting in the work. Lachlan
M‘Gowan is very ancient now, and is so ill that
his exit cannot be far off. A comparatively
moderate sum will keep the medicine-bottle re-
plenished as long as the veteran will require the
contents thereof, and leave something over for his
wife and family, and The Bulletin trusts that
the amount and something over may be forth-
coming.
** * *
The “ Cotton King ” has apparently
settled down for a long run at Sydney Royal. The
scenery and Bland Holt’s reputation, and the
nendish atrocity which is nearly being perpetrated
with the lift, evidently go a long way to cover
deficiencies. This paper notes with regret, how-
ever, that Miss Watson isn’t in any real danger of
being squashed in the lift after all. That lady is
artfully entrapped and shut in, and, while she
shrieks in her cage, the great machine gradually
comes down on her head to sit on her and flatten
her out. Baker rescues her by superhuman efforts
at the last moment—and then the lift doesn’t come
quite down to the floor after all. If she wasn’t
rescued in time there is room for her to faint on
the floor and lie there with the lift on top of her
quite uninjured—at least, it appears so. This
spoils a beautiful illusion, and The Bulletin
ceases to sympathise with Miss Watson’s suffer-
