Haimston s Circus has put up a new pro-
gramme, which is, if possible, even a little more
extensive than the last one. The large mother
elephant, who has been out of the bill hitherto,
takes a hand, and a lot of new and commendable
horse is introduced. There are 18 separate acts
in the present bill, and none of the old acts are
rung 1 in a second time. Business is respectable,
and but for extreme competition in the show busi-
ness it would probably be more so.
** * *
Henry Harwood, in days of yore a success-
ful theatrical manager and a favorite actor until
quite recently, died last Sunday of heart-disease,
induced by the Smellbourne bank-failures. All
the money he had saved during his long and honor-
able career on the boards was lost in the boom-
smash, and his last years were full of sorrow. Old
identities speak admiringly of Harwood's early
triumphs as a humorous actor—mostly in Mel-
bourne—and nearly everyone whose “Austra-
lian experiences ’’ at the theatres date back
to fifteen years ago has laughed immoder-
ately , a k most natural of comedians.
The writer will always swear by Harwood’s short-
sighted Duke in “Tambour-Major” as the fun-
niest stage character he ever saw. The humor of
the part was produced without any apparent
effort; one accepted the “dear Duke” as a ludi-
crous reality. And so with Harwood’s later pic-
ture of Uncle Cattermole in “ The Private Secre-
tary.” Cattermole lived on the stage, and will
never die in the memories of many thousands of
Australians until they in their turns have gone
where the good actors go.
Nothing ever succeds at Melbourne Bijou
—except success. A season of very bad business
with the Cartwright comedy co. was followed,
after a brief interval, by a considerable rush of
public to an amateur performance of “Maritana.”
The old opera drew good houses for seven nights.
True the prices were popular, and charitable and
friendly patronage accounted for some portion of
the attendance, but, after making due allowance
for favorable circs., there remains the fact that
“Maritana” attracted hundreds who wouldn’t
roll up to a high-class comedy co. It merely
proved, said somebody, that Melbourne is hungry
for tuneful music. Anyhow, what’s the matter
with the Bijou when the bill is suitable to the
occasion ?
** * #
The Trin. Col. production of “Alcestis”
at Melb. Town Hall occurs on June 16th, and the
arrangements for a gigantic show are almost com-
plete. “ Alcestis as everybody ought to know,
but doesn’t—is a light tragedy from the pen of
Euripides, deceased, and nothing short of heroic
treatment could pull it through. Hercules figures
as a principal character. Hercules sober is a sub-
ject that requires vast surroundings, and when he
goes upon a mythological burst, as in the drama
aforesaid, he wants more space than usual.
Therefore, it has been decided to extend a stage
to the full width of the Town Hall, and on this
fine commanding site architect Annear will build
an ancient Greek “set” forty feet high. Also
“Alcestis” having been furnished with music (said
to be very imposing music) by Prof. Marshall
Hall, will carry an orchestra of 60 earnest instru-
mentalists, or more, and a chorus of 200 voices,
supplied, by the local Liedertafels. The chorus,
divided into two armies, will sing in ambush at
either end of the stage, and the auditorium is to
run, as nearly as possible, on Greek lines. In
short, the Trinity College management, after duly
counting the cost, is prepared to make “ Alcestis ”
the show of the Australian century—in its way.
Meanwhile, the cast of characters—especially Her-
cules—must put forth strenuous efforts to tackle
the lofty theme in a 40ft. spirit, and acquire the
art of seeming excited on a large scale.
*# * *
The greasy adulation bestowed upon plays
and players by a servile daily press in Sydney is
accountable for the wearisome pertinacity with
which managers “demand” what they call “fair
treatment” in Melbourne papers. The notion
that anything less than fulsome flattery is unfair
treatment has been soaked into the managerial
mind by the everlasting drip of fat notices and
sweet paragraphs from Sydney writers, who hang
around the theatres begging for small items of
news wherewith to puff the show. Every time a
reasonably just criticism appears in a Melb. organ
(which isn’t often) a furious manager or his agent
rushes round to the editor, or the sub., and wants
to know the reason of this outrage, not because
he can’t admit the truth when he sees it in print,
but because he has not had to endure the
truth in Sydney, and doesn’t mean to put up
with it elsewhere if indignant howling can work
his point. As a rule his howling docs work the
point for the next few months. The tired journal
throws “good ” notices to him to keep him quiet,
though it seldom feeds him with “ locals ” on the
dear old Sydney scale of puffery. Managers all
round are dissatisfied with the comparative stand-
offishness of Melbourne’s press deadheads. They
want greasier grease, and more of it. Also, the
malcontents can t understand why Providence
should permit the all-powerful Age to remain
under the control of a tough, old, unsentimental
exclusive Scotchman with an awkward habit of
taking his young theatrical critics off the job if
they show unmistakeable symptoms of having
been “ nobbled ” in any way.
The Sundry Shows of Melbourne, as the bard is
bound to say,
Have altered very little since the other Saturday,
So he bunches them together in a careless kind of
way.
The Royal circus-drama still is lighted up by fire,
Whilst a lady in a nightgown walks the telephonic
wire ;
And natty Amy Gourlay, in a naughty, naughty
part,
Persuades a youthful Bertie to accept her as his
“ tart,”
Which proves that there is virtue in a damsel’s
going wrong,
If she sticks to crimson stockings, and can sing a
comic song.
We prefer the fallen sister who enjoys a little joke
To the tall and tearful sinner in a melancholy
cloak, J
Who tells her base betrayer that he promised to
be true ;
She’s an old, exhausted subject; but the other girl
is new.
We likewise love the uncle of those Babies in the
Wood
(Oh, by the way, Miss Millie Young is playing
Robin Hood),
1 or the Baron’s legs are evil, and his nose is red
with crime,
And his soul is steeped in liquor, and his hiccups
are sublime.
The show at the Princess’s is a mighty fine affair :
It fills the bill for splendor, with a little bit to
spare.
The Wedding Breakfast tableau is a smasher, no
mistake —
But why not put the Baron in possession of the
cake ?
Another tuneful nunky, with a face as black as
coal,
Is singing at the Halek, on the topic of his soul,
To a crowd of lazy niggers, till he dies upon the
floor,
And the angels waft him upwards to the blessed
Golden Shore.
Will they do as much for Kennedy , now raking in
the spoil
That comes of treating “ subjects ” to a pint of
castor oil ?
Do you think those gentle angels for Professor K.
would call,
If he perished (like Svengali) in St. George’s
crowded Hall,
After snapping of his fingers at an epileptic wight,
Who, having chewed a candle, must pretend to
feel “ all right ? ”
Tis a somewhat solemn question, and we warn
the mesmerist
To prepare to meet a Someone who has got him on
the list.
At the Rickards institution, on their horizontal
bars,
Three great gymnastic Hegelmanns are recognised
as “stars,”
And the people marvel muchly when they hear the
Grammaphone
Tell a funny little story on its funny little “own.”
All the working-men in Melbourne will be making
a display
When The Bulletin is published; ’tis the annual
Eight Hours’ Day ;
And we, who chant this drivel, will be perched
upon a car,
Our toil-worn fingers resting on the strings of a
guitar,
And a banner waving o’er us, labelled “Rhyme-
smiths ” —which we are.

Sweet miracle of common-sense,
You know (you say) that Wilson B.
Could with your services dispense.
Forbid, O Lord,
Such things untoward !
But whisper, Beauty, what, think you,
Would bring that change ? Ah, won’t you tell ?
Unfitness? No, we think ’twould be
Because you p’r’aps might act too well.
O, pretty Maud ?
Demos : “I put a crude sketch of the ‘ Eureka Stockade
play in the Imperial Review, some years ago, with Lalor,
Vern, Black, Carboni, Raffaele, &c., but it lacked a proper
dramatic nidus. The last scene was one of the ladies’
committee, at needlework on ihe Australian Flag of the
Southern Cross. A sort of prologue introduced Governor
Hotham and Jacky Tommy Smith, the Melb. Mayor.”
J.H.B. : “ Just landed in Hongkong. Annexations by
Russian, French and German in China are mere flea-bites
compared to the Brough Co.’s absolute seizure
of this place. They are playing to crowded houses
every night which must be cheering when they
think of the empty houses they.have played to at times
in Australia. Mrs. Brough is greatly admired, Miss
Hardy loved, and the rest of the co. thoroughly appreci-
ated ; but Bob Brough is fairly worshipped. Sassiety has
taken him enthusiastically to its exclusive bosom in pri-
vate—tiffins him at its high-toned club, and gives garden-
parties where the females can get at him ; and each night
on making his first entry the house simply rises at him
open-mouthed. Hamilton, the elephantine, tells me that
nightly they turn away some 201) applicants for dollar
seats.”
Haresfoot: “ Harwood’s real name was Biggs. His
father was well-known about Melb. in the last generation.
Harwood used to say his stage education began under
Brooke, in S} T dney. Cathcart is the only other man ever
known in Australia with such a stage record of versatility
as Harwood’s, if indeed he did not go beyond Cathcart, for
Harwood acted everything except the tip-top roles.
Tragedy, heavies, juveniles, light-comedy, low comedy,
old men, eccentrics, burlesque, he did all in his time, a
perfect manager’s man. Here are some of the parts he
sustained : Marquis de Treval, in ‘ Lavater,’ a subtle
piece of acting, not in the comedy line at all; Henri de
Sartorys, in ‘ Frou-Frou,’ a dignified leading man ; the
villainous hero in Wilkie Collins ‘Man and Wife,’ very
able; Cassandra, in Arkhurst’s ‘ Siege of Troy,’ the
height of burlesque ; Mo. Davis, in ‘ Flying Scud,’
the typical Jew of the Boards. Evidently Har-
wood had Jewish blood in his veins. His ordinary
speech had a suspicion of the national lisp. In ‘ Hamlet ’
he acted, at various times, the Ghost, King, Polonius,
First Gravedigger, First Actor, and Osric. His Macduff
was a fine, heart} 7 performance. I have known him to
double the Ghost and King' in ‘ Hamlet.’ He did this,
when required, with the same readiness as he displayed in
taking the clown in the pantomime at Melb. Royal when
Charles Young turned rusty. So good was he that he
acted clown all through the run of the pantomime, which
was ‘ Valentine arid Orson,’ with Sir William and Lady
Don. In addition, he played the very heavy character of
King Pippin, in the opening. Nevertheless, Harwood
was not in the very first line as an actor. Cleverness
summed him up. The last parts he acted were
Cattermole (a fine performance) and Dogberry. His
latest appearances were under the pressure of necessity.
His disease of the heart must have meant broken heart.
Ever frugal, he had made a nest in his delig-htful villa of
Montreux, at Heidelberg, near Melb., but the burst Banks
of the Boom ruined him. Strange to remark, he did not
evince the elasticity of Coppin. He seemed to give the
wicked world best. A proud spirit was quenched.
Steeped to the lips in the stage, Harwood was j-et always
a well-informed citizen. His first Melb. mark was as
‘Moveonsir, a Policeman,’ in the Royal Christmas Panto-
mime of ‘ Whittington,’ 1857. No man was ever so much
identified with the Melb. pantomimes. He was with Barry
Sullivan throughout Sullivan’s glorious management in
Melb., 1863-5. After this, matters were uncertain, till
1867, when the firm of Lambert, Vincent, Harwood,
Stewart, Bellair, and Hennings took the Royal,
where Harwood long had the reins. Various changes
brought in Coppin and Greville. It was a great dis-
appointment for him to be o usted from the Royal, but
yet, on the whole, he kept piling up his bank-account,
the industrious ant laughing at the grasshoppers. For
the last 20 years he has done nothing of such note as in
the previous 20. Although he had his bit of capital, it
was never enough to cope with rivals who entered the
field. His first wife was Miss Margie Chester; his
second, and widow, Miss Docy Stewart, whom he married
as the widow of Mr. Collins. To crown his achievements,
I forgot, to add he act ed Mazeppa, at Geelong, on horse-
back. He used to tell of how he was once working, as a
young man, at a sawpit in the bush, and saw a troupe of
comedians pass in the coach, all so jolly, but he had no
idea he would ever become a mummer himself.”

COLUMN
Namoi: Your money is, of course, a deposit
made since the 1893 reconstruction, else it wouldn’t be
withdrawable. In case of liquidation or other smash, the
deposits made since the ’93 reconstruction have to be paid
before the old reconstructed depositors can get anything.
Therefore your position is just about as good as anything
could be. All the capital and all the £5,000,000 or more
of reconstructed deposits would have to be entirely lost
before you could lose a penny.
* » » »
Policy-holder : You will find “ stationery ”
among the assets in the balance-sheets for 1894, 1895 and
1896. Writer hasn’t looked further back. Most other
offices simply lump this item in with “ furniture, fittings,
&c.” Also, the concern in question is a mutual affair ;
every policy-holder has a vote ; the books aren’t “ closed
when an election is on ” (what books are there to close ?),
and you are obviously mixing it up with a certain pro-
prietary concern where voting is confined to shareholders.
* * * *
Speculator : There is no real “ sudden slump; ”
though quotations were recently run up there were no
sales at the alleged rise. In your place we would hold
and chance it It is a pretty bad gamble, but if you sold
you would get a mere nothing, and if the institution
broke suddenly you might find yourself placed on a “B ”
list of contributaries and have to pay just the same.
And present quotations are so unreliable that you might
have to pay the alleged buyer to take your shares away.
* * * •
As Victoria and Maoriland are both going on
the loan-boom once more, Tub Bulletin again suggests
the awful necessity for a law to strictly define and regu-
late the loan business. At present the situation is this:
There is no limit to the amount which may be borrowed
each year. There is no limit to the amount of debt per
inhabitant which may be accumulated (there is no limit
anywhere else either, but as these provinces have bigger
debts in proportion to population than any other place on
earth they badly need to lead the way in this respect).
Also, there is no restriction whatever as to the purposes
to which borrowed money may be applied. And this is
the biggest disaster of the iot—especially in Tasmania
and N.S.W.—for though every politician is more or
less shocked at the dreadful way the last loan was spent,
no one dares to rise up and say that the proposal to spend
a part of the next loan on gaols, and roads, and bush-
clearing, and other unproductive works in his electorate
is utter profligacy.
If the people of the Seven Provinces are too
much gone in the knees to pay their way, as the degener-
ate Britisher does, Tim Bulletin again suggests these two
much-needed reforms : (1) That the purposes for which
loans may be raised hould be strictly defined by Act of
Parliament, and should be confined to directly reproduc-
tive purposes ; all the roads, bridges, lock-ups, repairs,
and other non-paying works which are now put into the
loan estimates to relieve the revenue being shut down
upon remorselessly. (2) The present amount of debt per
head should be the maximum. If a province owes £4O
per head now, and its population increases 10,000 in the
course of a year, that would allow of another £400,000
being borrowed, but no more. It would be a great deal
better if borrowing was still further restricted so that the
amount of debt per head would show a small annual
decrease, but probably this is too much to ask for all at
once. Anyhow, the loan biz badly wants some kind of
regulation It is about the only thing in Australasia which
isn’t regulated, and why the community which has
statutes to deal with dogs, rabbits, burrs, bye-roads,
germs in fruit, and so many other small items, should
leave this vast question to run itself is beyond under-
standing.
There is only one visible reason why the loan
disease shouldn’t be regulated by law. As matters stand,
every politician gets all the roads, and culverts, and wood-
blocking, and miscellaneous grants and repairs he can for
his electorate out of the loan-vote, and thus earns local
popularity. He also denounces in vague general terms
the way loan-moneys are spent, and gets more popularity.
He also yells for economy in the abstract, and gets still
more popularity. The Ministry buys support by dis-
tributing all manner of small grants out of loan-moneys ;
and earns more support by reducing taxation (which is
done by charging ordinary expenditure to loans); and
also gets a lot of popularity by pointing to its surplus
(made by charging current ex’s to loans), and by advo-
cating economy, and by telling about the shameful things
the last Ministry did with the loans. If anyone could
shove as far as the committee-stage a bill to define what
is legitimate loan expenditure a lot of these soulless finan-
cial hypocrites would have to get down on one side or
other of the fence, and explain themselves.
• * * •
During 1897, over .£BOO,OOO passed through the
Maoriland totalisator, and the State’s miserable 1J per
cent, brought £12,079 to the Treasury. There is no
earthly reason why the tote should not pay 3, or even 4,
per ceut. At 3 percent., with the larger population of
N.S.W. and Victoria, it should be worth £40,000 a year in
either of these provinces. At 4 per cent, it should run to
£50,000. This is one of the unexplored sources of revenue
which might be set aside to form a Public Works Fund, so
that the harmless, necessary bridge and culvert and rail-
way could be built without handing a load of debt down
to posterity.
* * • »
The Royal Bank (Melbourne) continues to im-
prove steadily, as shown by the profits for the last eight
half-years :
Sept.,’94 .. £2875 Sept.,’96 .. £2044
March, ’95 .. 2136 March, ’97 .. 3398
Sept.,’9s .. 2038 Sept.,’97 .. 3625
March, ’96 .. 2099 March, ’9B .. 3864
The last half-year is thus the best which the Royal has
had since the great bank smash year—the profits now
equalling £5 3s. per cent, per annum on the reduced
capital of £150,000. A 3 per cent, dividend for the half-
year absorbs £2250, and the remainder, with previous
balance, leaves £4138 to carry forward. 'The Royal has a
small, steady business and a strong cash reserve
(1-102,847 —equal to 32 per cent, of all liabilities to the
public The profits show a remarkable revival, and it
seems that this little institution has fairly struggled
round the corner—the present corner that is. But in
view of possible future ones, it is about time the Royal
started a new reserve fund. The vanishment of £170,000
during the late financial cleaning up was a big loss, and
the work of making it good should be tackled in a vigorous
spirit. The great mistake made by the banks which wrote
off large boom losses was that none of them (save the
Royal of Queensland) definitely hypothecated a fixed pro-
portion of their future earnings towards rebuilding their
vanished reserves and closing up the big gap in the capital
account. The Melbourne Royal could easily afford to ear-
mark one-third of its earnings for this purpose. Also, it
could easily afford to invest ,-630,000 of the needlessly
bloated cash reserve in State securities, and thus add £9OO
a year to its takings.
* * * *
The British debenture-holders of Mildura now
report that they will sue the Vic. Government for some-
thing tremendous in the way of “ compensation.” Which
is so very like the usual Bull financier that it is only what
might be expected. Vic. Government granted the
Chaffeys a large tract of land on condition that certain
specified irrigation works were constructed. Some of
them were badly constructed, and the rest were not con-
structed at all. Then the Chaffey co. went broke under a
multitude of lamentable circumstances, and their grant,
according to law and equity, reverts to the Vic. Government
for non-fulfilment of the conditions. The Government,
however, instead of forfeiting, vested the land in trustees,
and advanced money to keep the enterprise afloat, thereby
giving the debenture-holders a chance of recovering their
money. What they now ask is that Victoria should pay
Chaffey’s debts at once by handing over to them the for-
feited land-grant, or else that the State should help them
to float a new co., and guarantee payment of a dividend,
and let them have the land-grant on the old conditions,
and give up its control, and lose the money it has ad-
vanced, and generally put the shattered enterprise,
which was wrecked by ignorance and champagne and
boodle, on its legs again at the public expense, and for
their sole benefit. Which isn’t much when it is said
quickly.
* * * •
The Bank of Victoria announces the repayment
of another 20 per cent, of its reconstructed deposits. This
just about finishes the repayment of the weary 4J per
cent, liability incurred in 1893. Of the 12 banks which
reconstructed in that year, only five —the North Queens-
land, Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney, Royal of
Queensland, National, and Victoria—managed to meet
their obligations. Six others made various kinds of terms
by a second reconstruction process, and the seventh —the
City of Melbourne—died young and unregretted, and no-
body put any flowers on its grave.
* * * *
The prospects of the Union Trustee Co. (Mel-
bourne) look a trifle better, though there is still much
room for improvement. The year’s profits to 28th Peb.
are £3005, and a 4 per cent, dividend absorbs £2953, leav-
ing a very narrow margin of £52. And even this is a
much larger margin than the Union Co. has generally
shown of late years :
Profit. Dividend.
Feb. 1893 .. .. £4307 £4429
„ 1894 ... .. 2797 2953
„ 1895 .. .. 3648 2953
„ 1896 .. .. 553 1476
„ 1897 .. .. 2742 2953
„ 1898 .. .. 3005 2953
£17,052 £17,717
This shows the melancholy result that the Union Trustee
Co. has, in four years out of the last six, paid more away
in dividends than its entire earnings, and the net result is
that it is £660 poorer now than it was in 1893. It
has no reserve-fund. Last year it created an Invest-
ment Fluctuation Fund of £2789 by writing up to
market value Government securities formerly put in at
cost. This year the Investment Fluctuation Fund is
down to £674. Something pretty dreadful seems to have
struck it during the year, for £2lOO is a large disappear-
ance. The Union Co. has £10,125 in 3 per cent, stock ;
£23,520 in 3£ per cents. ; £4068 in 4 per cent, securities,
and about £24,000 in mortgages, which should yield
6or 6 per cent. And as it only makes 4 per cent,
all round, it looks as if the trustee business was a
small loss to the shareholders, though doubtless it sup-
plies billets for a number of deserving officials. Anyhow,
a co. which has less than £2200 of accumulations after 13
years isn’t getting ahead any too fast. The Investment
Fluctuation Fund certainly can’t stand another year like
the last, and it isn’t a very good feature that nearly one-
fourth of the mortgages are foreclosed. The Union
Trustee Co. should evidently have cut its dividend down
to 3 per cent, five years ago, and kept it there.
* * * •
Ballarat Trustees Co. has a half-year’s profit of
£7OB, and £344 brought forward makes a total available
balance of £1052. Shareholders get a dividend at the rate
of 7 per cent, per annum (£525) ; £250 is written off good-
will, and £277 carried forward. The Ballarat Trustees
Co. is something unusual, inasmuch as it has no reserve
fund, and its assets are considerably less than its nominal
liabilities to shareholders. The gap is filled by the
remarkable paper item “goodwill, £1750.” The only
good feature about this lamentable way of squaring the
accounts is that the goodwill item is being steadily written
off ;in 1893 it stood at £4OOO. But, until it is entirely
written-off and something in the way of a reserve is
accumulated, the 7 per cent, dividend is wholly unjustifi-
able. The co. wisely keeps almost clear of the unlucky
mortgage business, its principal asset being £10,850 in-
vested in State and municipal securities, and it ought to be
a very good concern when the depressing “ goodwill ” is
wiped out.
* * * •
The last news about the Sydney atmospheric
gas process which blew up Minister Garrard is that gas
can be made by the patent which the two missing gentle-
men left behind them —only it costs 6s. 6d. per 1000 ft.
Which is just about as joyful as if there was no gas at all.
The other new gas process—that which is now booming at
Frankston (Vic.) —is already beginning to have the cold,
damp blanket of criticism piled on top of it. It is now
alleged that it isn’t a new idea, but a very old one, and
that the same kind of gas might have been in full blast
any time for years past, only certain disadvantages, not
very clear to the person who isn’t a gas expert, were in
the way. Apparently the new gas is genuine so far as it
goes —how far it goes being still uncertain. The fact that
it hasn’t yet blown up any Vic. Minister is at least one
item to its credit.
• * * »
Dear B. —No monkey-tricks about Stephenson’s
Melb. atmospheric-gas apparatus. I was there, and rather
narked the promoters by the suspicious way I took every-
thing to pieces and shifted the concern all about the
office-floor, on the look-out for pipes, &c. The points
which most need elucidation are the nature of the
chemicals used and the length of time the gas will burn.
The inventor freely gives the names of certain few and
inexpensive chemicals, and says gas so produced will last
six weeks ; but the sceptical public has not seen the
materials purchased, and will certainly not sit down and
wait beside one burner for six weeks. The gas-engineer
of Melb. Gas co. ridicules the whole thing, and says
it is impossible to produce the gas in large quantities.
However, John Wallace, M.L.0., of mining fame, who is
at the back of the idea, advises unbelievers to buy the
chemicals themselves, take them to Frankston, and, after
satisfying themselves that there is nothing cronk about
the machinery, place them personally in the carburetter
and witness the whole process. As far as I can judge, and
putting large population-centres aside, the powerful light
which Stephenson’s small apparatus produces would be
invaluable for stations and small townships.—M‘G.
* • * *
The enquiry into the Melb. tramway-men’s
grievances seems likely to end in a fiasco, three of the
committee having withdrawn, because chairman Maloney
wished to extend the sphere of enquiry to all employes—
past and present—of the Tramway co. Madden, Harris
and Zox objected and resigned, so that now nobody
knows whether Sprigg and his men are in the right.

During the last couple of years several far-
western N.S.W. P.M.’s have made assignments to their
creditors. One or two have been removed to other dis-
tricts ; but there are one or two who have not. And, as
the Licensing Court decisions in particular of one of these
latter have been peculiar, the public begin to say things.
* * » *
Several southern towns in Bananaland are
groaning under the despotism of the civil servant usurer.
Borrow a couple of hundred off your boss and your billet
is a surety. You may booze yourself blue, commit- half
the crimes in the Decaiosrue, and damn departmental
regulations sky-high, but so long as you meet your official
superior’s interest bill punctually every half-year you’re
safe as houses. The thing’s becoming a public scandal.
* * » »
Softgoods Sargood and co. paid all their Mel-
bourne hands who were retained (though unemployed),
pending the reconstruction of the firm’s business-pre-
mises full wages meanwhile. Nobody was discharged,
and regularly, every week, each collected his
usual wage, less 25 per cent. At the banquet given in
the new offices to celebrate the resumption of affairs on
the old footing, the firm informed all hands that, on ap-
plication to the cashier, the withheld 25 per cent, deduc-
tions would be paid forthwith. They had retained this
proportion merely out of kindness, well-knowing that idle
hands are disposed to spend more than is good for them.
Frantic applause—and no wonder. Such employers are
rare.
• * • *
Pop : “ Read somewhere, the other day, of a
State-subsidised pawnbroking establishment—in Rome, if
I recollect rightly—where no interest whatever is charged.
In Sydney, now, thrives a certain three-ball-man, who
is charging only one penny per month in return for the
loan of five bob 1 He lends pretty closely up to
market-values, too. This beats the (British) old country
badly ; and, though it represents nominally 20 per cent,
per annum, it doesn’t pay directly. But it does indirectly :
the pawnbroker in question finds it a splendid advt. for
his big attached jewellery and second-hand dealer’s shop,
and an excellent device for replenishing his stock —with
expired pledges, which, according to trade etiquette, the
chiefly-interested pawnbroker usually is allowed the option
of buying-in for himself at the periodical auction-sale
clearances. No-interest is already within sight in this
country, too, evidently.

Dear Bulletin. —Selection has only had the
effect in the Warren (N.S.W.) country of making the
lessees improve their areas, and add to the carrying capa-
city of their holdings by forming smaller paddocks and
dam-building. Haddon Rig, our show station, situated as
it is on both sides of the perennial Marthaguy, has had
over 20,000 acres selected, and the whole of these cookies
seem to be prosperous and attached permanently to the
land. Yet the station sent away last year 1600 bales of
wool, a larger clip than in the banyan days when the
selector was unknown. The season was an average one.
—W. S. Targe re.
* • • *
De Queer to The Bulletin from “ the Gate to
Klondyke ” : “ Plenty of work here, but wages miserable.
Men working in saw-mills for 10 cents, an hour, or $2O a
month and tucker. The form of agreement between the
contractors for the Yukon railway and the men they
wanted wasn’t presented for signature till the men were
on board the boat for Telegraph Creek, when it turned
out to be of such severe conditions that the men, noting
also that no rate of wage was specified, refused to sign,
and many of them jumped ashore just as the boat was
cast off. This railway job is a gigantic reach-out, some-
thing on the principle of the M’llwraith proposal to give
away the mineral lands of Queensland to a railway syndi-
cate. This Yukon railway contract has been given to
M'Kenzie and co. by the Dominion Government, but has
not been sanctioned by the Canadian House of Commons.
The contractors are to get a block of 25,000 acres for
every mile of line constructed (Govt, taking alternate
blocks within the mineral belt), and are to pay a royalty
of 1 per cent, for all gold won off of their land. Seeing
that prospectors and diggers are charged $lO for a miners’
license for a year and a royalty of 10 per cent, on all gold
won over $2500 a year, you will understand that diggers
look upon the vvhole thing as a great grab and swindle.
There are not many Australian miners going through here
to the Yukon ; about 100 at the outside.”

* * * '»
Papua forges ahead. Last year’s imports, ex-
ports, output of gold, production of copra and india-
rubber, beat the record of any previous year. Gold was
the largest item of export, but exact figures are not
obtainable. Through Cooktown Customs House passed
71480 z., valued at £25,018. Probably, however, total out-
put was twice as much. Rubber trade is expanding also.
In year ’95-’96 total export was only 3 tons ; last year it
rose to 16 tons. The revenue of the Possession also in-
creased by £lo,ooo—an item not to be despised. H. Ex.
has great trouble in inducing the natives to comply with a
few simple sanitary burial regulations, and one of the
pleasing duties discharged in the course of the guberna-
torial career consisted in visiting a native village, exhum-
ing 60 Papuan cadavers (some of them very recent), and
re-burying them.
* * * *
Report of the Sydney Stock Exchange : —“ The
share market on Saturday was lifeless.” Yes : the Sab-
bath tipster has ceased from tipping, and the Sydney
broker is at rest. Holders of Girilambones at more than
double the ruling prices are assured of the great future
of the mine, and, again, the reports from the North Nym-
agee are “ encouraging.” The victims of the numberless
other mines tipped with the same “ earnestness,” and
now abandoned as worthless, in liquidation, or rapidly
approaching that stage, are passed over in silence.
• * * *
How is it that such publicity is given to all ex-
emptions or partial exemptions granted or refused at
Cobar? Most of the rest of N.S.W. is in a state of per-
manent exemption. “ Cobar-Chesney two months exemp-
tion granted but not less than 20 men to be employed.”
Around the rich Wentworth mines at Lucknow there are
many leases on which there are not 20 men employed alto-
gether, and apparently no attempt is made to enforce any
labor conditions. “It is this way,” says one warden ;
“as long as no one objects it is all right, but, you see,
there are no less than six candidates for Parliament in
Cobar.”

Talk about your rich Westralian crushing!
This from a back number of S.M. Herald (1872)
“ Krohmann’s, Hill End : The total crushing of 139 tons
gave 14,0670 z. gold, and about 3500 z. more are expected
from specimens and tailings. A div. of 12s. 6d. per share
has been declared —£50,000 ; £2OOO added to reserve, and
£3,500 set aside to meet current expenses.” On the day
of this great crushing, Krohmann’s shares were selling at
645. cum div., and there was a great weakness in the
market. Probably the yield was not so good as antici-
pated. It never, is. Krohmann was so hard-up the day
he struck it in his claim that his wife had prepared him a
meal on soup made from butchers’ well-cleaned bones.
He came home to dinner with over lOOoz. of gold in a bag.
He took to Germany some £70,000 in cash, became a
burgomaster in his old native village, lent big sum
to German princes, whose properties were alread
y
well “ dipped,” and died without the price of a chest of
tea. Literal fact.
* * * *
Last four-weeks’ production of the famous Mt.
Lyell, from 10,962 tons of ore : Copper, 310 tons ; silver,
35,7330 z. ; gold, 16890 z. The ore taken from the open
cuts is just under 3 per cent, copper, that from No. 4
tunnel over 7 per cent. Big as it is, this production is
only a few tons of copper a week higher than that of the
obscure Cobar tributors, and Cobar should get ahead
within a few months! Mining values are mostly a
matter of sentiment. The Cobar tributors, who are
simply “ shovelling it in,” don’t think their interests lie
in the blazoning-forth of returns, so their figures can only
be got at in a haphazard way. The boom at Mt. Lyell has
given a value to one mine, so far a non-producer, greater
than that of the Cobar mine itself, tributors’ interests and
all! At Cobar the next block of ground, taking in a part
of the town, was sold recently for a few thousand pounds,
having a surface-value of at least two-thirds of the amount
paid for it. No boom at Cobar —and the writer prays it
may never have one like that in Tasmania.
* * • •
The game.and persistent CrotSy, discoverer of
Mt. Lyell, died in London a few days ago. He with
others worked the mine some years for gold and long
peddled it about Sydney and Melb. without finding a pur-
chaser or a syndicate to work it. Finally he placed half
of the mine with Orr, Bowes Kelly and others, and to-day
it has a market-value of over three millions. Crotty soon
quarrelled with the directors on the financing and work-
ing of the mine, and devoted his time, money, and atten-
tion to North Mt. Lyell, the real measure of which is
about to be taken.
* • • *
As a dealer in gold, John Chinaman must turn
a pretty penny. There are small Q. mining towns in
which the Chow is the sole gold-merchant—and he fixes
his own prices. The value of gold in China must be high
compared with Australia, for writer remembers Chinese
dealers in the North who would give you from twenty
shillings and sixpence up to twenty-one shillings for
every sovereign you would give them. Interesting to
know how much Australian gold goes to Chow-larid every
year.

A few Mt. Morgan figures not particularly well
known. In one year ’BB-’S9 the shareholders received
£1,150,000 in dividends. The cause of this splendid re-
turn was the working of 4oz. stone. For the half-year
ending May, 'B9, 36,675 tons were treated for 168,3560 z.,
showing an average yield of 4oz. lldwt. 19gr. This 4oz.
stone, however, was by no means the richest treated, for
the stone treated for the half-year ending May, ’B7,
yielded an average of soz. 16dwts. 7gr. The works just
now are treating about 12,000 tons per month, and the
management boasts that 4dwt. a ton pays for everything.

Victoria, Charters Towers, 884 oz. for the month
without the cyanide clean-up. The 55th div. of 6d. de-
clared. Market Bs. 3d., and the mine reported to be
looking as well as ever. For three years past the impres-
sion seems to have been that it was time the Vic. took a
rest from divs. —reckoning by averages on the Towers;
hence the only variation in the shares has been from
6s. 9d. to 10s., and the latter sum has been paid in divs.
meanwhile.
* * * *
Mills United compelled at last to make
a 6d. call. Funds exhausted, and the faces in ore of low
grade. Better prospects ahead—they are never behind.
How hard it is to tell the life of a gold-mine ! There are
copper, tin and other mines that have been paying divs.
for over a quarter of a century. Where in the whole
world can this be said of a gold-mine ? Probably the
Long Tunnel (Vic.) has the longest continuous record—
and Mt. Morgan has the longest outlook. But if all the
world’s div.-paying gold-mines were averaged their paying
lives would be found wonderfully short, and the reason
for wanting from 20 per cent, to 50 per cent, on invest-
ment would be seen.

» * * w
Never had Premier Forrest to face such a depu-
tation as that he met lately at Kalgoorlie on the alluvial
rights question. The men selected for the job displayed
much more readiness than the average W.A. Minister of
the Crown and gave John posers which made him wriggle.
Fair average sample. Delegate Waite : “A miner’s right
is a deed of agreement by virtue of which thousands of
men are to-day in this country. The Govt, has treated
this contract with contempt by the 10ft. limitation.”
Forrest labored heavily in his reply, and as soon as he
finished was told to his teeth it was unsatisfactory. The
“ riot ” followed. It will be a long time before J. Forrest
wants such another deputation.

• * * *
Probably the most poverty-stricken gold-camp
in Austra’ia is Castlemaine, Vic., and even there, last
week, a digger unearthed a piece of gold, to which three
lbs. of stone was attached. The gold weighed 61b., and
conveyed more joy to one poor digger than the biggest
Boulder crushing to 99 shareholders in Bull’s land.
• * • *
I pegged-out a claim at Duck-creek opal-field,
Q. (writes a correspondent). I had never dug opal before,
but was confident and happy. Sank a shaft; and hope-
fully scrutinised every piece of stone. By-and-bye, I was
startled by a strange noise overheard. Dragged myself
up, and saw a greasy black-gin squatted on my mullock-
heap with a lap-full of opals, crooning to herself, and
softly rubbing a stone of the first water with her apology
for a skirt. I was tempted to brain her with the spade,
and drop her down the sepulchre just excavated. Spit-
ting with unerring precision on the toe of my boot, she
yabbered, “ Mine think you plenty plurry fool.” Subse-
quent I ascertained that these hags are kept by the
“old hands” for fossicking purposes, etc., and make a
good thing out of the claims of innocent new hands at the
game.

SOCIETY
The row that is going on among the Mel-
bourne Salvationists casts an incidental side-light,
or alleged side-light, on the sordid surroundings
of the Army. The plaintiff is a lady officer who
alleges that when she fell ill, after a long record
of good service, she was allowed a pittance of 2s 3d
a week out of the big-drum funds for her support.
Also she claims that an attempt was made to
hustle her out of the service, presumably to save
the 2s 3d, by appointing her to labor in cold,
damp places which were calculated to break her
up. One of tbam was the Rescue Home, described
thus:—
The hygicoic conditions there were most unfavorable.
Ihe Corporation Tip was in full swing opposite, the
odours of which were injurious to health. The Home is
situated in one of the lowest levels in Richmond, near
the Yarra. Damp odours pervaded the rooms, and the
1 (o^^aiW

paper of the walls of my bedroom was in tatters from
dampness—the walls were mildewed and the paper peel-
"J?* Rwas in the months of May and June, and the
othcers had to sit in rooms without fires.
What a picture ! Think of being “ rescued ”by a
process of cold shudders at mid-winter in a damp
fireless building opposite a Corporation Tip, with
the odour of the festering ham-bone in the air
and cold raw Word for sustenance. And compare
that with a life of Sin, and a good fire, and a soft
couch, and something in the shape of transparent
straw-tinted consolation in a bottle. There is no
earthly show for virtue if its accompaniments are
cold feet, and a red nose with a speck of moisture on
the end of it, and perpetual goose-flesh, and two
Rescued girls in one hard bed trying to keep warm
by thinking of Jeremiah. And when the breeze
that blows off the decayed ham-bone wakes up the
Rescued, and the moaning of the wind in the
damp, loose paper won’t let them sleep again, and
there is nothing to consider except the moon-
beams, and the rats, and the far-off New Jeru-
salem —well, where does the joy come in ? It is
hard enough to be reasonably good anywhere, but
to be good in Richmond opposite the Corporation
Tip is simply impossible.
* • » *
Amusing to note how the Salvation Army
has caught the “regular” slang. They all
solemnly address each other as “ Captain,” “ Cor-
poral,” “ Adjutant,” and so on. And one hears —
“Where are you quartered now?” “Oh, I’m
billeted in Carlton.”
Australian Governors—permanent or * ‘ act-
ing”—are desired to swallow and digest the fol-
lowing remarks from Home Chat, a little London
sassiety paper, of the very “loyalest ” character :
“At one time, theatrical-managers always kept
the curtain down till the arrival of Royalties, but
the Prince disliked the inconvenience to the rest
of the _ audience by the delay, and gave strict
orders it should not again occur. The Prince and
Princess are annoyed if attention is drawn to their
arrival by the playing of ‘God Save the Queen’
or * God Bless the Prince of Wales.’ ” After all,
The Bulletin is in favor of inviting Albert
Edward or the Duke of York to come along and
say something on the subject of a (say) Melbourne
audience rising under the infliction of the Han-
them when Brassey toddles to his seat 20 minutes
behind time. Also, his Royal Nibs might be
induced to snort at the managers who introduced
the custom of delaying a performance until the
local tin-god turns up.
* * » *
—Gov. Buxton, S.A.’s chronic travelling
Viceroy, follows Brassey’s example and goes ’Ome
shortly for six months. His decision to visit Eng-
land has delighted Adelaide society, and the early
prospect of having the liberal and newly-married
Way at Government House instead of His 3-X,
who has been a terrible social frost, comes as one
of the most gladsome events of the year. In fact,
society people cold-bloodedly hope’ Gov. Buxton
won’t come back again.

Dear Bulletin. —Tie
“something lost.” When
registering a letter at an

* * *
One of the improve-
ments made in Melbourne’s
lovely Yarra was the blast-
ing away of the rocks at
the old fall above Prinoes-
bridge, in order to give
freer escape to the flood-
waters. So far as concerns
the upper part of the soulful
sewer, things are the worse
for the change. The an-
cient dog that was thrown
overboard in Richmond or
Collingwood used to float
down over these falls, and,
as he couldn’tfloatup again,
all eastern Melbourne was
rid of him. Now the Yarra
is a tidal stream as far as
Collingwood, and the dog
goes down with the tide in
the morning and comes up
again with the tide at
night, and keeps going up
and down till he disappears
through effluxion of time.
Four miles of the river
are now practically a layer
of dogs that wash wearily
eastward with the rising
tide, and wearily wash
westward with the tide as
it falls again. And in hot
weather the great dog de-
posit is remembersome.
• * *
Lord Ranfurly is head of the house of
Knox. And the house of Knox is as Orange as
they make em. Not surprising, therefore, that
Ranfurly, albeit officially supposed to have no
religious or political prejudices, should boil over
on tempting occasion. Ihe other day, when
receiving an Otago Presbyterian Synod deputa-
tion, the young man said that he - favored the pure
Oospel derived from the Bible alone, and undis-
curbed by doctrines which can neither be corro-
borated by Scripture nor countenanced by common
sense. Which deliverance has put Maoriland
R.C. s upon their hind-legs. And they’re going
to get Chamberlain to ask Ranfurly what he
means by publicly letting loose his family dogina-
tisms. And Ranfurly will answer that the spirit
of the original Knox was upon him. And Joe will
d n the original Knox.

One consistently-remarkable feature about
6 g i r i with a . h° rr id tale of woe to pour into the
ear of the magistrate is that the “outrage 99 hap-
pens on (say) Tuesday night, and she only men-
tions the matter to her relatives next Fridav
fortnight, or even later. The latest Sydney
victim according to the evidence of her sister
came home in an excited state one night and said
that a policeman had moved her on, or threatened
to lock her up. Then days rolled on in the usual
circular fashion, and after a long interval the tale
grew into a picturesque horror in which the officer
gagged and handcuffed her, and then committed a
capital offence at 11 p.m. and apparently at a
busy street corner. Case dismissed with scorn.
The girl who loses her virtue, and only remem-
bers the fact a week or a fortnight or a month
afterwards, used to be accepted without question,
but her crude tale begins to lose its novelty.

Dear B. —Wanted, an explanation of the
mysterious connection between spec-
tacles and saintliness. Just been at
a Salvation Army meeting in which
eight out of fourteen wore glasses,
and two out of the other six held their
hymn-books close to their noses.
Their sight was imperfect.
The thing has nothing to do
with much reading, for, as a
rule, you find the spec-
tacled'saint poorly acquain-
ted with any book but the
Bible. Does naturally
weak sight indicate a brain
peculiarly adapted to receive
religious impressions? I
have noticed that several
weak-sighted young men of
my acquaintance, when at-
tacked by a religious fanatic,
succumbed immediately t o
Biblo-mania.—J.F.D.

Viator : “ The Godly in
Melb. never complain of the
pubs, being open on Good Friday,
but would be horrified at ‘ Babes in
the Wood ’ being acted.”
Chief Justice Way’s wedding was very
quiet. In fact, rumors were purposely circulated
that it was to eventuate at Hahndorf—a hills
township 30 miles from Adelaide; but at the last
moment the ceremony was performed at a private
house in an Adelaide suburb.
* » * *
Adelaide doctors are ramping at the cabled
news that a Miss Da Costa, a wealthy lady who
died in England awhile ago, has bequeathed a
large sum for the benefit of convalescent patients
from Adelaide Hospital ! The striking doctors
have all along maintained that it means sudden
death to go to the hospital under the present
regime, and now the hospital staff announces that
the legacy “ will be a boon to the increasing num-
ber of corivalescents ” from the institution!
Which charmingly adroit announcement fairly
knocks the stuffing out of the opposition.
» * » *
The great Australian Thirst was well
exemplified at Gundagai recently. The drill-
instructor to the Eirst Australian Horse, when
drilling the local warriors on a hot day, told all
who wanted a drink to fall out, whereupon evevy
man fell out and made a bee-line for the nearest
pub., leaving the instructor to follow on his own.
Verily the Real Mackays !

regularly get drunk to
gether. “ Twin souls
with but a single

* * *
An heirloom in a
Sydney Caledonian house-
hold is an old-fashioned tea-
set, on each article of which
is an inscription. On the
teapot, “ Be canny with the
tea ”; on thesugar-basin, ditto
with the sugar ; on the milk-
jug, ditto with the milk. Thus
doth the Scot imbibe canni-
ness “ with his mother’s milk,”
tea, and sugar !

EQUIVOCAL.
HE: “I think I’m improving."
SHE: “ Yes—making great strides.”
“ A Poor Pedagogue ” :
Having: had nine years service under N.S. W. Public In
struction Department I can confirm truth of Bulletin
par. (2,4 ’93) re State schools. When 1 was pupil-teaching
in a large city school, the principal, no doubt having in
mind the instruction about “ fees in arrear return,”
hinted at compulsory payment by pupil-teachers of fees
not collected by them. In small country schools I have
known numbers of pedagogues (self included) who them-
selves paid up arrears rather than have to w'orry over the
“ return of fees in arrear.” No doubt the “ steadily-
insist ” clause has much to do with this.—S. V.
* * * *
Dear B.— Re that regulation (quoted in
Bulletin) giving the Commissioner of Vic. Rail-
ways the right to use any article patented by em-
ployes without paying royalty, it might be men-
tioned that, according to Rudyard Kipling, a
similar regulation exists in the Indian railway-
department, with the magnificent result (accord-
ing to same authority) that no employe in that
department ever does make an invention, or
cherishes any ambition ever to do so.—P.L.

On the outer seat she sat—
Quiet! quiet! my heart’s riot:
From the shelter of her hat
Brown eyes surely glanced demurely,
And her ankles trim would peep
From their hiding amid laces,
And they set my heart aleap
With their fairy curves and graces.
Now they’ve covered up my maid ;
’Stead of soulful, I am doleful,
And the wayward mind that strayed
Rapt in fancy’s necromancy’s
Given over to the throes
Of continual despairing—
D—n their advertised hose !
Give me those my love was wearing.
T.D.

* * * *
A pathetic protest against the circumfer-
ence of women’s hats at public entertainments
came last week from “ Country Bumpkin,” in a
letter to Melb. Argus. “Though a six-footer,”
wrote “C. 8.,” “ I had not » possible chance, and
my experience must have been similar to that of
many at to-night’s Exhibition concert.” He had
paid to see Albani warble, but three awful hats
completely obstructed his view of the music.
The latest achievement of one of the
younger N.S.W. District-Court Judges. Brown
sues Jones to recover damages for assault. Judge
m 10 minutes sides with Jones (he generally un-
consciously becomes a partisan in 10 minutes).
Brown s counsel seek a non-suit (so that they may
get a jury). Judge suavely allows it —but orders
Brown to pay double fees and thumping costs, and
directs the Registrar that no further action is to
be permitted until the costs, &c., are paid.

Eaglehawk, Bendigo’s big suburb, decided
to celebrate the Diamond Jubilee by building a
new town-hall. Then, having admired the plans,
it invited the patriots to celebrate to the extent of
•*5OOO at 3£ per cent., “ as it was thought that for
such a purpose the money could be got very
cheap.” Result : five offers for >£1250 in all.
“ Patriotism ” rarely sacrifices 10s. per cent.
♦ * * *
With Spanish privateers scouring the seas,
and not too particular about their prey, what
hauls could be made from some of the vessels
trading to Australia. To capture the Mariposa,
which left for America the other week with
.£350,000 worth of specie aboard, would be a scoop
comprehensive enough in itself to set up a gang
of authorised adventurers for life. The job would
be comparatively easy, too, if a well-chosen portion
of her crew could be previously bought over with
promises of booty.

Queen’s first Drawing Room this year :
Lady Marjorie Carington, who had the entnje with her
mother, Lady Carington, was the most admired of the
debutantes. . . . The carriages were grievously shabby
with a few distinguished exceptions; notably the

Holdar M. : “ Noticed to-day a
leading teetotal-person skip nimbly
aside as an advancing brewer’s dray,
groaning with sparkling amber, threat-
ened to run him down. Savagely
resentful of this indignity, the cold-
tea-man fumed impotently on the
kerbstone. The case of that literary
foreigner, who lived in dread of a vile
death beneath the muddy wheels of a
harlot-loaded cab, crouches before the
realisation of the awful thunder that
would awaken the welkin were an
involuntary sacrifice made by the
Water Bore ’neath such a Juggernaut
as the publican’s ambulance.

Dear Bulletin. —The story of
the missing corpse found in the London
hotel bath really does not contain too
much pigment. In a decent London
club set apart for Australians and
people from British Guiana, Sierra
Leone and Kobe, I am now paying
18d. per diem for a bath. I think the
cuticle of the resident Briton de-
teriorates under hydropathy. Any-
how, he doesn’t wash seriously. By
the way, in Vienna an ex-M.L.A.,
Vic., who lost his seat for the same
reason as Alcibiades, is now paying
3s. 4d. a day for his bath. —Yours,
E.D. London, 13 Feb.

“ PINKING DONE.”
Do they “ pink ” the optic of de-
ceased or put blushes on the cold
cheeks ? or what ? Noticeable that the

me pink ! ”

* * * *
The cat came back ! It transpired at the
inquest that the five little boys who were washed
away by a freshet in an Adelaide creek early this
month were trying to drown a cat. Three boys
lost their lives, but the cat ran home unhurt.

Of current Melb. journalism,
says the organ of David Syme:—
The gossip has to be crisp, the stories have
to be short, and the topical articles spiced
with epigrams.
In what paper do these topical articles
spiced with epigrams appear ? It must
be printed in a cellar and published in
a drain.

SEX OF ONE AND A DOZING
OF THE OTHER.
Justice, as we know, is blind,
But, when from bad man’s fury
Some meek, fair girl with eyes resigned
Claims legal guardianship, we find
So should be judge and jury.
* * * •
To a cleric in the Never-Never
came one day a woman with two small
children to be christened. The clergy-
man, having duly baptised the bant-
lings, asked if she wished to be
“ churched.” She assented and accom-
panied the divine to the altar. But
when he had got as far as “ hath pre-
served you in the great dangers of child-
birth,” he was electrified by the obser-
vation, “If it’s any satisfaction to you, sir, I’ll
go through with it, but — hadn’t we letter wait till
the mother comes ? ”

Dear B.—Did you ever meet the superior
Chow ? Our vegetable John found the table dirty
where he had to deposit the cabbagee, applee,
&c., so turned on our little sister, thus: “Dirty
lillee gel. Why you no keepee table clean ?
Mother gettee old, no can workee so much, boys
must go out blisness in town, so houseeclean you
mus’ do. Naughty lit gel! ” — Domestic.
The archaic reporter of a Sydney
daily, in his account of a wedding very
recently, put it that “Mr. So-and-so
took Miss to wife. ” An American
paper just to hand goes one better by
chronicling that “ yesterday Miss Myra
Simpson, long a valued contributor to
this paper, took Hal Jenks, of this
city, to husband.” And, after all, why
not?
* * * •
The result of the drought in
some of the S.A. farming centres has
been to largely increase the immorality
which sells itself for money, and, should
next season also be rainless, illegitimacy
is likely to show a sad advance, despite
the women’s vote and W.C.T.U. in-
fluence.

In a W.A. police-court, recently,
a bright-green policeman deposed that
a prisoner had been “most obstropu-
lous,” having “resisted volubly,” and
“hit him (the trap) two kicks.” In
the same court, not long before, a man
charged with bicycle-stealing cross-ex-
amined a witness as to his “theologi-
cal ” knowledge of the parts of a bike.
• * * *
Judge Hodges in court: —“De-
fendant has to satisfy the court that he
came lawfully by the posts, not merely
that he came by them honestly.” Which
infers that it is sometimes lawful to
be dishonest and other times dishonest
to be lawful.

A Southern city buxom pave-
ment lady, much affected by visiting
men -o’ - warsmen, cheerfully stumps
about on two crutches and one leg.
On the day at Kalgoorlie that wound up
with the row at the railway station, one local
pub. took J>26o for drinks. This accounts for a
lot of things.
John Forrest himself says he was not struck at
Kalgoorlie, and that the worst that happened to
him was “ a poke in the ribs with an umbrella ”•
not, by the way, the sort of weapon an alluvial
digger usually carries. Yet the Riot Act was
read.
“ I’ll fight any one of you, but I can’t take on a
crowd,” was among the expressions alleged by
big John Forrest’s press friends to have been used
by their champion during the Kalgoorlie row.
But, as no proceedings have been taken against
John, apparently either this part of the report
(like much else of it) isn’t true, or a Premier
isn’t responsible for language calculated to pro-
voke a breach of the peace.
One of the queerest features of the “ Kalgoorlie
riot ” business was not presented until afterwards.
It was when some nondescripts were prosecuted,
and when, a conviction on the evidence seeming
doubtful, the paid magistrate broke in with the
remark that he himself had observed the prisoner,
and promptly followed that up with a six months’
sentence. In one case, too, the riotous behavior
consisted partly of “compliments” paid to the
sitting magistrate. That one got nine months.
Of all the congratulatory and sympathising
messages which Westralian Premier Forrest re-
ceived after his Kalgoorlie experiences, the one
that seems to strike big John’s admirers as the
most admirable came from a W.A. paid magis-
trate—“ My God, lam proud of you.” Just what
Big John, who is, apparently, this R.M.’s god
—anyway, the message was addressed to John—
did to evoke this note of admiration is not clear at
this distance. For the Premier, who hadn’t been
Miss EDITH MORLEY (Charles Cartwright's
daughter), in “A Marriage of Convenience,” at
Sydney Criterion.
Photo., Talma, Melbourne.
out in the sun all day, seems to have been about
the first of the thousands to lose his head ; and,
according to the accounts given by his own friends,
broke out into language at an early stage of the
proceedings and “talked fight” as well as the
most drunken of the crowd. Afterwards, when
the excitement became a little warmer, John’s

The first messages to the Eastern papers report-
ing the row at Kalgoorlie were so filled with
“infuriated mob” and dust and gore that they
were too strong even for such a respectable paper
as the S.A. Register. But the S.M. Herald
let ’em have it all. And now the furious attack
on the Premier dwindles to a “ poke in the ribs
with an umbrella,” and the troopers with rifles are
reduced to a single policeman with a gun, and the
shouts for another Eureka Stockade are pinned to
a crippled town-crier, whose business it is (or was
—he is “doing” nine months now) to draw a
crowd by making a fool of himself or otherwise.

Just before Premier Forrest left Perth on
his now historical visit to Menzies and Kalgoorlie,
and while the alluvial diggers were working them-
selves up to proper demonstrating form, Vic.
veteran C. E. Jones lectured on “The Eureka
Stockade.” And it was avowedly apropos, too.

TEA.
Competitive DESIGNS are invited of a POSTER for
TEA, to be used on Hoardings.
£5
will be given to the competitor who sends in the design
meeting most with our approval; our decision to be final.
Designs may be artistic, catchy in composition of
lettering, coloring, or phraseology. Must not exceed
three colors, and be original. Colors liable to fade by
exposure to be avoided.
Competitors may send in as many designs as they
please.
All designs sent in to become our property, with the
right to use them as we may desire at any time without
further paj-ment.
Competition to CLOSE on APRIL 30, 1898.
Address .
In response to this :
The Artists Society of Melbourne, having run out of
tea, invite a competition from the tea merchants of Mel-
bourne to supply the deficiency. The price of two Chests
of Tea will be given to the merchant who sends in the tea
most meeting with the approval of the artists, whose
decision will be final.
Sample chests must be of superior quality, and those
teas most liable to deteriorate by keeping to be avoided.
Competitors may send in as many chests of tea as they
please.
All chests of tea sent in to become the property of the
Artists’ Society, with the right to use them as they may
desire at any time without further payment. The guile-
less artists hope by these means to keep themselves in
tea for the rest of their lives, at the trifling cost of two
chests!
Address, “Gamboge, Emerald Green & C 0.,” Art
Society’s Rooms, Hanky-Panky Place, Melbourne.

Up-to-date pathos—from Melb. Age :
~V£T ANTED, Cancer Cured, any price, no cure no
’’ pay. Sufferer, Age Office
• * • *
With a personal grievance at the back of
their agitation, Sydney and Melb. dailies now-
jump at all chances of proving the inadequacy of
the present cable system, or rather the S.A, por-
tion of it. But the artful S.A. authorities avoid
blame by holding back information as to inter-
ruptions, and even when a memorandum is sent,
the fact that the overland line is responsible for
delay is carefully withheld as a rule. The news-
paper men have also been astonished of late at the
receipt of jocular hints from S.A. that the tele-
graphs in Mauritius and other outlandish places
are in a state of serious disrepair—-a trouble which
can have no possible connection with the almost
chronic interruption of Australian cables.
THE PRICE WE PAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The price we pay for the bread we eat
Is bitter as aloes among the sweet;
Though the path seems fair to the venturing feet—
The price we pay.
The price we pay with the forced caress
To the foolish lips that we seem to bless,
Giving the more while we grudge the less —
The price we pay !
We were made so frail that we cannot toil,
But we stoop full low for the golden spoil ;
Though our fingers shrink as they touch the soil—
And the price we pay.
H.M.B.
He went to see Albani
At the Exhibition spacious,
For she’s the great Sopraney
Who gets cables from Her Gracious.
The papers, advertising
Her surprising vocalising,
Had prompted him to come to town to cheer her ;
But an intervening lady
Blocked his vision with a cady,
And when Albani sang he didn’t hear her.
Alas ! poor luckless sonny,
Protesting, all in vain, he
Got nothing for the money
He had paid to see Albani.
A “ wonderful ovation ”
Voiced the public admiration,
The while he sat a sad and silent mourner,
He got no soulful thrillings
For his pair of shiny shillings.
’Cos why ? He couldn’t see the preema doaner.
LOOSE LOGIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas a frequent remark of admonishing friends,
Mac. was “ burning both ends of the candle ” ;
For his morals were loose, and his life was fast,
And the pace that he went was a scandal.
But Mac. would reply to counsel and chaff,
With logical lip and a light-hearted laugh—
“ Faith, ’tis shillings that come and pounds that go ;
There’s a little we pay—and a lot we owe 1
“ And though loot one lends and the loot one spends
Looks like ‘ burning the candle at both of its ends,’
This apparently un-economical feat,
Don’t ye see, is the way to make both ends meet ! ”
