A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 19, 1898.
Mt Dear Myee—
Easter week danced along a tempo prestissimo,
and again the battle was to the thin. I noticed
that the stout people had only half as good a time
of it, the other half being taken up with attending
to their palpitations. These were set going by
Lady Hampden’s first ball on Tuesday; were fur-
ther aggravated by the big St. Vincent’s dance
next evening; hurried on to racing speed by
Rand wick doings on Thursday afternoon ; a cold
sweat set in with an Albani reception at the
Ladies’ Club ; broke into a gallop at Government
House ball the same night; and, finally, Rand-
wick on Saturday saw one vast dead-beat, which
the Messiah at 8 couldn’t resurrect.
The Tuesday ball at Govt. House was tossed
high up among titles, diamonds, and the like.
Lords abounded, and Ladies were as common as
women. Lady Holled Smith had a jolly smile
for everyone, and looked as if barrack life
was the merriest thing in the world. Mrs. Willie
Mitchell, who has been much in evidence since
her return from Europe, reminded me of “ Never-
more ” and Edgar Allen Poe, and the pallid bust of
Pallas, and no end of other things. A big black
bird stood on her hair, and peered down haughtily
over the diamond butterfly in her toupee. Sister
and brother of this same black bird sat on either
Bhoulder, and held their wings well up from the
red satin of their keeper’s ball-dress. The effect
was truly fly. Miss Marcia Cox, whose engage-
ment to Mr. Learmonth was announced some
time ago, kept up the family reputation for good
looks in a yellow frock and hair that crimped of
its own accord.
Thursday’s ball was invaded by nine debutantes
in nine white dresses, and twice nine white satin
shoes, and eighteen gloves, and ninety bouquets.
But, stay. Perhaps I exaggerate those bou-
quets. As I think it over, it grows clear
that each debutante had one bunch, and one
only. And yet the memory of that evening
shows me r ‘garden beds where only white
things grow” all over the ballroom. Any-
how, there were no spare spaces in the rooms,
Albani filling up the debutantes’ gaps with her
diamonds and amiable smile. She and Beatrice
Lamb sat and chatted together a long time, and
the guests watched as if it were the first act of a
society drama they had paid to see.
The fair and placid Miss Lamb is uncommonly
popular in her own company, and lam told by a
lady who has shared her dressing-room that her
wardrobe never includes stilts. Quite a remarkable
omission for a leading lady, isn’t it ? While on the
topic of this co., you maybe interested to know
that Miss Gethyn Darragh says she is not married
to anyone or anything, except her work and her
Frenchy frocks, which first brought her into notice
when she was playing insignificant parts in Charles
Wyndham’s company.
In between the vice-regai balls came St. Vin-
cent’s hop. For this the Town Hall was trimmed
up with red and yellow poppies, banana trees and
looking-glasses, and much greenery paid light
compliments to the Irish element of the evening.
Lady Rupert Clarjte further underlined this com-
pliment with a dress of deepest, brightest green,
such as one rarely sees after March 17th. That
dress got into my eye, and I may be wrong when
I say that Albani wore pale-green satin. I have
also an irresistible inclination to tell you that
Mrs. Douglas Mackenzie, one of the two hard-
working secretaries, was gowned in green, but
hasten myself out of temptation by recording
Mrs. Prendergast’s hand-painted princess robe of
white corded silk, and oretty Mrs. Caro’s rose-
colored brocade.
A feature of the big public balls of the day is
the gallery crowd, who pay two shillings to sit
and watch the wheels go round below them. The
gallery comes early and stays late. It has no
supper, and nobody takes any notice of it.
Society reporters never ask it what it wears. It
is not invited to even as much as a quadrille, and
I don’t like to dwell on its feelings when the ball-
room is emptied into the supper haunts, and it
sits and gazes hungrily at the decorations. And
yet—and yet, that gallery goes away in the small
hours of the morning, unfed and stiff with sitting,
but satisfied, and able to rattle off, “ I seen Lady
’Ampden at the ball the other night in fawn satin
and Mrs. ’Arris was in black it was a lovely ball
I did enjoy it so much I am surprised you wasn’t
there.” Two shillings is a trivial price to pay for
this knowledge.
Another ballroom feature, but not a slow one,
is the new sj;yle of dancing. Everything is
quicker than possible, and you only do half-a-
dozen turns at a time. At St. Vincent’s some of
the vice-regal party danced as if for a wager, and
the music ran away from the musicians, and the
dancers ran away from the music. Annie Sum-
merbelle, who has not been heard in public for a
long time, played, as an extra, a waltz of her own
(“ The Almida ”) that will be published in a few
days. You will be glad to hear that this clever
young musician has several new compositions
coming on shortly. For the last few years she has
been supporting herself and her little girl by
giving music-lessons.
The last day of the races took everybody to
Randwick, although fatigue scored heavily in
many well-known feminine faces. “ The profes-
sion ” was well represented, and a beauty actress
was heard loudly lamenting at the end of the day
that there was nothing left her but the stage.
Mrs. Hunt, who used to be Miss Lizzie Marks,
combined smartness with girlishness in her grey-
and-apricot frock ; and I liked a white silk dress
made with a three-flounced skirt that touched the
ground on all sides. All the new walking skirts
are as long as last year’s ball dresses. They
trail a little all round, and look charming the first
day, but fade about the hem as quickly as a spring
flower. Randwick saw the first decided move in
favor of pinafores for grown-ups. There were
half-a-dozen of these on the lawn, and a smart
American lady’s black dress, made with a very full
back and a carefully-fitted pinafore in front, was
novel, and looked better than it reads. The most
unfashionable female in the place was Miss West,
whose sly win was hooted wildly by the crowd.
While on the subject of animals, have you heard
that London gave a dramatic matinee lately in
aid of starving cats ? I won’t say much about it,
as the starvation time for gouli-goulis—which
their other name is mosquitoes—is just setting in,
and I am thinking of getting up a dramatic
matinee for myself and the rest of us. But cats /
And the theatre was lent by Hawtrey, who must
have seen a good many starving women and chil-
dren in his time. This matinee business explains
why so many English unmatched females keep
pussies instead of husbands. The eats go shares
with them in the cats’-meat. I heard of an old
lady to whom a magpie attached itself in the
direst days of her poverty. She used to go every
Saturday, in shabby black, to the butcher, with
maggie trotting after her, and ask timidly for “ a
ha’p’orth of meat for my magpie.” And the but-
cher used to take her halfpenny, and give her
half-a-dozen little scraps that drop from joints
when they are cut off the animal, and then she
would go home, and play she was maggie, and
dole herself out little bits of meat as long as they
lasted, while the other maggie caught worms in
the back-yard. To bring this story to a fine con-
clusion, I ought to tell you that the poor old lady
ate the worms and gave the meat to maggie, but
don’t you think it’s harrowing enough as it stands?
Ever since Albani came to Australia, the saying
has been: “wait till you hear her in oratorio.”
“The Messiah, ”on Saturday, gave us the first oppor-
tunity for this, but it gave us a diamond belt as
well. When you know that that belt cost five
thousand pounds, you won’t mind my mentioning
it at the beginning of this notice. The diamonds
were so big and brilliant and convincing that
Albani did well to keep them out of sight until
the end of her visit to Botany Bay. The rest of
her toilette tried hard to live up to the belt, and
succeeded better than you would expect, with
jewelled feathers, and red corsage roses, and a
Worth-y black frock that sparkled all over with
jewelled trimming.
“ The Messiah ” had advanced half-an-hour on
his journey before the Star put in her appearance.
Then she came rustling along like a wave of the
sea, bowed to the audience, wheeled round and
bowed hard to the Philharmonic, jumped up at
Hazon and shook his hand effusively over the
conductor’s box, and, when her bowing was
over, took a seat beside Orlando Harley. Miss
Berry, in white satin, and Mr. Pauli sat together
on the other side of the platform, and Pauli’s
toothache created a lot of sympathy among
damsels who could see the agonising facial contor-
tions that chased themselves round the baritone’s
countenance.
By the way, rumor says that the same Herr
Pauli is going to stay in Australia, and give comic
opera a new Ryley presently.
And, now that I am come to the point when a
few words about the Oratorio cannot be put off
any longer, I remember that the company have
left us for ever,'and that Saturday is dead as
the roses of yesteryear, and my criticism gabbles
itself out in one clause. Albani was fine and
fervent, but not in the least hair-raising; Miss
Berry and the two gentlemen were like little men
in big clothes ; and all that was sensational was
lent by Hazon and our own admirably-trained
Philharmonic people.
From the Tree of Life to the Tree of Knowledge
is a few steps down. This latter shrub sprang up
at the Criterion Theatre two days before its time,
and consequently there were not as many people
as there should have been at the first night’s per-
formance. A little pruning away of the interludes
is the only improvement you could ask. The play
is up to the very last minute, the staging is most
select, and the dressing will appeal to everyone
who prefers a subtle, simple frock to paralysingly-
smart raiment.
Miss Lamb and Miss Darragh divide the dresses
between them. The beautiful, wicked woman,
who is the Tree and Miss Lamb combined, is also
a dream of dear dresses. That tea-gown she wears
in the Manor drawing-room is a remarkable thing
even in tea-gowns, which have a special license of
their own to be remarkable. As far as I could see,
it was built up of primrose satin with an extrava-
gantly embroidered front, and full, sweeping,
back-breadths that creep out imperceptibly from
a little, white, jewelled yoke. It gave me a keen
aesthetic pleasure to watch Miss Lamb move about
the drawing-room in this noble creation. But it
went to my heart to see Cartwright toss her round
the room with all those breadths at stake.
Miss Darragh plhys the slim and graceful in-
genue with a piquant, pretty face that reminds
you somewhat of Ellen Terry. She wears her
clothes with an elegance that is quite touching,
when you consider that she is only twenty, and
has to dress in muslins and girlv-girly silks. I
wanted to see the wardrobe she bought after she
came into her fortune, and would like to have a
long look at her wedding dress, but the play saves
itself from insolvency by ending up just before the
nuptials. Miss Stewart, who played the maid in
“ A Marriage of Convenience,” is transformed
into a very nice old lady this time. I wonder the
Major didn’t fall in love with her. Nom Rickards
was pressed into the service at a moment’s notice,
and took Miss Morley’s place as the housemaid.
Everyone will be sorry to know that the latter
was involved in a Centennial Park horse-accident
(involving several facial surgical stitches) last Sun-
day, and will be unable to appear for a while.
Now that the festive season is on the way with
us, you may like to know of some very smart
evening blouses that Hordern’s in Pitt-street have
just imported. For theatres the blouse is univer-
sal, and, after ten years hard wear, and innumer-
able modifications, and reconstructions, it still re-
mains the most popular feature of feminine frock-
ing.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The news of Mr. Champion’s inheritance
(.£7OOO a year) is a very, very hard knock to Melb.
girls. If only the “ close creature ” had tell’t them
of his prospects in time, how they tuould have ‘ sat
beside him on mossy banks and stuck musk-roses
in his sweet, large ears,’ so to speak. Certainly,
all along there were rumors of an aristocratic
English fiancee, but a long, strong pull by the
local feminines might have put her nose out of
joint. Such things have happened before now.
Even as it was, the lucky legatee had his faithful
flock of disciples—girl-graduates and such-like —
who wrote slashing hang-prudery columns on
woman’s rights and things in the Champion.
“A Yankee, talking of Nat. Goodwin’s acting
in *ln Mizzoura,’ complained that Jim Radburn
was not half as well pourtrayed in Australia as in
’Murka. ‘ Why,’ said he, ‘ when Nat. played
the part here he left out half his good business.
In the States, after every half-dozen sentences,
Nat. would send out a stream of tobacco-juice
across the stage as nat’ral as life. It beats
me why he dropped the “business” over here.’
Paul Bourget, in ‘ Outre-Mer,’ mentions Good-
win’s fine performance of this part, and mentions,
too, the touch of ’Murkan nature he lends to it
with ‘ his far-streaming jets of saliva.’ Ugh !
“ Conventionists, crowing confidently on their
own native roosts again, hark back to the
grievances of their Melb. sojourn. Apparently
none of them covet our ‘Old Man of the Sea.’
Probably he riled them at the ‘ Big House ’ with
the tolerant mien of a village squire welcoming
charity-brats to tea and buns—-and this after
buttering up their Federation oratory ‘as unsur-
passed in the Imperial Parliament! ’ Naturally,
potentates who take themselves seriously as Con-
stitution-builders, history-makers, nation-founders
and such-like, objected to waiting meekly on the
mat till dilatory flunkeys chose to open the hos-
pitable doors. Neither did they relish being
harangued in the lump, when they went out to
dinner, like so many hospital-nurses or pilots in
need of intellectual enlightenment.
“Fearful and wonderful fit, those baggy plaid
shooting - pants of Baron Gilbert’s, in ‘ The
Babes.’ They remind me of 1 the bride’s device,’
in a German comic paper. The fond frau is no
tailoress, but where there’s a will there’s a way,
and the picture shows fat, elderly Fritz in his red-
flannel kilt, extending his nether man on the out-
spread homespun. Meantime, plump Gretchen,
his brand-new wife, kneels alongside, and scissors
the shape of his legs out of the material.
“One night, lately, an inmate of the Princess
d.c. was found fault with for his clear, bell-like
laugh, . His innocent mirth annoyed his neighbors,
perhaps because, when they giggled, he couldn’t
see the joke, and, when the rest of the audience
felt sick and sorry, his hysterical neighing rubbed
the mourners the wrong way. When Gilbert’s
weird dog was shot to pieces, the hyena onlooker
yah-yahed for so long without taking breath that
people got frightened, and reasoned with him for
his own good.
“ Re the Salvation Army warrior who wooed a
sister-soldier without the Kernel’s consent, there’ll
never be much peace in religious circles till clerical
celibacy is compulsory again.
“ T’other day, for instance, when the bachelor-
shepherd of a suburban kirk plighted his troth to
a ewe-lamb of the fold his Elders (so 'tis said)
visited him officially, lamented his choice as
‘ worldly,’ and wound up by wrestling fervently
in prayer that their dear young pastor might see
his way to renounce the donah. Unlike the re-
pentant Hallelujah lad aforesaid, the meenister, I
hear, rose from his knees obdurate and dry-eyed,
and though further entreated (this time by women
‘ well-wishers ’) not to shut his eyes to such tokens
of parochial unrest as anarchy in the Girls’ Guild
and spiritual deadness in the. Ladies’ League, the
culprit got married almost immediately, and is
now taking the consequences.
“ The late Ernest Parker had been ailing for
quite a year, and recently his illness seems to have
affected his manner in a marked degree, till at
times he appeared, for short spells, quite eccentric.
One of his recent peculiarities was to let his hair
grow, and nothing would induce him to submit
his head to the barber’s shears. Acquaintances,
as a gentle hint, would present him with three-
penny pieces, which he duly strung together on a
ring and displayed as a trophy. Although
he drew a good income from the shipping business,
festive ‘Ernie’ is said to have left his widow (a
daughter of R. E. Lewis) and children but slen-
derly provided for. Mr. Parker, who was so
swarthy as to be more than once ‘ Ranjied ’ by
irreverent street boys, bore a quite startling
resemblance to a well-known Sydney medico, the
son of a fashionable family. The two men were
so often mistaken for one another, and addressed
by the double's name, that they grew accustomed
to unexpected handshakes from ‘ friends ’ they
had never seen before.
“ When last heard of, the Brasseys’ fellow-pas-
sengers were in great glee over the successful
inauguration of their weekly concerts. Poor
wretches; probably by this time ‘the Ancient
Mariner’s ’ readings have pretty well deaved them
to death. The sound of the Stickit Orator’s own
voice seems to be self-intoxicating, and when once
started there seems to be no holding him. Do you
remember the night he took his elocutionary bit
between his teeth at that ’long-shore religious
entertainment? {Starting with Tennyson on the
‘ Death of the Dook,’he dished up sample after
sample, each gloomier than the last ;and the more
gruesome he sounded the jollier he looked. The
Sunbeam was lying out, and it was a dirty night,
so for his fad’s sweet sake the Tedious Tar had
braved two nasty trips. But he left in high good
humor, and actually remembered to forward a neat
little cheque later on. The recipients could
scarcely believe their eyes when the donation
came, ’cause once before when his Ex. landed un-
expectedly for matins, with ‘ salt spray frozen in
his hair and salt tears in his eyes,’the top offertory
score, 1 understand, was only half-a-crown, and
even that small mercy couldn’t be conclusively
traced to the ‘Worshipper-in-chief.’
“ Society scribes commend Lady Brassey’s con-
descension in arranging for her small daughter to
au revoir her teachers the day before the chick
and her governess followed Pa and Ma to Albany.
The same chroniclers like referring to little Miss
B. as ‘a Vere de Vere,’ just as if fond old Dad
had no finger in the pie.
“The ‘extra’ ladies in the Bland Holt co. are
all recruited from the ranks of sassiety amateurs,
which explains why the female ‘supers’ in
Holtian melodrama always know how to manage
a train, wear decolletfi gowns and wield a fan with
grace and as to the manner born. Miss Adele
Kerr, who fills a small partin ‘The Cotton King,’
is a much-admired Melb. belle whose brilliant
hair and Junoesque figure were always in re-
quest for tableaux and amateur-theatrical per-
formances.
“Fanny Wiseman, who is playing ‘ Topsy ’ with
an ‘Uncle Tom’s Cabin ’ co. round the suburbs, is
the Topsy of the original co. which played the
pious cork-black play for three months on end at
the old Melb. Princess’s. The lady is now consider-
ably past her fiftieth year, yet, ‘ blacked-up,’ she
appears the identical 12-year-old Topy and acts
with the same go and spirit which made the part
popular 20 years agone.
“ At Heidelberg (Vic.) an old couple who run a
milkery were recently both ‘ down ’ with typhoid.
Until infection risk should cease, the sale of milk
from their dairy was sternly prohibited by the
local health officer. But fresh cases kept on de-
veloping in the locality and the anxious health-
man called again, to find that the benevolent
cowkeepers had obediently stopped all sales as
ordered and distributed the milk gratis instead.
The intelligent neighbours had gladly taken all
chances, and gratefully called for their free
typhoid-germs daily.
“The usefulness of bloomers. A Melb. lady
was thrown from her horse when riding on a
country road the other day. Her skirt caught on
the pummel, and she hung for a perilous moment.
Then the waistband gave, and the rider slid out
of her skirt, and sat on the ground in her bloomers.
Recovering, she quietly walked along homewards
after her horse, which reached the house long
before its rider. Its appearance brought out the
terrified family in search of the lost one, whom
they soon espied looking spruce and untorn. A
bloomerless woman under the above circumstances
would have had to lurk about behind trees till
nightfall.
“‘The Church Missionary Association ’ issues
frisky little leaflets periodically. The handbill
describing ‘An Australian Eleven ’ sportively re-
marks : ' These “ Eleven ” are some of our Repre-
sentatives “ fielding ”in far off lands. May they
excel “AH England ” in grace! May they per-
fume all the East with the fragrance of a holy life. ’
Seethe pun in ‘Grace?’ Also, if the devoted
band can carry out the aspiration in the last line
and go one better than ‘ the immemorial East ’ in
smells, they must indeed be fragrant neighbors.
Lower George-street would be a garden of roses
compared with their humpy. By the way,
why ‘East,’ when a Melb. writer means China?
Murkans don’t call us the antipodes, although
Englishers do. We Australians are too tired to
adjust the compass for ourselves. Of Miss A.
Nisbet, a misguided young woman who has
foolishly been allowed to go to Ku Cheng, the
bio-paragraphist safely states that she ‘ only
escaped martyrdom by being in Hobart on fur-
lough on that ever-nremorable Aug. 1, ’95.’ This
is the latest form of advt., the assertion-negative :
‘ She was not murdered in the Ku Cheng
massacre.’ ‘Mr. Wilson Barrett has not fol-
lowed the story in dramatising “ The Manxman. ” ’
‘Messrs. Tom, Dick and Harry are not in favor
of the Commonwealth Bill.’
“‘Elijah,’ with a disgracefully-unrehearsed
chorus, was the last Albani oratorio in Melb.
Exhibition. Evidently chosen to give Pauli a
show, as the baritone has all the fat in the above
work. Elijah can’t be a tenor —too manly a char-
acter to have stage-lovery high notes allotted him,
and a bass is so uninteresting. I’ll swear there are
no tenors in the Wesleyan heaven. Ravens
wouldn’t have bothered giving a basso gratis
victuals. Therefore, all the frumps in camphor-
smelling, creased wraps who turned out en masse
to have such a treat on Easter Monday night
weren’t disappointed. ‘ They went to worship
sinful man,’ like J. Davidson’s nun, thinking
all the time how elevating oratorio is, mingling
with the mystic, and satisfying the sensuous and
the spiritual simultaneously. Albani wore black,
with a surface glitter of sparkling something-or-
other, and, during the waits, donned a frothy,
frilly fichu, for the Ex. is a Sahara of draughts,
and an expensive prima-donna must wrap up.
The co’s. departure per Sydney express next day
was uneventful. Mme. wore a tailor-made, and,
though the day was warm, muffled a boa round her
neck. Somebody gave her a yellow posy, and she
went off still smiling. The Naicest all adore
Albani; say a word other than grovelling about
her in a drawingroom, and you are frowned into
oblivion.
“ Don’t you hate readers who deface circuiating-
library books by tomtit comments or dragged-iti
foot-notes ? In the ‘ Life ’ of explorer Franklin,
for instance, the author remarks incidentally that,
when the subject of the biography went to Tas-
mania as Governor, his niece, not mentioned by
name, accompanied the vice-regal family-party.
I came across a library copy of this work wherein,
at every casual reference to this unimportant,
anonymous kinswoman, a pencilled note appears
in the margin, ‘Mrs. Price,’or ‘Mother of Captain
Tom Price.’
“At local entertainments people sitting any-
where near ‘ the übiquitous couple ’ (you know
the big, beef man and the pretty little woman) are
reminded of the days when they (the hearers) used
to thrill over penny dreadfuls. Neither of the
omnipresent twain are taciturn or low-voiced, and
their favorite topics are the faults of poor, absent
hubby. Miserable wretch, how his long ears must
tingle ! Even his parting kisses and tears are
chronicled in detail. Maybe those ‘ tears ’ are
mythical, but what matter, so long as the tradi-
tion answers the purpose of making the big, beefy
man jealous ?
“ Travelling Australians are finding out that
the much-cracked-up English domestics are a
delusion and a snare. A Melb. lady, now settled
in Bays water, London, writes disgustedly to her
family here : ‘I have five servants to do the work
which two Australian girls would undertake and
think nothing of. The nurse whom I brought
from Melbourne can run rings round the other
four. They are all so uppish, too, and Jane won’t
dream of doing any of Mary Ann’s duties, and
Sarah would ‘ wish to go when my month is hup,
m’m,’ if asked to take hold and give a hand on
Jane’s job. How I long for a friendly Antipo-
dean general who is nurse, parlor-maid, cook and
laundress in one, and is always breaking her own
record in each department—also most of the
crockery. ’

the ‘ grand tour ’ twice, and will probably make
his third with his future wife as a honeymoon trip ’
(italics mine). ‘ Mr. B went over to Sydney
on Tuesday to be married to Miss F , the
Sydney blonde!’ Judging from that last descrip-
tion, fair people must be even rarer in N.S.W.
than here.

S. Chief Justice Way publicly intimated
a week or two before his marriage that his judicial
position would prevent the acceptance of wedding
presents. His wife brought him a ready-made
family, the eldest son being about 19 years old.
R regenerated women. Just when a cer-
tain French Consul of long ago had got settled in
Australia, the rumor buzzed that the Countess,
who did the honors so charmingly, was the famous
Mogador, of the Cancan at the Jardin Mabille.

test the quasi-authoritative Chamberlainesque
rumor that the title of P.C., conferred in bulk at
the Jubilee fete, would, as an Imperial recogni-

liaments.
Dr. Cockriurn stands a good chance of
being made Federal Agent-General if Australian
union eventuates. Reeves (M.L.), Perceval (Tas.),
C‘ark( Vic.) and Tozer (Q.) are out of the running,
and his claims are certainly treater than those of
either Wictenoom (W.A.) or Joseph Abbott—the
probable next N.S.W. Agent-General.
Up-to-date : Hard lines on Donald Mac-
lo'sing his through the post; but
why didn’t he insure it? George Robertson and
Co., when forwarding the MS. of Dr. Muskett’s
recently-published book from Sydney to Mel-
bourne, insured it. Any office, doing burglary or
accident business, will take these risks.
Josh. Symon, Q.C., who was left years
ago to carry solus the whole of the liabilities of
several land-boom syndicates, has, after immense
toil, paid all his debts, and is now reported to be
many thousands of pounds to the good. His com-
mercial ideals, like those of Mark Twain, are high,
and it has cost him years of unflagging work to live
up to them.

Once, when Aspinall was briefed for a de-
fence, through some blunder be got bold of plain-
tiff's brief, a few moments before the case began.
a lll,rrifJ d investigation of its contents.'
.Plaintiffs man was late in court. When he ar-
rived, Aspinall was in the thrill of his explanation
of plaintiff s claim. Apprised of the mistake, he
went on to the end, and then said, “ Your Honor
I find that, through some extraordinary blunder’
I have got hold of the wrong brief. My friend’
Mr. Dawson, appears for plaintiff. But I don’t
think that anything I have said has in any way
prejudiced the interests of his client, since I have
merely set forth the history of a claim which I
think the gentlemen of the jury will presently
discover hasn’t a leg to stand upon. Where’s my
brief ? ’’—and he sat down to get some notion of
the defence.
Bowler Richardson, when talking of pedi-
grees, gives his breeding as “ a cross between
Irish and gipsy.”
Wm. Go re Elliston, son of the old-time
actor, was proprietor of the Courier in ancient
Hobart days. He was very dignified—inherited
the pomp and majesty his father
affected. His speech and gesture
were “ affluent.” And it was as
“good as a play” to see him, as
church-warden, collect the shekels
every Sunday morning in old St.
David s. Once, his dignity was
sorely tested. But it came out
all right. A fat little subaltern
of the OGth was fast asleep in the
officers’ pew, when Plliston came
around with the plate and held it
just above the little man’s head.
A brother officer stuck a pin into
the sleeping man’s thigh. He
jumped up with an oath. His
head clashed against the offer-
tory-plate and the donations of
the faithful were scattered.
Elliston, with great majesty of
demeanour, carried the empty
plate to old parson Bedford.
“ Eh, what’s this ?—what’s this,
Elliston ? ” whispered Bedford.
“ The soldiers, sir,” answered
Elliston aloud, “have wasted
the vineyard of the Lord.”
“ Then the soldiers ought to be
ashamed of themselves,” puffed
the old .man; “but set the
verger to pick up the money.”
And he went on with the service.
Tom Coward, a well-known
Queensland resident of many years’
standing, has some interesting re-
miniscences of Arthur Palmer. “ A
medium man with a long beard, who
invariably walked with his left hand
in his pocket and with his right hand
on his beard; head bent, as if in thought.
In shearing times you’d often hear his
forcible tones: ‘ Shear those sheep, don’t
butcher them.’ ” (Just fancy old Palmer
talking in this milk-and-water strain!)
“His language was sometimes harsh, bordering 1
on the profane ” ! Bordering on the profane is
superb.

Some of the ’9B men transported to Botany
and Van Diemen’s Land : John Hayes, of
Vaueluse, who banished the snakes from his
grounds by laying down a barrier of Irish earth
specially imported for the purpose. Holt, who
was afterwards suspected of inspiring the “ revo-
lution” of Pennant Hills. Henry Despard, who
escaped, and was killed in fight against the Eng-
lish in the Peninsula. Dry, father of Richard
Dry, Premier of Tasmania. Tony Brady,
Cock of the North, who died in Sydney in the
sixties, aged 91 years. Arthur Carew, who lived
some years of his exile in Northern Tasmania, and
whose sentence was afterwards remitted. He
went home and died on his estates. These are
a few of many who have, apparently, been for-
gotten.
Eleanor Marx Aveling (a daughter of Karl
Marx), who has suicided in London, was one of
the ugliest women the writer has met, and one of
the most interesting. She was fat, floppy, and
thick-featured, with coarse, dark hair, and she
wore spectacles. She was fearfully and wonder-
fully learned. She was the first translator of
Ibsen’s “Lady From the Sea,” and did many
other translations for the publishers, for which she
got no credit and not much cash. When writer
knew her she lived with Dr. Edward Aveling
(author of ‘ The Student’s Darwin ” and scientific
and socialist books) in some offices fitted up as
living rooms in Chancery Lane. Aveling is a
gnomish little man with a big head and huge
goggle eyes, who at one time had the reputation
of being the best scientific teacher in London.
Recently-deceased Tommy Wells, of Melb.
Argus, had a pathetic experience connected with
his writing-up a small-pox scare. Tommy had
visited the infected ships and interviewed the
medical men who had treated the patients, but
not till his copy was filed did it strike him that
he himself might bo carrying about small-pox in-
fection. As a model husband, Tommy cou’d not
stay away from home that night, but neither could
he run the risk of introducing the dread disease to
his own fireside, so he compromised by getting
over the back-fence, stripping himself in the fowb
house, and hanging up his suspected garments on
the clothes-line. But hereupon awoke Wells’s
faithful watchdog, and, not recognising his master
in the “altogether,” went so vigorously for his
legs that Tommy was forced to take to the roof of
the woodshed, and there spent a very bad quarter
of an hour before he succeeded in convincing the
inconvenient tyke of his identity.

From a recent Saturday’s Age :
A pathetic little incident occurred in connection with
the first drawing-room of the season, which was held by
the Queen in person on Friday last. For the first time
her Majesty wore large gold-rimmed spectacles, through
which she regarded the ladies who made their obeisance
before her.
But “ large gold-rimmed spectacles ” aren’t neces-
sarily “ pathetic.” They are only Chinese.
Dear B. —He that uninteresting cable
about Lord Rosebery scratching his horses. Per-
haps the cableman knew that Rosebery has two
uncles in Queen-land —the Primroses. One of
them is a stock-and-station agent in Toowoomba,
the other runs a mineral-water factory in Brisbane
—in fact, is the proprietor of Helidon Spa. The
children of the former are the “deceased expecto-
rate ” of Rosebery.—A.

Thomas Joseph Byrnes, aged 38, is the
youngest Premier in Australia. He obtained a
seat in Q. Leg. Assembly only five years ago,
having previously sat as a nominee in the Council.
“ In my day,” old Parkes used to say, “gentlemen
sat in the Assembly 10 and 12 and 14 years before
they ambitioned the acceptance of a portfolio ;
now they hardly warm their seats before they
reach out for the Premiership.”
One dark and stormy night, old Jack
Robertson, making for the Watson’s Bay boat,
fell into the water. “What's that?” cried the
skipper of the steamer, hearing the splash. “ The
blanky Government, blank your eye!” puffed
John, holding on to a pile! “Look alive!”
They hauled the old hoy out, rubbed him down,
and gave him a drink. “All right, my dear
fellows,” said he. “ The country’s saved. But it
had a damn near chance of being swamped that
time.”
At Sydney Temperance Hall, the other
night, Mrs. Harrison Lee, the water-spoutist,
instanced Edison, Nansen, and others as warriors
of the teetotal cause, notable examples of the
effects of cold-tea. Which is about as reasonable
as it would be to cite Bismarck as an instance of
the virtues of much beer and boundless tobacco.
And since Edison—like Bismarck—is a “chain-
smoker”—smokes all the times, that is, and lights
each (green) cigar front the butt of its predecessor
—The Bulletin fails to see why every other man
shouldn’t be allowed to select his own particular
line of intemperance.
John Donnelan Balfo, who was connected
with the ’4B trouble in ould Ireland (on the Govt,
side), catne out to Tasmania to push his fortunes.
He was a fine speaker, and went in for politics
when the little island got her constitution. And,
of course, to begin with, he was always in opposi-
tion. On one occasion he inveighed against the
“ bribery and corruption ” of the Government.
At which T. D. Chanman, then Premier, retorted :
“ It is hardly fair, Air. Speaker, for the honorable
member to employ his expert knowledge in bribery
and corruption in an attack upon the Government.
The honorable member bears the reputation—l
know not how justly—of having employed his un-
doubted talents as a spy for the British Govern-
ment during the reeent rebelli >n in Ireland. The
instinct may be still strong within him. But he
will find no room for its employment in this coun-
try. The gentleman would, therefore, do well to
educate his abilities in the direction of honorable
pursuits.” It sounded like an 18th century
speech. But it shut up Balfe for some time.
Old Jack Robertson had a healthy dislike
of official pomp or ceremony. One time, he ac-
companied the then Governor on a New England
trip. Half-a-dozen troopers were on escort duty.
Jack noticed that the horses fretted a good deal —
the clang and clatter of the troopers’ swords and
accoutrements made them nervous—so lie halted
the drag. “Look here,” he snuffled, to the officer
in charge, “just you have j'our men unsling those
blanky fire-irons, and put ’em in the drag. Then
your horses won’t worry, and I sha’n’t swear my
soul to tatters.” Then to the Governor, “ I’ve
been longing, your Excellency, for a chance to
relieve my feelings.” “Go ahead, Mr. Robert-
son,” said Hercules R., “ I know how you feel.”
One who declared himself to be “the only
living member of the Burke and Wills exploring
expedition” asked Wellington (M.L.) Benevolent
Trustees for help, the other day. Francis—that
was the name given by the tall and elderly sur-
vivor—said that he lately shook 12 years N.S.W.
dust off his weary feet. Thirty-three years ago he
had sojourned in Victoria, and 25 years ago he
was in M.L. His legs and his heart had given out
in Sydney. He knew Soddon, Jock M'Kenzie
and Carroll in the long ago. The trustees gave
the old man a rest in the Ohiro Home pending
further enquiries. Apropos, there are as many
“ sole survivors ” of the Burke and Wills party as
there are of the Balaclava charge. The official
list of the original party is as follows: R. O’H.
Burke (leader), G. J. Landells, W. J. Wills, Thos.
Beckler, Ludwig Beekler, C. Ferguson, T.
M‘Donough, W. Patton, Patrick Langan, Owen
Cowan, W. Brake, R. Fletcher, Jno. King, Hy.
Crocker, Jno. Drakeford. The names of Wright
and Grey do not appear, as they joined the party
after they had started. The hero of the above
par. may also have done so, but •‘here is no record
of the fact.
“The story goes,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, “that the present Duke
of Portland is Sidney George Druce, of Sydney,
N.S.W. The matter has been made public here
with the duke’s name omitted, and a grave is to
be opened in Highgate Cemetery to see if a cer-
tain coffin contains a roll of lead instead of a
mouldering skeleton. Here is the tale : The sth
Portland duke, Wm. Jno. Cavendish-Bentinck,
is supposed to have led a double life as Thomas
Charles Druce. C. hyphen 8., unmarried, died
without issue in 1879, when the great-grandson of
the 3rd duke, a penniless officer in the Guards,
succeeded, through three sudden deaths in eight
months, to the estates and title. T.C.D. lived
with a woman for years, and several illegitimates
were born ; then married her, and a month or so
afterwards a son Walter arrived, prior to the sup-
posed death of his father. Walter married, and
the twenty-one-year-old had in Sydney is his heir.
T.C.D. is supposed to have been buried in 1564;
C-B. died in 1579, and fairly circumstantial
evidence points to the two being one. T.C.D.
was the owner of the Baker-street Bazaar, Pad-
dington; frequently disappeared for varying
periods, was eccentric, and underneath the house
were various tunnels he had made in the earth.
C-B. almost lived in the subterranean passages he
had constructed all over his estate, was supposed

The two patriots, Dwyer and Davis, whose
remains will take star parts in N.S.W. 170 S cele-
bration, pursued totally different ways of living
when transported to Botany Bay. Dwyer, in Ire-
land, was a fighter, an irreconcilable, irrepressible
rebel, ready to go to any extremes in the teeth of
British rule; but, on landing at Botany Bay, he
donned a mild, genial, peace-loving manner, was
strictly observant of the rules of the System, and
respectful and docile to the representatives of
authority. In a short time he obtained a ticket-
of-leave, won the esteem of those over him, and
was appointed to a Govt, billet. Davis, on the
other hand, who never really struck a blow in
Ireland but was accused of making pikes for the
insurgents, and whose temper had been uniformly
mild, exhibited on arrival at Botany Bay a con-
tumacious contempt for local ordinances and for
all who tried to enforce them. He frequently ex-
ploded into fierce defiance, and was several times
triced up and flogged. Although he afterwards
prospered in business and became wealthy, he
never took to the Government, nor the Govern-
ment to him.

Aboriginalities.
Our doctor and a huge bushman had been
“on the tear ” for some days before they had a
row, which reached that stage at which the bush-
man demanded an apology. “ ’Pology, pology,”
said the doctor, whiskily. “ See you d d
first.” Then as he saw the man of timber draw-
ing off to strike—“ Tell you wot I will do f’ you,
ole chap. I’ll pull couple o’ teeth f’ you ! ” The
large man from the country looked at him with the
trembling eye of a week’s continual firewater.
“ Right y’are ; that’ll do. Yer got to do some-
thing,” and then, arm-in-arm, the apologist and
the apologee reeled up the street to the surgery.
“ Now,” said the doctor, with a hoarse laugh, “ is
there any ’ticular tooth y’ want to get rid of.”
“No; pull away. They’re all ’bout the same!”
They were, as a matter of fact, all perfect. The
doc. grabbed his rusty forceps, and after five
minutes’ hard graft he triumphantly waved in the
air, which was thick with bush-blasphemy, a per-
fect molar. “Now which one’ll you have out
next ? ” The doctor had tasted blood. “ No,
no,” gasped the burly one. “No more, no more.”
“Right y’are. Come on,” said the doo. And
then the affair, like everything else in Australia,
ended in filling ’em up again.

W.K.A. . ‘ The silent pass and shake of
the matchbox are universal in the bush. Probably
done to attract attention, for bushmen are silent
in their ways. Bush mates never bid each other
‘goodnight’ or ‘good morning,’ save, perhaps,
when they find themselves in a strange hotel, and
then they do it for sympathy. A bushman will
go through the farce of cutting up a pipe of
imaginary bacca; and his mates know at once he
is short of the requisite. Most bushmen turn into
blanket-bed before they take their hats off; and I
knew a squatter whose town house was a little
palace, who never went upstairs to bed without his
bat on. The real bushman is clean when he

Cattle up on the North Coast, N.S.W.,
are fond of chewing bones, doubtless on account
of some constituent —probably lime—lacking in
the pasturage. I remember seeing a beast killed
up that way, in the paunch of which was found a
piece of the shinbone of a bullock, about 4in. long,
and into the hole in the bone was jammed another
piece about 2in. long. The whole was polished as
smooth as a billiard-ball, and dyed a green color
from the juice of the animal’s daily food. In an-
other case, a cricket-ball was found in the stomach
of a bullock, in a hardly recognisable condition.
The station-hands used to practise cricket occa-
sionally, and one mighty smiter had lifted the
ball far and high into the adjoining paddock, when
the bullock, it was supposed, had been attracted
by the saltish taste of the ball, and had swallowed
it holus-bolus.—Alia.
* * * *
An idea of what “Sturt’s stony desert,”
far north-west N.S.W., is like after seasonable
rain. At the middle of February one station was
cutting mulga to keep life in its stock. Before the
end of March, five weeks afterwards, that station
was cutting Mitchell grass, 3ft. 6in. high, and
stacking it!

One more for the yellow man. A Chow,
who was getting some building-timber out at a
Riverina pine-mill, asked the manager to lay him
on to a good carpenter to put up his fan-tan em-
porium. “ Why you not get your own country-
men ?” he was asked—“ plenty Chinese carpenters
in Narandera.” “No feah,” said John, ; “my
countlee carpentler too muchee deah, want ’em l l
pong, 16 pong—no savee ! Englee man want ’em
5 pong—6 pong—put ’em all up. You savee? ”
* * » *
There is a queer cairn at Marthavale,
Gippsland (Vic.), built by an old miner. Some
years back this man and a mate worked on the
river bank, paddocking. There was a slip, and
Croft was smothered. Holmes, his mate, worked
in the district for many years afterwards, and
never passed the scene of the accident without
dropping a stick upon the spot. Eighteen years
accumulations now stand as a monument over the
departed Croft, and the pile is reverenced by resi-
dents, and of late years has been dignified as the
habitat of a moody spook that sits on top for ever
filling a spectral pipe with spiritual “ toby.”

Werri: Record tramps.
An old Warrego battler,
known as the “ Rainmaker,”
in one day trudged from
Eulo to Cunnamulla, a good
50 miles ; but he couldn’t re-
peat it the second day. A
little man named Sam Smith
stepped the 45 miles from
Deniliquin to Echuca in 9|
hours, with a 281 b. swag, a
bottle of whisky, and one of
his own dampers. And a
digger, hurrying to Tiboo-
burra specking field, in one
day went from Hungerford
to Hamilton’s Hut (Q. bor-
der), a hard 67 miles. His
load was a digger’s pick,
shovel, dish, billy, tucker,
and a camp-oven.
♦ * *
Lever and Steet were
working in an open-cut up
the hill where the granite
was hard and much “shoot-
ing ” was necessary. Larry,
a little, old, hard-faced Mile-
sian, was fossicking lower
down near the creek, and ab-
solutely refused to stand from
under when a charge was
touched off. If a splinter or
two of rock kicked up the
clay in his vicinity, Larry
simply turned, shook his fist,
and hurled curses up the hill
at Lever and his mate. One
afternoon Steet called out to
Larry, as usual, advising him
to get under cover, and
Larry, as usual, took no no-
tice, but bent over his mortar
and went on dollying, sulkily
ind ifferent. The fuse “ spat, ”
the dynamite kicked, and
junks of granite began to
move round, and Lever and
Steet, who always kept one
scared eye in Larry’s direc-
tion, saw a lump of metal as
big as a shoe-trunk hurtling
down-hill, making a bee-line
for the veteran. A wild yell
went up, but at the same
moment Larry and the mis-
sile disappeared over the em-
bankment together. Then
the mates, corpse-colored and
horrified, rushed down the
declivity to gather up the
mangled remains, and were
met by Larry, who came climbing over the
bank purple and dancing mad. “ I’ll have the
lore on yez ! I’ll have the lore on yez, so I will! ”
he shrieked. “ Look here, blast yez ! ye’ve broke
me poipe ! ”

“ Seven by Seven ” :• —
Dear Bullrtin.—“6 x B’s” recent remarks re cheap
labor, particularly among shed-hands, are quite correct,
The way that a man who takes contract work pays those
who actually do it is simply scandalous. I have travelled
all N.S. Wales, and have found the thing worst in New
England. For instance, at one shed I found the shearers
getting the current figure— viz., LI per ton. There were
six rouseabouts, and the boss had cooking for these, at
10s. a week, a man who had also in his spare time to roll,
unroll, and sweep, to say nothing of drafting, branding,
and penning-up and shepherding. The brother of tins
“ rousers’ ” cook had 12s. fid. per week for driving the
station team of 20 bullocks, and taking the wool to the
railway-station. He had a wife and six children to keep.
The owner of this station was worth i 100,000,'and had
come- to the locality 20 years previously humping his
bluey. Now he has one of the finest pastoral properties
in New England.

With full grey beard, turned-
down tieless collar and broad-
brimined hard hat, detective J. 0.
Carter, of Russell - street barracks
(Melb.), is the greatest “take-down”
of all the city detectives. He is a
brother of W. T. C., formerly Vie.
M.L.A., and now engineering the
Carter torpedo in London, and is
one of the oldest Gippsland natives
extant. In his youth, Carter was
the victim of a unique accident.
Riding a vicious buckjumper, he
was leaning back in the orthodox
way when the prad in some mysteri-
ous fashion managed to kick him on
the back of the head ! The pros-
pective d. had a near squeak for
his life, and half his off-duty time at
Russell-street is spent in trying to
make his colleagues swallow the
yarn.

“Grip ” to The Bulletin :
At the Svdney depot for the conversion
of cornstalks into mounted constables, a
system prevails which is as much out of
date as a Botany Bay bushranger. It is
possibly all right, by way of letting the
gas out of a raw, up-country recruit, with
a record for sitting anything that can
buck, to tell him straight that “he can’t
ride.” But the mounted drill, upon a
special steed with cast-iron fetlock and
steel-set sinews, and without stirrups, is
an eye-opener even to experienced bush
riders ; while the sword exercise, by
reason of the strain on hitherto untaxed
muscles, is often a terror to an other-
wise athletic man. And when to these
delights are added unnecessary brutality
of address, and raucous and continuous
bad language—the effect on the recruit is
often complete disgust, and “chuck-it.”
Much good material is thus lost, for the
probationer has not only to be personally
of good character but must also be tho-
roughly reputable in his connections.
Also, when the country—pretty »teep country, too
—of the Clarkes and Connells was swarming with
troopers (heavily handicapped, certainly), it was con-
temptuously declared by the native local gully-rakers
(crosses of centaur and yahoo) that a large percentage of
the mounted police couldn’t “ sit ” worth a cent in
timber, or on the ranges—with the foe in front. And
now says disgusted young bushie, full-up of the “ fooree
“Talked at ’sif we was dogs! If some of the Belmore
bosses ever find themselves up our way, we’ll show ’em
what riding is ! ” Anyway, so far as Young-male Aus-
tralia-city or backblocks-is concerned, a course of in-
struction in cuss-words and general blanky is entirely
superfluous. A judicious blend of strict discipline, indis-
pensable in the semi-military organisation, with such
consideration as is consistent with a civil constitution—
and the suffrage—would soon substitute a cheerful esprit
de corps for the present prevailing dissatisfaction.

* *
It’s common enough in the West to see
men and horses with their eyes bunged from the
attacks of the venomous little black fly that
imagines it has a right to make his nest in your
organ of vision. This year, however, has brought
forth the novel phenomenon of kangaroos afflicted
in the same way—even to the extent of positive
blindness. The luxuriant vegetation of an un-
usually wet season lias brought out the flies in
wonderful vigor and numbers.
——
Queensland’s new Premier—Hon. Tommy
Byrnes—is one of the few front-rank Australasian
politicians who have never tasted the bitters of
Opposition. In no sense has he fought his way to
the front, Pitchforked into the Leg. Council as
Solicitor-General —to secure the R.C. vote for the
Ministry of the day—-he was equally fortunate in
gliding into the Assembly for a sleepy pocket-
borough. The only stunning blow he has received
was at last general election, when Brisbane re-
jected him in favor of a commonplace, inarticulate
Scotsman named Fraser. All the same, Byrnes
is one of the most eloquent, capable, and pro-
mising mi'll in Australia, and his career —-should he
be allowed to have a career—as Premier will be
worth watching. And he is only 38 years old.
The selection of Tomjoe Byrnes as Nevermind
Nelson’s successor may yet burst the Q[. Coali-
tion bladder, in which event the noise will bo
Mr. T. J. BYRNES, the new Q. Premier.
Photo., The Wiley Studio, Brisbane.
heard and the stink felt afar off. Few of Banana-
land’s prominent politicians can afford to indulge
in personalities. Anyhow, it is pretty certain
that the squatter-huckster-boomster crowd in Q.
Leg. Council and Assembly won’t stand Byrnes at
any price—he is with them but not of them.
Under the circumstances, Byrnes’ first duty is to
appeal to the people ; but, seemingly, he isn’t
game. Anyway, if he gets a chance to keep in
power, The Bulletin hopes he’ll turn over a new
political leaf. He is intimately associated with,
perhaps, the most discreditable political gang this
country has known; and if, for the credit of
young Australia, he ever means to go straight, it
s quite time he began.

Business, Bobbery , etc.
The man who waits his chance to get even is in
evidence among the cashiers of big Sydney emporiums.
Sometimes during a rush he is “ woodened ” with a bad
or “holey” coin, and when a coin of same face value
comes along he pops in the cronk one instead and returns
it to the counter as no good, and the customer generally
parts up with a good one, although he swears by his gods
he hadn’t noticed anything wrong with it. Neither had
the counter girl, but she doesn’t emphasise the fact much.
* * * *
The sly-grog-shanty-keepers of inland Victoria
set at defiance the itinerary policeman who, it turns out,
has no power to interfere with them ; the excise officer
(Customs) being the only person entitled to act. Right
through from Warrnambool to Croagingolong there are
hundreds of distilleries, the owners of which are known
to the police, who daily swag the liquor warm from the
still, but the special detective can secure no convictions,
the “ bush telegraph ” being so effective that detection is
next to impossible. Melbourne liquor-sellers don’t supply
a tenth of the whisky drunk in the country districts, and
1 lie illicit local distillers are providing a good and palat-
able spirit underselling and undercutting almost 50
per cent., and making profit big enough to cover a possible
rattling fine. The local resident very rarely gives the
cronk distiller away; and when he does, he generally
suffers severely in his belongings—whether by the act of
God or of man he never finds evidence to prove.
* * • *
Vic. tradesmen, especially tailors, who for years
past have fattened upon garnishee orders in compelling
debtors to ante-up, have lately received ugly shocks.
Schools, in fact shoals, of travellers have inundated Govt,
and other offices, soliciting orders on easy (verbal) terms
for clothing or anything else the victim could be cajoled
to contract for : the bed-rock of recovery being the gar-
nishee order. These latter compulsory liquidations have
become so numerous and regular that the heads of depart-
ments (in which the debtors were employed) have, at last,
kicked at the extra labor imposed upon them of being
transformed into debt-collectors, and the creditors are
being quickly snuffed out, by a very simple process. A
garnishee order is only operative at the hour the salary is
due. If the order be served upon the boss before that
time he simply notifies the creditor to lift the same, other-
wise a summons to compel him to do so will at once be
issued. That is knock-out No. 1, but the smash on the
solar plexus is the bogus mortgaging by the underling, or
sub, of his salary for any amount he pleases to his boss ;
this puts him well on velvet. And that is how a clerk at
30s. a week somehow manages to dress at the rate of a
thousand a year
• • * *
Arthur :—“ If it were possible to analyse ‘ the
strength ’ of some of the principal firms in the wholesale
Australian trading community, it would be found that
sectarian funds have, to a great extent, supplied their
capital. Both a big hardware and a big preserving firm
rely almost wholly on Wesleyan investments at 6
per cent, per annum. It is quite common pro-
perty that the commonweal of a strange (and un-
canny) religious community is almost entirely de-
pendent upon the profits made by a timber biz.; whilst a
very prosperous Wesleyan grocery house carries a heavy
liability to the firm’s co-religionists, and two staunch
Orange timber-merchants stand almost wholly upon the
investments made in their business by R.O. institutions.
Three big Australian softgoods firms have either ‘deno-
minational ’ money invested in their undertakings or owe
their success to the founders of their businesses having
originally identified themselves with those religious
denominations that had the most loaves and fishes to
distribute at the time. The writer advises the young
commercial ‘ gent ’ (softgoods people alone address firms as
‘gents.’ Why, Heaven alone knows !) who wants to get
ahead not to go either west or to Klondike. Be a Baptist,
a Presbyterian, or a Wesleyan. Better still! Wear your
hair long, cover it with a curious and original hat. Swill
tea in public and found a sect of your own !! ! You’ll
assuredly be able, if you choose, to guzzle fizz in private
and in jolly and pretty company, too! Recollect! ye
embryo ‘ gents,’ that 500 or 5000 religious cranks —even in
this limited community—await exploitation by any
shrewd person who has ‘ nous ’ enough to run the reli-
gious racket on commercial principles.”

The people of a certain Australian city are
cursing the cast-iron impudence of a local daily in yelping
against municipal introduction of electric-lighting and
street wood-blocking. They note that the paper’s public-
spirited chairman of directors is a very large gas share-
holder. Wood-blocking would necessitate the removal of
the gas-mains to the footpaths. “La publique—c’est
moi!"
Rap • “ Attorney-General Isaacs’ new Vic. in-
solvency Act (much patched and damaged by the House
of 48 Rich Landlords), which only came into force on 1/1/98,
has already been found unworkable in its present form. It
is now officially notified that the Act will shortly be ‘repub-
lished in a revised form.’ Three of the leading Vic.
assignees, recently interviewed by writer, candidly admit
that as the Act stands at present, they ‘ dunno where
they are.’ Insolvency Judge Molesworth acknowledged, a
couple of months ago, in effect, that he would have to
take some time to find the true solutions of the innumer-
able problems with which the Act bristles. But, ye gods !
what a magnificent Eldorado this Act will be for the
lawyers who are fortunate enough to have wealthy and
unscrupulous clients, game enough to go bung on liabili-
ties (say) £lOO,OOO and assets nil.’ To an outsider, it
looks exactly as though the new Act had been specially
framed for the sustenance of the numberless * gents one ’
who, for lack of clients, are now acting as ,i hers on the
Stock Exchange or as backers of horses J. ponies at

A curious will was the subject of application to
Mr. Justice Cooper t’other day in Brisbane. In the afore-
said will 1 -tator had left spaces for the amounts of
several legacies and the names of the legatees, but had
never filled them up. The only persons named in the
will were the executors, and one of them having declined
to act, the whole of the testator’s property, £6OO, fell to
the other.
• * * *
The U.S. Commission which President M'Kinley
appointed to go across and endeavor to secure Europe’s
attendance at a bimetallic conference having failed
utterly, the ’Murkan silver party is now going bald-
headed for independent action, and Bryan (according to
present arrangements) is to run for the Presidency on the
ticket. Moreover, Senate elections recently inspire the
silver party with hope. Barring Moreton Frewen and a
few others, however, English bimetallists stand out for in-
ternational action or none at all.
* • * *
Up to about ’93, Britain had practically a
monopoly in the production of the world’s tin-plate. In
that year, owing to the protection afforded by the M'Kin-
ley tariff, the industry was started in the U.S., with the
result that last year they turned out about 450,000,0001b5.
of tin-plates, a - 45 p.c. advance over the production of
’96. The mills in England and Wales are suffering even
to the point of extinction.

“ E. & 0.E.” pours out his wrath :
Dear Bulletin.— “Q.’s” splenetic outpouring about
Australian artists as a body, is clearly the outcome of a
bias against individuals. His little tilt at mannerisms
bewrays him a dweller in Philistia, else would _he recog-
nise in the Beardsley fringe an effective sornmier 'gainst
the axe of criticism, and appreciate the wear-prolonging
effect of a large, flowing bow on an already-dimmed
dicky. “ Q.” prates about lack of “ pluck and patience.”
Has he ever tried subsistence for weeks on one meal a
day ? —bananas specially recommended for cheapness and
filling-out properties. Artists don’t advertise their daily
menus, but when in reminiscent mood one may hear per-
haps of a prize-taker who has lived for two years on
apples and brown bread—object, to save enough to get
’Ome; or of another who never dared stay in school
at lunch-time lest his stomach should cry out and shame
him. Such minor matters as clothes, tobacco, and an
occasional beer are better left to professional secresy. As
for rent, the genuine art student never pays rent. “ The
high and mighty pretend that it is beneath their dignity
to live by their art,” says “Q.” What about such one-
time art students as Longstaff, Fox, Mackennal, Bunny,
Quinn, and Oates? Didn’t they live by their art ? At any
rate, they existed. And the Little Billees exist. Also, they
exist by journalistic work. “Q.” conveniently overlooks
the difference in age and experience between Minns,
Streeton, or Spence and the students of whom he is a
self-constituted critic. Let him count how many local
openings there are for black-and-white work. The Bul-
letin and perhaps Punch. Also let “ Q.” picture Little
Billee anxiously waiting publication-day with consolatory
assurance that if he hasn’t got a drawing accepted his
spare time next week will be- mainly occupied in taking
up the slack of his belt, and then spread his gall in the
direction of so-called leading weeklies which crib from
English papers instead of encouraging local talent. “ Q.”
growls “ because so few of our artists produce any pic-
tures,” and this after exhorting them to subsist on jour-
nalistic work ! Does “Q.” know that success in black-
and-white can only be obtained by unreinittent applica-
tion ? Will he name a first-class artist living by journalistic
work (no limit as to country) who produces pictures
otherwise than spasmodically? “Q.’s” facts are not
facts, and his arguments are self-contradictory. The
artists are all right. Alsu, when the artists complain of
their lot it will be time enough for “ Q.” to criticise. At
present their greatest hardship is that, lacking cash for
models, each in turn has to sit for, and be caricatured by,
his fellows. This means no studies from the nude during
winter.
Curious to think that not the faintest echo
of the tremendous Dreyfus hubbub can have
reached the unfortunate subject thereof in what is
practically his grave —jealously guarded from the
least communication with the outer world, never
hearing a voice except in command, and con-
demned himself to perpetual silence.

TWENTIETH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 26th April, 1898.
The directors have pleasure in submitting to shareholders the annexed balance-sheet and statement of accounts to
31st March, duly audited and certified. ni
The net profits (including £2,523 Os. 5d., balance brought forward from last half-year) amount to £6,|87 14s ot
which the usual dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum will absorb £2,250, leaving a balance of £4,13< 14s. to De
Ca ‘ l The*business continues to be well-maintained, notwithstanding the severe competition in rates. The cash reserves,
it will be observed, are unusually large, but this is only a temporary feature in connection with exchange
and there is no superabundance of unemployed funds; in fact, the directors have no doubt they can e -
tageously any deposits entrusted to them, and they renew their hope that shareholders will not lose anj 11
of introducing business to the bank either in the way of deposits or advances of a desirable character.
By order of the board.
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

Viator: “Mention of the laid-by ship
Sobraon—now a reformatory in her old age as a
penance for the sins of her youth—reminds me ot
the delicious little music-house she had on deck,
with its lovely Soufleto piano. Oh, what a scat-
tering of millions of microbes when it rippled, on
a moonlight night, and the bulwarks were lined
with couples watching the top-masts gently rake
the stars. We wanted no ballets, no operas, no
plays. Donizetti’s Elixir of Love was on tap all
the time. What a boundless capacity of loving
there is in the human soul ! It extends to all
womankind, all mankind. How well, after all the
years, I remember the Sunday morning on which
the obliging little piano led off for—
The arms of love that compass me
Would all mankind embrace.
Ed. Bulletin. —You ask, “Are singers
better pushers than painters?” They are ; their
stupendous conceit gives them more confidence,
but the essence of musician-worship is its sim-
plicity as an art. Anybody can rave over a sonata
or a song. You only have to say it is ‘ lovely,
or “exquisite,” or “sublime,’ and nothing more
i 3 expected of you, even if you profess to
“understand” music, whereas with painting an
outsider can’t talk for five minutes without
putting his foot in it. Another point is the fact
that the singer is seen in all the glory of his long
hair, while it is only the work of the painter (tor
which the average art-affectationist cares nothing)
