POLITICAL
POINTS
A metaphorical marvel from Sydney D.T.
headlines : “Mir. Reid in a Nutshell.”
Q. Labor-members are one by one repudi-
ating “Socialism in our time.” Their cry is
now, “ Office in our time.”
G. H. Reid is the twentieth who has held
the office of Attorney-General in N.S. Wales since
the institution of Responsible Govt, in 1856.
“ Well,” said one prominent S.A. Tory to
another lately, “ y >u may abuse Kingston as you
hke, but he is the only man in politics with the
pluck to say ‘ No ’ to the extremer demands of the
Labor-party. Downer would have given them
everything they wanted.”
A SURE SION.
(Time, midnight.—Melbourne Town Hall.)
SIMPLUS: “I wonder if she’s a decent woman?”
NOWE M: “ No, I’m hanyed if she is.”
S.: “ How do you know?”
N.: “ She’s not ashamed to show her face!”
Poor Liberty ! Though the organ of a
“National” association which lately recognised
the necessity for inter-provincial federation within
itself, it knows that its ultra-Tory supporters are
the strongest' enemies of Australian Federation.
In its dilemma, it frankly sits on the rail.
“Newspapers should be carried free of
postage because of their State-recognised educa-
tional value.” But, then, Sydney’s wealthiest
newspaper opposes free education itself, not
realising that if an aid to education be free the
prime article itself should a fortiori be free.
Brisbane Courier welcomes Byrnes as Q.
Premier on the ground that if he cannot succeed
in keeping the party in office, he will at least
make an admirable leader of Opposition. Opposi-
tion to whom ? Does the Brisbane daily view the
advent of a Glassey Ministry with equanimity ?
The “organ of the landed interests of
N.S. W.” has issued a programme—abolition of
payment of members, electoral reform (N.S.W.
has been too free altogether with the franchise, it
seems), protection of the producer, abolition of the
land-tax—and (Labor members and their sup-
porters please note) opposition to “ this Federal
mania.”
Anti-Federationist Snooks, M.L.A., boasts
he can now command space by the column in
Sydney D.T. Before he set out to slay Federa-
tion with his strong breath, the D.T. compressed
his best efforts into “ Mr. Snooks also spoke ”
just as it would say “Balaam’s ass made a few
remarks.”
Hancock snaps off with Trenwith. The
rift between Tren with and Vic. Labor Party began
even before he started for England. T.’s worst
grievance is, doubtless, Hancock's abler manage-
ment of the party during the jaunt. Hancock has
all that Trenwith lacks. But, then, Trenwith has
all Hancock lacks.
Barrister E. M. Lilley, notable only as
the son of his father, and as a successful Brisbane
advocate, has joined Groom and Drake in another
attempt to rally the Q. Opposition forces. Young
Lilley has none of the old man’s subtlety, fluency
and wit, but he is forceful, dogged, and above
trifles. He should make himself felt in the arid
region of Mongrelian politics.
S.A. Advertiser wavers in its opposition
to Federation, and editor Bonython seems to con-
template a quiet somersault while there’s time.
Opposition to Federation is very half-hearted in
S.A. Even Labor members are wavering, and
the great mass of farmers support the new Con-
stitution. S.A. Convention delegates have decided
to stump the country in favor of the Bill.
It is stated, on apparently reliable legal
authority, that if, in the close season, a wild duck
be shot on one side of the Murray and fall dead
upon the other, the offender can be penalised for a
breach of the Game-laws by the authorities of
both N.S.W. and Vic.— i.e., punished twice for
the same offence, contrary to a main principle of
common law all over the empire. If this be so
and the authority is emphatic—there’s another
argument in favor of Federation and an all-round
code.
Vic. Government evidently did a wise
thing when it didn’t appoint a Judge on the Law
Commission. Quite a number of witnesses had
dreadful things to say about the Judges—their
age, incapacity, deafness, red - tapeism, their
hideous slowness, the way they shirk work and
responsibility by hanging up all difficult points
for any number of months for the consideration
of the 1 ull Cout, and the like. And they could
hardly have appeared before a Commission con-
sisting largely of his ’Onah to tell his ’Onah what
a failure he is.
The spectre of A. G. Taylor, which still
perambulates occasionally at night down the
columns of the Australian Star, gibbers hor-
ribly about the way the other provinces are
striving to “crawl back” into union with the
mother province,” which they deserted 50 years
or so ago. Every Federal movement up to date
has been started by N.S. W., so if Federation and
crawling are synonymous terms, then it is
N.S.W.’s exclusive crawl. But a small fact like
that doesn’t stand in the way when the maddened
ex-giraffe of Mudgee gets fairly started.
The anti-Federal meeting, held at New-
castle (N.S.W.) by Dick, M.L.A, with
“cheers for majority rule and free speech.” When
the hederal Amending Act was before Parlia-
ment Dick voted for an arrangement by which
Federation was not to be carried unless it got at
least 120,000 votes, being an absolute majority of
the N.S. W. electors on the roll. If 125,000 people
voted for federation, and one person voted
a<iainst it, the fair thing, in Dick’s idea, was that
the one person should win. Think of the ghastly
in>ny of cheering Dick in the interests of majority
rule !
Ed. Bulletin. —Really, you mustn’t speak
as you do of Allan M‘Lean. To most people he
certainly is a pawky Scot of no particular account,
but-at Maffra, his electorate and Gippsland home,
he is what Ewen Oameron is in his district—only
a step lower than Jehovah. Allan M'Lean, of
the firm of that name, who handles thousands of
cattle and sheep yearly : Allan M‘Lean, big share-
holder and director of Maffra sugar-beet factory,
simply bosses that part of Gippsland. That is
where they swear by the stock-tax and the extra
sugar-duties, and where the anti-Federation vote
will be heaviest.—F.C.
John Haynes announces that he and a
large number of ot,her N.S.W. politicians are pre-
sently going to raise Gehenna by demanding the
abolition of the income-tax and the abolition of
the. land-tax exemption. John thinks it a fair
and reasonable and democratic thing (1) that the
man who has =£sooo a year derived otherwise than
from land should not be taxed, and (2) that the
man who struggles to'make a bare living on 150
acres of barren soil should be. Also he says that
he detests “ class legislation,” and that “ true
democratic government rests but on this founda-
tion—one law for all.” ~ Johnaynes’s idea of “ one
law for all ” is unique. '
.The astute R. E. O’Connor made a curious
slip' when addressing the members of Sydney
University, in favor of the Convention Bill, last
week. Edmund Barton, who preceded him, spoke
in an academical tone, suitable to his audience.
Mr. O’Connor, however, started off with a detailed
list of the N.S.W. rivers, sources, courses and all,
and was considerably startled, at the conclusion of
his elementary lesson in geography, by being
saluted with a most enthusiastic cheer from his
audience, which comprised all the shining lights
of the ’Varsity. The gentle “Dick ” was at first
evidently at a loss to account for the tremendous
demonstration which greeted him.
REAL DISTRESS.—“OUT OF THE
MOUTHS," &c.
“Poor father's dead two years, and mother's got a
baby three months old!"
SIC TRANSIT.
11.30 p.m. Cabby and dude wrangling over fare.
DUDE: “ What! Seyen-and-six to drive me to
Bondi! Preposterous. Is there no other
way of getting there? ”
CABBY (driving off in the dark): “ Yes; yer can
go out by the blanky sewer if yer like !”
Melb. Argus now refers to it as the
Commonwealth Bill. The .Tory organ was wont
to shudder at the word “ Commonwealth,” but its
ancient prejudice has yielded to the respect due
to the last syllable.
Among the five Premiers, Kingston, of
S.A., is the only one who seems to be putting his
whole big soul into the Federal movement. The
next best man in the crowd is probably Fysh, of
Tasmania; he may be taken as representing
Premier Braddon, whose ill-health makes active
campaigning difficult. John Forrest is favorable
in his phlegmatic way. Turner, of Victoria, is
distinctly favorable, but, so far, he takes the whole
business in the same unemotional way as he might
take lib. of tripe, and Alfred Deakin is jumping
his claim. Reid has dropped out of sight since his
announcement that he will give his personal vote
for the Bill, and it is doubtful if he will even
speak on the subject again.
Benevolent Bull again ! —with the usual
anti-climax :
LONDON, Monday Night.—Since the completion of
arrangements for the lease of Wei-hai-wei from China,
British prestige has been re-established in Pekin, the
disinterestedness of the British Government being recog-
nised.
Great Britain has been conceded territory’ inland from
Wei-hai-wei extending within a radius of 10 miles.
Lormer, the arid, white-headed boy of
Melb. Age, on Federation :
On the Protectionist aspect, of the question I realise
great danger hecause no provision whatever is inarle to
safeguard Victoria’s policy of Protection. If compelled to
break down all interprovincial harriers we have not the
slightest guarantee that our storehouses will not be filled
by industrial invaders from America, Germany, or even
Japan. ... We are likely to lose the substance while
grasping at. the shadow. . . . We may, therefore, assume
that the first Federal Parliament will contain a large pro-
portion of Conservatives and Freetraders who will' plorv
In sweeping away our Protectionist policy.
The Bulletin suggests that Lyn'e, of N.S.W.,
who is dead sure that the Federal Parliament will
pde on a £9,000,000 protective tariff, and Lormer
should be shut up together in a large room with an
axe apiece, and left, to argue the matter out.
Apparently the N.S.W. Ministry has
dropped the Federal question overboard. Premier
lieid since his half-hearted announcement that he
will personally vote for the Bill, has kept a dead
silence, and it is now understood that he washes
his hands of all responsibility. Minister Car-
ruthers spoke once in favor of the Bill—that was a
good while ago—and then he also dropped out.
Old Sunday Brunker, Garrard, Cook and Young
keep the dull blank silence that best fits their
miserable intellects. Just-Ice Minister Ghould
goes further and says Federation is “ outside poli-
tics ’’—politics being a subject that concerns itself
exclusively with borrowing, and culverts, and
new gaol-flogging regulations. All these medio-
crities claim to be in favor of Federation, but not
one will give the cause which he allegedly admires
a helping hand.
An attempt is being made in Perth (W.A.)
by the lower-class Chinese to establish a
branch of their infamous Yee Hing
or secret society, which seems to be
a robbery -of - everybody - for - the-
benefit of-members organisation.
Its field of energy includes the
securing of billets for mem-
bers of the society, and
any Chinese cook, house-
servant, or gardener
outside the pale is lucky
if lie escapes the machi-
nations of the Yee
Hing. Salt will be sur-
reptitiously introduced
into the soup-pot,
stolen a rticlesconcealed
amongst his belongings,
and even more serious
crimes imputed to him
with the object of ac-
complishing his ruin.
The head men of the or-
ganisation live in clover
on the proceeds of the
levies made on the mem-
bers, and the Chinese im-
perial authorities regard the
Yee Hing as so dangerous
that to be proved to belong
to it entails, capital punish-
ment. The Chinese merchants
of Perth, knowing that the Yee
Hing, if established there, will
blackmail them, are appealing to
Government to prevent its regis-
tration as a “ benefit society.’’
The average country editor,
whose paper is not his own or partly his own, finds
the coming of a general election a time of
trouble. If he be docile, or will meekly
stand down and let the mortgagee or local
candidate for Parliament, or the representa-
tive of either, seize his quill, he may outlive
the period of trial, but if not, a letter will be
despatched to some big city newspaper office
saying, “Can you send us up a good man on
trial for a month or two?”
John See is in favor of a daily Hansard ;
W. J. Lyne, of newspaper-postage ; Henry Cope-
land, of publishing Govt, advts. in some Govt,
publication ;andtherank-and-fileof N.S.W. Protec-
tionist army is in favor of amalgamating Hansard,
Govt. Gazette, and Agricultural Gazette and
issuing the combined production, with Govt,
advts., at a nominal figure. It is the vision of
State combines of this sort which makes the hair
of metropolitan dailies stand on end, and inspires
them to curse socialism.
The wailful Fysh wrestled with federal
finance before an audience (of 10G) at Hobart, the
other night, but was not convincing. He seemed
to know that he hadn't been successful, so waved
the old flag of war before the affrighted eyes of
the Tasnianiacs: “Tasmania wrest federate. If we
stand ou«J the other provinces will put a cordon
of war vessels round the island. Then where will
our commerce and insular freedom be?” It was
a ludicrous sort of a howl, but it stirred things up
a little, and made Tasmania open one eye in its
sleep.
“Been There,” to The Bulletin
To be a N.S.W. Labor-member is not the coinforfame,
soul-refreshing billet the inexperienced outsider regards
with watery mouth. If that unfortunate politician sticks
to the apparel and habits of the class he is supposed to
represent he hears himself referred to by brother-legisla-
tors as a vulgar coster. If he dons a shiny belltopper, the
crowd that claims to have elect d him dubs him a snob.
If he wears a gold chain to support his gold railway-pass,
he is said to have sold his vote to the enemy. If he rides
free on a tram, he is called mean ; and if he pays for a cab,
he is said to he jaunting luxuriously at the expense of
the workers. If he enters a public bar, he must shout
beer for all hands, drunk or sober ; if hedip into a private
bar, he becomes as odious as if he wore two shiny bell-
toppers. Tramps, deadbeats, and their wives and mothers
find out his house, and hammer at the door as part-pro-
prietors. If he lips them 11, he must go to the Benevo-
lent Asylum ; if he doesn’t, they remind him that another
election is near, and that hard graft will then be hia
portion. Altogether, his road is peculiarly rooky and his
end mostly pieces.
According to Gilbert Casey’s latest letter
to Bamanaland, New Australia is at present run
on mixed principles—sort of socialistic-individual-
istic ’arf-and ’arf. Six leagues of the latest land-
grant are held communally ; the other is divided
into blocks of 105 acres each, titles in the name of
each adult male, widow, or spinster. Gilbert,
who was once a particularly red-hot Communist,
talks thusly :
I have selected, and am the proprietor of, the whole of
Las Ovejas, including the township and cultivation, my
associates having selected all round.
Casey’s Queensland tirades against every variety
of landowner would have made your blood run
cold. Further, he proceeds, in genuine landed
pride:—
Do you remember, I always said Las Ovejas would be
mine? And it is so!
New Australian history runs thus: From Com-
munism to Socialism; Socialism to land coin-
munalisation ; land communalisation to land-
lordism.
Glowing with knowledge of true worth,
Far-reaching arms he stretches forth
To bless the weaker ones of earth,
The Heavy Father of the North ;
And should he borrow, beg, or steal,
Why do they murmur?—none the less
He does it for the general weal
In pure disinterestedness.
T.D.

“Pattie Browne flaps her wings early in April at
Sydney Cri. in 1 Little Minister,’ now drawing packed
houses here,” writes Thb Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent. “To the Covent Garden masquerade ball, that
lugubriously English imitation of the ‘ bal de l’op6ra,’
P. B. went as a housemaid, and never gave the dust a
chance to settle. This entertainment is kept alive by
the theatrical wig and costume makers, who pot-hunt for
the ‘ best-oressed character ’ prizes, and the wild, riotous
abandon—within limits—is supplied, as at the ‘ Moulin
Rouge,’ by recognised females under managerial conces-
sion. Sometimes an amateur takes a hand, though, and
then the fun is lively, though uncertain.”
Theorbo : “The first man I heard sing‘Elijah’ wasmasJJ
sive, long-bearded Waller, of Sydney. What became of
him ? He was the only Elijah that ever looked like
Elijah. Pauli and Santley sadly needed make-up. Far-
quharson sang it well. The original was Staudigl, a
German.”
Theorbo: “ I happened to be right at the far-east,
portal of Melb. Exhibition, Easter Saturday night, when
Albani sang, ‘ I know that my Redeemer liveth.’ A
tremendous distance, yet every word rang as clearly as
w.heu she made the glass of Sydenham Crystal Palace
tingle with her timbre in the same song before an in-
numerable crowd.”
The Druids Gala band-contest, at Melb. Town Hall, was
remarkable for the unwisdom of the Railway Military
Band from Sydney in not agreeing to divide first and
second prizes with Coade’s Melb. Band, after the judges
had given a “ dead-heat.” When the contestants came
to play it off there was seemingly much the same doubt
as before, but if so, the local men got the benefit of it,
and the visitors lost the difference between £7O and £llO
—not to mention the half-share in a £5O trophy.
English papers speak of the “ cordial reception which
awaited Miss Ada Crossley ” at the Harrison concerts in
Sheffield. “ The popular contralto’s ripened voice,” it is
stated, “delighted everybody.”
P.: “ Gov. Brassey, according to a Bulletin par., can’t
stand Rebottaro’s ‘Simon the Cellarer.’ Neither can I,
and Ive been wanting a chance to say so. Rebottaro’s is
with variations ; as, e.g., the ‘ ho, ho, ho ’ (rhyming with
‘no, no, no’) becomes‘ha, ha, ha, ha’—a great many
‘ ha, ha’s ’; I didn’t count them. It jars.”
The world has heard of the magnificent sentry who re-
fused to allow Napoleon to pass against his own orders.
He turned up at one of the exhibition gates for an Albani
concert and stopped the two carriages holding Albani and
co. “ Well,” says baritone Pauli, or some one, “if we
don’t go in there’s no show ! ”
Melb.: “We impatiently await Walter-r Bentley’s
Passion Plaj r . Of course his old schoolmate, Pastor
Mar-r shall, who was so enthusiastic over Walter’s .Silver
King, will be to the fore again. Ah, never will I forget
the Savvbath morn when Bentley and co. turned up at
Marshall’s Collins-street kirk, just this once, to celebrate
the eternal unity of Church and The gentlemen
in freshly-reaped blue cheeks, the ladies under flower-
gardens. The pastor, in return, put on a slouehy billy-
cock, and found the gods were really quite well-behaved
persons ! ”
Theorbo : “ A brass-band contest has been gratifying us
m Melb. Such critics as Melb. and Sydney ought to keep
up a large town band, of the stringy type, for popular
education in music. Even Paris had nothing regular of
the kind till about 30 years ago, when Pasdeloup formed
his orchestra, followed by those of Lamoureux and Habe-
neck, showing that a vein had been struck. These three
rival conductors gave splendid openings to young com-
posers, such as Massenet, St. Saens, Lecocq, Planquette
and Aud ran, and did an inestimable service by familiarising
the Parisians with Wagner, besides burnishing up the
treasures of their own great Berlioz. In Australia, this
would be the means to keep us up to date.”
Dear Bulletin.— Still harping on the subject of Mum-
mer Morality. Rickards ought to know a little about it,
and what does he say? “ Never let your donah go upon
the stage, unless you want to bring some sleepless nights
on : I tell you, as a pal—you’ll be sure to lose your gal,
the moment that she puts the bloomin’ tights on.” —
Chorus Singer.
Maggie Moore's season in Adelaide was such a big suc-
cess that they ad ltd an extra six nights to the original 10
ere moving on towards Perth. Good accounts are given
of Maggie’s new farcical comedy show “The Prodigal
Father,” with which she hopes to score in Sydney and
Melbourne anon.
The one impressive feature of the Druids’ procession in
Melb. on Easter Monday, was a scrumptious girl who
occupied a lofty position as Druid Queen.
Dear Bulletin.— A recent “ Sundry Show ” par. about
“ Two Little Vagabonds ” (on tour) stated that the Misses
Titheradge and Terriss were somewhat unconvincing as
hoys, and Miss Hughes was “ pass ible.” I saw that show
in Hobart. The Titheradge and Terriss girls are two very
fine human boys, and the man who isn’t touched by the
scene between Miss Hughes and those two waifs is either
a silly cynic or a bad lot.— Randolph Bedford.
Melbourne Liedertafel, with Marshall Hall as conductor,
has elected Elsie Hall as hon. life member of the society,
“ in recognition of her distinguished services at the 255th
concert, and as an expression of admiration towards her
as a young and talented Australian pianiste.”
Little Gulliver is the only remnant of Williamson’s one-
time attempt to get up the pantomime of “ Gulliver’s
Travels.”
Melb. : “ Turning up the vols. of the Nuova Antolouia,
Italian magazine, in the Public Library, I find Albani
starring in opera, through Italy, in 1872. Eheu ! ”
A Melb. paper points out that, as regards the “ Babes
in the Wood,” the programmes mention everyone con-
cerned in the production, down to the wigmaker and
bride-cakemaker —all except the author.
The amateur, or semi-amateur, tenor is usually a mild
and feeble actor who hopes to concilitate the audience
with his next solo. The Don Osesar at one recent show
was a slap-dash sort of young man, slinging his bits of
dialogue off his chest quite regardless of the dictionary.
He didn’t trouble to put all the syllables into such a word
as ignominy. “ Ignomy ” was good enough for him. And
when Don Jose invited him to sign an agreement to leave
Madrid he replied in a hasty, ten-minutes-for-luncheon
style, “Gimme penninkanpaper— 'tis done.” All the
young men in the audience appeared to like him im-
mensely. Typical Australian native brand of Don Caesar
—sort of musical-footballer kind of bloke with no stuck-
up pride about him.
Melb. : “A ventriloquist, in the Eastern Market, is
better than Maccabe, Winton, or Emmett. Clean-shaven,
he stands close to the people, without moving his lips,
and the voice actually seems to come from the dummy.”
“ One out of every ten of the actors and actresses recog-
nised as such by the Actors’ Benevolent Fund (Lon.) had
to appeal for assistance last year,” says Reynolds, com-
menting at the same time upon the ridiculously small
wages upon which chorus-girls are expected to lead
decent lives. A miserable wage causes immorality, and
immorality begets thriftlessness in those girls who are
able to advance in the purfeshun. The evil is not easily
cured. Irving set a good example to managers years ago
when he produced “ Corsican Brothers.” The super-
numerary girls engaged for the bn! masque scene were paid
£2 a week each. At that time there were two naughty
oyster-shops, one on either side of the dress-circle en-
trance to the Lyceum, and the £2 a week girls went to
the oyster-shops more than ever. As an inducement to
virtue the liberal salary was a failure.
What Adelaide has been choking itself
over lately. Q. : Why won’t the Chief Justice be
happy in his married life? A. : ’Cause she’ll
always have her own Way, and he’ll always have
the Blues. N. B.—To properly grasp that tremen-
dous joke, remember that several young Blues go
in his Honor’s parcel.
“Charley” Collins, member for Narrabri
in N.S.W. Assembly, is dead, to the deep regret
of a very wide circle —for few men had more
sincere friends. As a politician, he was just.about
as valuable as 75 per cent, of his fellow-members ;
as a man, a genial, wholesome, good-hearted
citizen of the world, he will long live in the
affections of all who knew him. Mr. Collins was
a son of Israel, and his fine physique and habits of
self-command seemed to promise him a patriarchal
age. Yet he died suddenly of brain-effusion at
little over 40 years of age.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Harry Knight, the well-known Sydney owner
and driver of trotting horses, has flown into print, in.
which he states that N.S.W. owners are not so much to
blame for the decline of trotting as defective handicap-
ping. He points out that at Bathurst Show his horse,
Harold 8., ssecs. behind Silver Cloud in a mile, was
beaten by that mare, notwithstanding which defeat, at
Orange, the following week, Harold was placed 20secs.
behind the mare. Silver Cloud again won, and, when the
pair were handicapped for another race, they were put
on the same marks. Harold won his mile trot in Bathurst
in 2’34, and Silver Cloud in 2’36, so Mr. Knight rightly
considers Harold should only have been 4secs. behind the
mare in two miles. In a race at Brighton, referred
to by Mr. Knight, in which his horse was placed on the
ridiculous mark of 20secs. behind in a mile, Kathleen was
“thrown in.” Everybody saw it, speculation was killed,
and she won with her driver holding a watch in his hand.
That sort of thing kills sport.
* * * *
The All-Aged Stakes at the A.J.O. meeting on
Thursday last proved what a good horse the Sydney Cup
winner, Merloolas, is. Mr. Wilson’s crack, Bobadii, and
Merloolas, entered the straight both under ihe whip, the
former with a two-lengths’ lead, and with the flails going
all down the straight Bobadii, with every ounce out, won
by a length and a quarter in lmin. 41sec. Mr. J. Northern
gave the “ books ” a much-needed turn by winning the
City Handicap with the 15 to 1 chance, Flodden ; and
Amberite, as expected, won the Cumberland Stakes easily
from old Battalion. On Saturday, Merloolas, weighted
at 9st. 21b., added to his winnings by carrying off the
Place Handicap from True Blue, who ran a good race, but
was unable to foot it with the country gentleman.
Mirella was the only one Amberite had to beat in the
A. J.C. Plate, and he did it comfortably, winning in smin.
39sec.—6sec. within the time allowed by the club to
entitle the winner to the whole stakes. In the Final
Handicap The Chief had the race in hand and slowed
down, when the experienced Harris saw his chance, and
bringing Vedette along with a rush, passed The Chief on
the post and won by a head. Johnson, who rode The
Chief, will probably be more careful in the future.
* » * *
Hordern’s colors, which had quite regained
the favor of the crowd since the Skipper affair, received
a sad set-back at the A. J.C. meeting. Gunbearer started
the shivoo by going out a 2 to 1 favorite for the First
Nursery Handicap, and being handled with sufficient sus-
picion to allow of his jockey being sent up for a “ sixer.”
On Thursday, in the youngsters’ race, Miss West was
made a hot favorite by the crowd, and ran nowhere ; but
on Saturday, when sevens went begging, she had appa-
rently recovered form and came out and won the Second
Nursery Handicap. N -edless to say, the crowd were not
pleased, and they showed it unanimously.
• • s •
The disqualification of jockey Cleal over the
riding of Gunbearer in the First Nursery Handicap has
caused a good deal of comment in racing circles. If
deal’s punishment was for an unpardonable error of
judgment, well and good; but if for a deliberate “pull-
ing” of the colt, as it appeared to the onlookers, then the
case is one for further enquiry. Cleal, by the way, was
first jockey to the stable, and a clear result of his dis-
qualification is that the stable cannot ever again employ
him.
* * * *
Onkaparinga (S.A.) club’s Easter Monday meet-
ing was a big success. This club, which only races once a
year, provides a £lOOO steeple, collared this year by
Songster, a Victorian horse. The Millers sent Whernside
all the way from Ballarat, but weight told, and the great
horse could only struggle second.
Bookies were almost entirely absent from Onka-
paringa meeting. £21,000 odd went through the machine,
netting the club £l5OO in commission. Tote arrange-
ments greatly impressed chairman Miller, of Vic. R.C.,
who was present, and he went back to Ballarat a stronger
tote-advocate than ever.
• * • *
Jerry Boland, who died, the other day, in Mel-
bourne, used to run the “ arch-medium ” sweeps on the
big races. One time a woman—a Jewess—held the
winner of the Cup. She learned of her luck late in the
evening, and instantly ran off to interview Jerry. She
was told to come next day, and actually sat on the front
door-step all night. When she got her cheque she fell
into a fit of hysterics. And when she got the money she
opened a public-house. “So ye see,” said Jerry, “ she
wint to the divil entirely.”
* * * •
Dear Bulletin.—A suggestion to “ Tattersall.”
#n next big race let him have two sweeps —one to be
drawn before the race, and one on the day of the
race, and advertise the fact thoroughly. Then the public
could show’ him which they wanted most. —John Smith.
* * * *
Before the days of the “tote” in Maoriland, a
bookie made a heavy volume against a horse which the
owner had solemnly assured him was “ dead.” When the
race started the “stiff” was going great guns, and held
a commanding lead as he passed the lawn. “ Here ! I
say 1” yelled the bookie; “what are you doing there?
You’d no business there, you know.” The crow’d grinned
extensively. The “ stiff ” still held the lead coming up
the straight, and Ikey nearly fainted. But it was all
right. The dead ’un lost cleverly by a head. “ Well, by
—— ! ” said he of the proboscis, as he wiped his fevered
brow’; “no more dead uns for me.” And he w'ondered
why the crowd laughed.
* * » *
Holdar M. : “ The efforts of the law to cope
with the gambling instinct range between the sublime
and the gorblime. The police lately laid hands on the
mi d and honest poker-machine, which has been allowed
to -eke undisputed root for years past. Yet out at the
Agricultural Show’, the other day, writer called a con-
stable’s attention to a spieler w’ho, with the ‘ buttoner’s’
eternal aid, was ‘ raking it in.’ The law listened lan-
guidly, slowly cleared its throat, and told this scribe that
there were about two dozen distinct varieties of swindle
in progress round the ground, and as it was without
specific instructions it couldn’t interfere. So the confi-
dence men pursued their gentle art w’ith impunity right
under the law’s nose.”
* * * *
Edmund Tattersall, longtime ruling partner in
the horse-sale business founded by Richard Tattersall,
w’as 83 when he died last month. He W'as the instigator
of the prodigal champagne lunch in connection With
blood-stock sales, and he lived to see the price of fashion-
able yearlings increase from hundreds to thousands under
the influence of gold-top. Also he was originator of
“ three cheers” for the “ plucky sportsman ” who makes
an exceptionally extravagant purchase. How’ well he
understood human nature !
• * » *
Footballers in Vic. are gnashing their teeth
because the lacrosseurs are playing nearly all their
matches, on enclosed grounds this season. Next season
the lacrosse men reckon they’ll charge for admission, the
public will roll up to see a clean, pretty game, and—good-
bye to football profits !
* • * *
One who has seen the Canadian game well
played tells The Bulletin that Sydneyites have practi-
cally never witnessed real lacrosse. The game the
Sydney team played in Melb. last year was a hybrid-
h’oekey-shinty-rounders sort of sport, and the said Sydney
boys were the first to admit it.
• * • *
“The cinematographic pictures of the Sydney
test-match displayed at the Alhambra,” writes The
Bulletin's London correspondent, “are capital for
realism and liveliness. The Bulletin’s hint re the blow-
sounds of the Fitz.-Corbett fight has been utilised, and,
when Ranji hits a ‘ fourer,’ the smack is duly thrown in.
The opening picture is announced as ‘ arrival of the
Governor,’ and depicts Brassey entering Flemington from
the ‘ straight.’ But the ballet-ites don’t know any better, .
and applaud just the same.”
“Daimio, vanquished by transmigration, is to
return to Australia to end his days as a midnight cab-
horse,” w’rites The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“ Etnmalea, owned by Hibbert, a well-known front-rank

the best-bar none—exponent of modern glove
methods) needs little description. There was nothing in

Jeffries landed heavily on the body, and then knocked
Peter clean off his feet with a left on the jaw. From this
out to the third round, when the referee mercifully gave
the fight in favor of Jeffries, the once mighty Jackson
merely stood up to be knocked down again. The pity
* * * *
“Kid” M‘Coy, the Yankee boxer who made
such short work of Australian Dan Creedon, is a true
modern fi Alter as regards newspaper work. He reckons
that the modern fighter wants the tierce aggressiveness of
Sullivan, the lightning quickness of Corbett, and the
stolid fore-arm punches of Fitzsimmons. Good old Peter
Jackson, when at his best, has been the nearest, so far, to
M‘Coy’s ideal.
* * * *
The president of the great French cycling body
—the Touring Club—has been warned that unless Zola is
struck off the list the 3000 military and naval members of
the club will resign en bloc.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.— “ Sporting Notions,” 5/3/98,
contains a statement that I venture to traverse “ The
artillery authorities assure you that to make a sphere thus
alter its course is a mathematical impossibility.” Is it ?
Well, the said gunners are different from those of ’6l to ’69.
It is a fact, unexplainable I admit, but still a fact, that a
rifle-ball always curves to the side of the twist. Hence
the tangent-scale sights of the Armstrong gun. Col. C.
H. Owen, prof, of artillery of the R. M. Academy, had a
theory of rolling in the air. Other high authorities of the
day had others. I (passed Ist in artillery) had, and have,
another still. Now, if a cylinder, revolving on its axis,
does not go straight, but deflects in a curve, whose equa-
tion is not yet determined, why should not a sphere,
which has an affinity of axes, not deflect as it pleases, or,
rather, as bowler Noble chooses? There’s some scientific
reason, and it must be connected with the density of the
resisting medium in which the projectile is moving. I
shall think this out and explain it in pamphlet form.—
Scotty tub Wrinkler. P.S.—W.T.T. is not seized of the
conditions of the “ curl in the air.” The billiard busi-
ness is due to friction on the cloth, but the deflection of
the Armstrong shot or shell and the cricket and base-ball
curve is due either to friction of the resisting medium in
transit or to some other source. Again, the curve of a
projectile in a resisting medium is not a parabola, but a
parabolic curve. In a vacuum it would be a pure para-
bola.—Scotty.
• * * *
Going to the first Coolgardie race-meeting, a
remittance-man, whose “ flush " periods were short and
merry, and a bookie with a talent for “ owing,” travelled
in the same compartment. A discussion arising between
them auent the resoective merits of two nags, the remit-
tancer backed his favorite to beat the bookie's for £5O
“ where they finished.” The owner of the bookie’s fancy
heard of the wager, and advised him to get Out of it for
the reason that he (the owner) might not be “ chasing it”
at Coolgardie, and he would be sorry that any previous
arrangements of his should prevent bookie having a show
for his money. “ Oh, don't you worry about that,” said
the book ; “ I don’t suppose he’ll ever pay me, and I’m
quite sure I’ll never pay him,.”
* * * *
The winner of a foot-race in Maoriland lately
was protested against for false entry. When the com-
mittee met to consider the matter he was asked if he
would swear an affidavit that he was not “ Jack Robin-
son,” a man of good performances. Swear it ? Of course
he would ; Bill Jones was his name. He was warned that
he would be prosecuted for perjury if he swore to a lie,
but he said it was all right, he was no liar. But in a fit
of absent-mindedness he signed the affidavit “ J. Robin-
son,” and he thought it unkind when the committee rose
as one man and “ hoofed ” him into the street.
* * * *
Jack Green, the Humber-rider, hails from New-
castle-on-Tyne. Jack has, up to date, put up 30 birth-
days without a hitch, and is still up to speed business
with his Dunlop-tyred mount on the cycle arena. Jack
will have a cut in at records shortly upon the English
Crystal Palace track, according to all accounts.
* * * *
Jack Green, the English cycling champion, who
left for his native land last Tuesday, is probably one of
the wealthiest racing men of to-day. During the 14
months he spent in Australia, he cleared nearly 1600 in
prizes and drew another £lOOO in machine and tyre allow-
ances. Green has been a successful racing cyclist for 15
years, and during the last five, as a pro., has been winning
all along the line. In addition, he has earned a good
salary as sec. of Elswick Cycle co., so that the who!- of
his prizes and allowances are clear profit. Green comes
from “ canny Newcastle.”
* • * *
Ken Lewis, the 10-mile Australasian champion,
made his d6but at Kyneton in ’72. Ken wasn’t aware of
the wheel industry at that period, but was amazingly
active with his feet as an infant. When Ken arrived" at
manhood’s estate and mounted his Dunlop-tyred Humber
the youthful characteristic became so particularly aggres-
sive and profitable that heaps of trophies and cash-prizes
fell into his possession.
* * * *
A take-down that went wrong. A sporting
writer had published something “ he didn’t oughter” in
the opinion of a trainer, who resolved to get even with him.
The trainer had charge of the favorite for a coming big
race, and three days before the event he sent an urgent
message to the newsman. “The favorite had broken down ;
come out and see him.” When the reporter arrived the
trainer had the horse’s foot in a bucket of hot water.
“ Just giving - a gallop, and he put his foot in a hole.
Have to scratch him.” The scribe rushed avvav to squeeze
in this important item, and the trainer chuckled with a
great and exceeding chuckle. Immediately afterwards
the horse was taken for a gallop and did put his foot in a
hole, breaking down hopelessly, and everybody agreed
that the sporting writer was fairly up-to-date.
A smoke-concert was held at Sydney German
Club last Thursday in aid of the Dudley Colliery
Disaster Relief Fund. A high-class musical pro-
gramme was laid on for the occasion, interspersed
with recitations, “lightning sketches,” conversa-
tion in German and English, and refreshments in
all languages. Who would not drink “bier ”in
so good a cause! The fund will be at least £2O
the richer from the event.
Melb. Argus surprised everybody by
poking vigorous borak at the recent amateur
swashbucklers’ sliivoo at Ballarat; whereat Age
correspondent Lambie waxed exceeding wrath and
Majaw Reay, of the ’Erald, chipped in a few
remarks about the military spirit here not being
strong enough to stand such a strain, haw. The
Argus man described how an impregnable mul-
lock-heap held by mounted riflemen was taken
possession of by the spectators, headed by a
“young lady in a pink silk blouse.” After'the
battle, a detachment of riflemen was found squirm-
ing along the ground in a far paddock and blazing
away at the imaginary enemy until an officer
informed them they were blanky fools not to know
that they had been all slain hours before. Like
Pat Malone, the hero of Johnny Coleman’s song,
they didn’t know they were dead.
At the annual conference of the Australian
United Commercial Travellers’ Association, at
Brisbane, when “ The Press ” was proposed the
mover took occasion, amid uproarious applause,
to compliment the South Queensland press on its
“ purity.” The Victorian and N.S. Wales papers
(it was alleged) were “filled with ‘sweep’ and
‘ guack ’ advertisements,” thereby offending the
virtuous souls of chaste commercial travellers.
Just fancy a gathering of drummers thanking the
Lord the press was becoming purified ! And, by
the way, the complimentary drummer was sar-
castic, though unconsciously—for no one ever
looks for mental subtlety in a bagman. The S.Q.
press may be “pure,” but that is because it has to
be “ pure.” The Law has washed it. It is a bit
left-handed to compliment anyone on having been
compulsorily washed.

ot the tunny-man that is setting in amongst the

humorous and mirth-provoking joke, even the
si esp i mg nigger-gag, is being freely inter-
spersed through the sermons with great effect,
And by reason of the contrast with the otherwise
dull and solemn surroundings almost any decently
good joke becomes inexpressibly funny when deli-
l e rV!iT PU i plt ’ • 5- tachurc h this scribe
attended last bunday night the most obese and
most profoundly respectable male saint, who
never before was heard to make any noise during
service except emphatic amens and violent and
pious groans whenever the pastor mentioned the
oven, was so tickled by the humorous discourse
that, after bravely struggling for some minutes to

lau g hter. It sounded as if Peacock had collapsed

herd to fax a glare on the offender that struck him
like the shock of Gideon s hosts, and knocked him

tfaud. then, when we had regained our com-
posure, the good man told us some more jokes for

“The reason I dislike Ranji,” writes
“Mummer ” to The Bulletin “is because he is
rr^^AdT 0 !:^^^ Britisher - wl r
if land ® d ln Adelaide he would not be interviewed,
WOl V d not th ® toadying press a line of
C ? P , y :he anted 5t all for ‘ friend ’ Stead the
® toler )' g° ods purveyor and literary ‘fence
,gq ° r \ ng the fact * hat > but for th e press, which
\ gets cp) "’ n . on its baggy knees and worships his
bogus Frinceship ’in every paragraph, he would
never be heard of outside the field where they play
the game of ‘Nigger with the stick.’ He.takes
the first opportunity, however, to assure the public
that he is an arden't Britisher ; his uncle, too, is
bravely fighting his Empress’s battles in India;
that is, said uncle was ardently murdering his own
countrymen in defence of the ‘ rights ’ of a white
robber and invader; and Ranji is proud of the
renegade. Of course, one does not expect much
brains in a man who devotes a lifetime to lash-
ing out with a club at a ball thrown at
his legs by another fellow ; but, surelv, cricketers
should be grateful to the press which boosts
the dark man’s game to a heroic altitude and
wastes whole columns debating whether the
‘ checker ’ ‘ chucks ’ or slings the ball, or the bats-
man snicks or glances it. Is there any other case
m the world’s history where such an insignificant
combination as an Indian, a bat and ball, has
been raised to such importance ? Besides, Ranji
is not the best, even at the idiotic game ; one man
on his own side, and two or three on ours, have
outshone him; but he is dark-skinned, and so
appeals to the affections of a verv large class of
Australian women. The Georgia minstrels, with
their monkey heads and thick lips, had just as
great a charm for our white women in years gone
by—so the women’s homage does not go for much,
But, such as it is, combined with the press, it
made the Ranji boom.”

One well-advertised but very amateur
swashbuckler proved himself an absolute martinet
at Rookwood (Sydney) encampment. During
manoeuvres by his tired and thirsty squad, a
subordinate officer had the temerity to give some
order, whereupon the haughty field marshal (or
something) turned upon him and ferociously ex-
claimed—“ How da-ah you give commands when
I am present, sir! ” But the gallant amateur’s
effort to ape a seasoned militaire hadn’t enough of
the “blanky” ring about it to seem convincing,
and onlookers merely guffawed and advised the
junior officer to “punch ’is jore.”
Write “ The Heavenly Twins " :
Like Montgomery’s Thurston, we too once had our
beautiful theories re the Child of Nature, and Becke’s
first yarns made us yearn to dwell where our chief worry
would lie in futile attempts to restrain the deliciously im-
moral advances of the black-haired maiden of the sensuous
curves. But we’ve learned a little since then — not much,
it's true, for we’re of the class who need to be taught by
experience, and even then we have to fall over a thing
several times before we realise it exists. But we have
learnt, and now we would gleefully' pawn our Waterbury
to aid a fund for the propagation o'f fatal diseases among
every colored race on earth—for our united knowledge em-
braces all other types of color, the Kanaka having wrecked
the last remnant of our dying belief in the alleged
virtues of the Child of Nature. In the aforementioned
days of our verdancy, we viewed the Q. Kanaka through
the eyes of Westerners, and, while denouncing the fact
that the nigger ousted the white worker, the main stock
of our spleen was directed at those responsible for that
fact, our feeling being one of pity that he should be
decoyed from his island-paradise to be educated in the
meanest vices of a rotten civilisation. But now, we think
that Queensland is the fittest place for the nigger, and
that the islands should be inhabited by the weary white
man. In short, we would recommend that every colored
person on earth should be dumped into Queensland, and
so leave the really decent places (“ the green fields afar
off ’) to folks that could appreciate them. We don’t
blame the Kanaka for his laziness—we are somewhat
“tired” ourselves—but we detest him for his smell—
especially in wet weather. For wasting, not using,
water in drought-time the average new-chum is
an old Western-stager compared with the guileless
Kanaka. For filthiness in the tucker line an alli-
gator, with its preference for putrefied corpse, is com-
paratively' squeamish. The Kanaka’s love of home is
“ all our eye.” He prefers to be a “ slave ” where he can
get drink and loaf on his weaker mates. And for mean
■villainy and lying and treachery—only 7 modified bv his
wooden-headedness and distrust of his mates—the Kanaka
beats the Javanee, and the missionary-boy tops the pile
every time. The Kanaka is better off in Queensland
than at home. He is better treated than the white
man being paid sick or well (he mostly is sick, for he
lies up for the least “tired” feeling); he mustn’t work
in the wet, mustn’t be sworn at, or punched, under
penalty of £5. His pay is better than that of many a
cookie’s laborer in N.S.W. ; his food is better, and quite
as varied as the tucker on a Western station, and he
mustn’t be worked over 10 hours a day. When his time is
??i a fi" ee Passage home (and must be provided for
it k j vesse l can take him), but his repugnance to the
alleged “ slavery ” is so great that he mostly'refuses to
leave it. As for the “ island maiden,” we never met her,
but, if she resembles the specimens of the sex we’ve met
in Queensland, we can cheerfully endure the disappoint-
n) ' el K t ' , We have developed lately a strong sympathy
with the Dutchman, who sits severely' on his nigger;
with the chartered companies who are stealing whole
countries from the black man ; and we want to pat the
head of the juvenile Boer who learns good marksmanship
at the expense of the casual African.
LITTLE GLFFEN.
Out of the focal and foremost fire,
Out of the hospital walls as dire,
Smitten of grapeshot and gangrene
(Eighteenth battle, and he sixteen !),
Spectre such as we seldom see,
Little Giffen of Tennessee !
“ Take him and welcome ! ” the surgeon said ;
‘'Much your doctor can help the dead ! ”
And so we took him and brought him where
The balm was sweet on the summer air ;
And we laid him down on a wholesome bed—
Utter Lazarus, heel to head !
Weary War with the bated breath,
Skeleton boy against skeleton Death,
Months of torture, how many such !
Weary weeks of the stick and crutch I
Still a glint of the steel-blue eye
Spoke of the spirit that wouldn’t die—
And didn’t! —nay, more ! in death’s despite,
The crippled skeleton learned to write !
“ Dear Mother,” at first, of course, and then,
“Dear Captain,” inquiring about the “men.”
Captain answered : “ Of eighty and five,
Giffen and I are left alive ! ”
—American.
A Ballarat Chow was run over by a lorry,
half-smothered in slurry, and caught in a fall of
dirt, all in one day, and next morning he turned
up to work as usual. Ah Cat has nine lives.
Delcomyn :—
When the details of the stranding of the China come to
hand, it will probably turn out that lovely woman was,
indirectly, the cause of it. Not the new woman, but- the
old, old woman, Eve’s own daughter. From the state-
ment made by the good Bishop of Melanesia, it would
seem that one of the usual entertainments was in full
swing when she drove on to the rocks, as the ladies were
sent ashore in evening-dress. The Bishop, as he
was neither a dancing man nor a flirting man
seemingly, saw the reef, but, not being in charge
of the bridge, was not able to stop the steamer.
Nearly any captain of a large passenger steamer
will tell you that he’d rather carry convicts than a saloon
full of women. He may be adamant himself, but it pla3 r s
the devil with his officers. Girls seem to think they have
a special license when on a long voyage, and a right to in-
vade every portion of the ship, the bridge included, no
matter how they impede and hinder the navigation, and
risk the safety of the ship by taking off the attention of
those on duty. The only place safe from them is the
engine-room. The “steamer-hack” is beginning to be
as well-recognised a type as the garrison-hack of the old
days. Some captains hold out and chance their popu-
larity, others are bamboozled. Let the new woman set
about teaching her giddy sea-going sisters sense.
But we never heard of a woman on the bridge of
a P. and O. steamer —nor of a P. and O. officer
neglecting his watch for the sake of a woman.
When you come to think of it, the company’s luck
has been marvellous. Which is only another way
of praising its discipline.

A ferryman at one of the Brisbane ferries
told the present scribe his hours were 12 a day for
the whole seven. He had only missed three days
in four years, through “dengue” fever, and his
pay—4s. fifd. a day—was stopped. The muni-
cipality farms the ferries out—a municipality run
largely by a set of glorified hucksters.
Dear Bulletin.— How would Philipia, or
Philipland or Philippi do as a new name for
N.S.W. ? Surely appropriate in that Philip signi-
fies “a lover of horses.” Then a few thousand
years hence a legend would gather around us that
Paul’s epistle to the Philippians was written to
comfort the primitive lags of Botany Bay.— J.F.P.

INVALIDS
AND
THE AGED.
Reaping.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a week in the year when your troubles
never trouble,
When you’re up with the sun and a-working at
the double,
And there isn’t time to grumble as you slip along
the stubble
On the old twine-binder in the morning !
Then it’s turn out, boys, to the cleaner
by the heap,
For the stack’s a-growing bigger, and
there’s plenty more to reap.
Oh! the world seems better when you’re
in waist-deep
On the old twine-binder in the morning !
You may talk of the times when the concertina’s
playing,
When the boys give a spree at the shanty where
you’re staying,
And Matilda Maud’s a-blushing at the things as
you’re a-saying
Like a rose what never has a thorn in !
But there’s whips more fun at the cleaner
by the heap
When the birds begin to warble and the
day begins to peep ;
Oh ! your life’s not long enough, and joy
seems cheap
On the old twine-binder in the morning !
There’s a day in the week when you’re finished all
but carting,
When the last bushel’s bagged and the teams are
all a-starting, '
And you seem to lose a comrade when the minute
conies for parting
From your old twine-binder in the morning!
So it’s good-bye, chaps, to the cleaner by
the heap,
For we’ve got to leave the paddocks to the
milkers and the sheep,
And the rooster’s a-crowing and the dust
lies deep
On the old twine-binder in the morning !
NIL.
The “ church with a chimney on it ” is an
accomplished fact, and can be seen in Thomas-st.,
Ultimo, Sydney. The chimney is no architect’s
dummy either, but a real working smoke-stack,
15ft. high. An inscription on the foundation-
stone of the edifice, which is just completed, states
that the building belongs to the “society of the
New Church.” Is the New Church a culinary
cult?

HEALTH IN ; THE BOTTLE.
“ D.C.L.” Scotch is a delicious and finely
blended whisky of excellent flavor, which, while
maintaining all the due stimulating properties of

fectly wholesome and mild beverage.*
Harry de Windt, the well-known traveller,
describes Chilkoot Pass, over which so much of
the traffic to the Klondyke goes :
The passage of the Chilkoot is generally made by night,
as the soft, deep snow is then in firmer condition. The
outfit was despatched at 3 p.m. to await us at Stonehouse,
about 2000 ft. below the summit, where a halt is made to
gather strength for the real struggle. By 10 p.m. all is
ready for a start. We have no mountaineering parapher-
nalia, and are armed simply with three stout wooden
staffs cut that afternoon, but these are discarded long
before the summit is reached. Passing the silent white
tents of our sleeping companions, we enter a dark and
narrow defile that becomes steeper and steeper as the
Pass is approached. The trail is rough and stony and
intersected by numberless streams, while tree-stumps,
gnarled roots, and tangled brushwood occasionally bring
us down headlong. Presently the forest becomes less
dense, and patches of snow__appear on either hand. An
hour later we take our first rest, drenched with perspira-
tion, at. Stonehouse, a rocky ledge overhanging the first
of seven or eight snowy “plateaus” that must be crossed
to reach the foot of the actual peak, which is itself nearly
1000 ft. high. The Chilkoot cannot really be called “a
Pass.” It would he considered a dangerous mountain in
Switzerland, and a question of guides, ropes, and ice-
axes. Stonehouse is the limit of trees. A huge granite
rock, shaped something like a human dwelling, suggested
the name. We found our Indians huddled up under
this, sheltered from, an icy wind that whistled through
my fur jacket as though it were muslin After a brief
halt, we pushed on, ascending a steep ridge until we
stood on the first ice-plateau. The travelling here was
much easier, and we went, cautiously ahead in Indian
file, two “Siwashes” (Indians) in front, two in the rear,
anil ourselves in the centre. And yet this portion of the
ascent is, perhaps, the most perilous. This “ plateau,”
like all the others, was broken away beneath by numerous
watercourses, and was simply a kind of crust, suspended
15ft to 20ft above the ground. Had there been fir-t.rees
below we should have been standing on the upper
branches ! There was absolutely no path or trail to guide
one, and huge crevasses where the snow had fallen in
upon some foaming torrent appeared at frequent inter-
vals. We progressed but slowly, for our guides probed
the snow carefully at every 'step. They knew that a
break through would mean certain death. These plateaus
increased in steepness uritil, midway up the last,
we had thrown Away our sticks and were scram-
bling painfully on hands and knees. It was necessary
to scratch holes in the snow with our fingers to
gain any ground. The Indians, with their heavy
packs, used short knives for this purpose, but stopped
every few moments to regain'their breath, for which I
was not sorry. It was impossible to rest for more than a
few moments, for to let go would have meant a fall of
perhaps a couple cf hundred feet to the foot of the slope.
The Bishop of Alaska has described this portion of the
ascent as “ hair-raising” work, and he does not exag-
gerate. To make matters worse, a thin drizzling rain
now fell, which chilled us to the bone, and made the
?oing even worse than before. These plateaus appeared
40 be oval in shape. Each terminates in a kind of nar-
row antechamber formed by enormous boulders. These
gloomy portals, which were passed with some difficulty,
recalled Dorb’s pictures of the “ Inferno,” and the out-
ook when we emerged from them on to another almost
perpendicular wall of ice, was not a cheerful one.
The scene was one of utter desolution. Here and
here, below us, masses of black rock dotted the
white expanse, like islands on a sea of snow ;
while overhead towered the grim spectral peak of the
Chilkoot, rendered still more vague and terrible by a
rapidly rising mist. This presently grew so dense that
further progress became impossible. Scaling the rocky
ridge that encloses the last “ plateau,” we descended into
a kind of cavern, which, though open to the rain, afforded
Home protection from the cutting blast. Here we managed
to light a smoky, spluttering fire, over which we shivered
until the grey dawn partly dispelled the fog and enabled us
to resume our journey. We soon reached the actual base
of the Chilkoot, and here hard work commenced in grim
earnest up the granite face of the mountain. The dis-
tance from our camping-ground to the summit is barely
000 ft., but the ascent occupied nearly two hours. There
8, of course, no path, nor would it be possible to make
one, for the rocks are loose and insecure, and the passage
of a man will often send a boulder crashing down, to the
‘eadly peril of those below. In some places it was
lecessary to squeeze round the wall of the precipice
*n narrow ledges of rock that trembled underfoot,
.nd threatened to dislodge and send one whirling
through space into eternity. The last 300 ft was like
scaling the walls of a house. With ropes and proper
•ppliances, the passage of this mountain could be made
ar easier ; but it was, under the circumstances, such ex-
lausting, heart-breaking work that I more than once had
erious thoughts of turning back. Finally, however, at
•bout 4 a.m., we stood on the summit, breathless, bleed-
og, and ragged, but safe. My aneroid gave the altitude
as 3620 ft above sea level.

One lady of the Sydriey pavemeht pursues
her calling (thus described because it consists sd
largely in calling out “ ducky ” after the male
passer-by till he stops; and calling out “blanky”
after him if he doesn’t stop) on a bicycle. Also
she carries ah elegant card-case. When she sees
the affluent-looking male lingering at the street
corner, she deftly steers her wheel alongside him,
dismounts with a giddy hop, and presents the
pasteboard. It reads—
, MadamE smitM,
WomAn or Loose Character.
Melbourne City Bench struck d new kind
of rogue the other day. His name was Jones, and
when asked if he had anything to say for himself;
he cleared his throat; and in respectful and well-
chosen language informed the court that he was a
bad lot who was already Serving sentences for
previous iniquities; and, said Jones, “I cannot
in decency ask your worships to cjeal leniently
with me.” Jones further remarked that a mari
couldn’t expect to go robbing add rogueing at
large, and playing the deube with law and the
decalogue; and, then throw himself on the mercy
of the court; he had hot, he said, the cold cheek
to do it. Then Jones subsided, add took his twO
years like a man, and marched out with the proud
consciousness of having thoroughly deserved all
he got.

Mahlstick to The Bulletin :
“Many will recall having admired Charles Summers’s
little clay statue of Pan, at the entrance to Melb. Public
Library,reading-room. It is about 31ft. high, and Red-
mond Barry loved it, as afl emanation of Summers’s
Original genius. Pan, with a most expressive face and
gesture, squeezes drinking water out of a bag, under his
arm. What a contrast, this, to the slop of Summers’s
colossal sitting figures of Albert-, Victoria, Wales, and
Alexandra downstairs, done hv the disgusted artist for the
money of Baronet Clarke. But Pan has just been taken
off his perch, arid tossed aside, as lumber. The conse-
quence it that two nasty chips have been knocked off
him, and the attendants are evidently going to Aunt Sally
him out of recognition.”
The game of . the newspaper is always
to make War. Anyone with the slightest
touch of the press knows that an ounce of War is
worth a ton of Philanthropy, and very much more
than anything else. Wolf ! wolf! The cable-
grapher’s bit of War is as indispensable as the
“You here!” of a melodrama. Whenever the
newspaper gets hold of a real danger, for which
“ wolf ” has flattened the public appetite, it does
everything to enlarge the sore, salt the wound,
and make peace impossible. The big papers of the
globe want to get their correspondents at work in
the gory shambles. Then those who cannot afford
to hire theaspiring Forbeses, Stanleys and Yillierses
are glad to be the little belligerent fleas ad in-
Jinitum.
In Victoria the orthodox barrister and
solicitor has a hideous loathing for the “amalgam”
—the matt who practises under the new Act as
both kinds of devil. The amalgam takes his
Client’s base straight into court; instead of telling
your solicitor all about your grievance against
Hooligan, aiid paying him to listen to you, and
then paying him to relate it all to another mail,
and paying the other man to listed to the first
man, and then paying him to tell the Judge about
it, one man does it all, which saves costs and
trouble. Lately barrister Topp got so enraged
that he declared before the Law Commission that
“only men who had been failures in ohe branch
or another practise as amalgams.” Now the
amalgams, who include such men as Fink,
Gaunson and Matthew Davies, want to find out
some way of falling on Topp for about a million
in libel damages.

Alice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was away when it happened, so he heard
nothing of the gossip which flew from tongue to
tongue in his native township. His sister could
have told him in her letters, but she didn’t, and
because he had no other correspondents he re-
mained in ignorance.
He did not remark the absence from his sister’s
letters of theonce-familiarstatements, “Alicewas
over last night and we had some music,” or “I
met Alice at church to-day,” or yet again “Alice
sends her regards.” At first he had wondered at
these omissions, and pictured some girlish quarrel,
and then, in the midst of the city world, it became
merely as an incident that is lightly touched in
passing.
But, going home for the Christmas holidays on
the box-seat of the coach, the driver told him,
coarsely enough, as some men tell such things ;
and he knew why Alice’s name had been dropped
from his sister’s letters.
He asked for details. “ How does she take
it?”
“ Oh, easy enough ! Seems as if she don’t care
a rap. Looks to me as happy as ever.”
Then there was silence, and the man saw himself
walking by her side away from the merry dancers
in the schoolhouse by the road—homeward across
the paddocks in the moonlight, her eyes glancing
up into his, her touch upon his arm, and her face
dimpled with rippling laughter. Two years ago!
Later on, in his bedroom, the thought returned
to the man and made him sleepless. “If he had
known, that night—if he had only known ! ”
In the morning the thought was with him still.
He turned his steps up the hill and down the other
side. The narrow path, since he last followed it,
had changed into a tiny watercourse, but the gate
creaked on its hinges just as it had creaked that
night when Alice was so late and tried to open it
softly. He remembered how they had both
laughed at her failure.
She was not much altered, he noticed now, as
she placed a milk-dish on the bench and came
towards him, wiping her hands on her apron.
“ When did you come home, Walter? Father
is down thtS garden.”
“ I came to see you,” he answered, looking at
her strangely.
“ Did you ?’’ she rejoined, smiling. “ I haven’t
many visitors now.”
lie ' made no reply. The idea that he had
nursed all night was uppermost, and he could
think of nothing to say to her.
At-length she said, “Will you come inside?
I m -just clearing up,” and he followed her into
the kitchen: silently. She placed a chair, but the
baby, sitting cm a mat, caught sight of him and,
holding out its little arms, called “Da-da!”
Suddenly then the mother picked it up and held
it close to her. It was the first time it had uttered
these words, and she burst into tears.
The man stole softly out again through the
creaking gate, and over the hill.
DARGO.
Retribution.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM a coward ! From boyhood I had known
the disgraceful truth, and had resolutely thought
it hidden in obscurity. But to-day it came out
into the broad daylight, and paraded itself before
my eyes in naked brutality.
It was a lovely summer day. The sun shone
hotly upon the water, as, standing on the quay, I
watched the attack of a tribe of sprats upon a
piece of half-submerged bread. Near me three
little girls were romping and laughing. No other
person was about.
The bread had been devoured, and the fish had
disappeared. With a yawn, I turned from the
spot. There was a sudden splash. I missed a
curly-haired girl, and che other two children were
clinging to my knees, beseeching me pitifully to
save poor Annie.
Poor Annie !
The sweat rushed out upon my forehead, and a
hand of ice seemed on my heart. I trembled vio-
lently and did not move.
A second time the scream of the drowning child
rose above the feeble splashing. The cry lingered
in my ears, indefinitely prolonged. With an oath,
I cast the two children from me, and rushed out into
the roadway in a horrible race with Time, but the
third and fatal scream seemed to paralyse my
limbs.
I hurried away, away, I knew not whither. I
chanced to enter a park, and, throwing myself
face downward, I listened breathlessly, fearfully.
Splash—splash ! I heard it a thousand times.
“ Murderer ! ” I cried aloud.
A city vagrant, close by, jumped to his feet, and
ran off, crying “ Police ! ”
The fool!
* * * *
We met by the merest chance. Her pretty
black eyes had looked into mine ; and the maddest,
merriest love that ever spurned the senses danced
us off in a whirligig that might have ended in
“ Tears, idle tears, my dear girl.”
“ I cannot help it. Say you never will leave
me.”
Thus love had tricked poor Daisy. Love,
with all its follies and extravagance ; love, with its
bitterness —and clinging arms around my neck, as
though the girl were drowning.
“ Don’t leave me, George, don’t leave me ! ”
I could put the question off with a kiss easily
enough, but a promise hurts my throat.
“ You don’t love me now, George.”
I was thinking deeply. The question made me
angry.
“ No, I don’t,” I said.
And flight suggested itself in a dozen different
ways. I grasped at an idea, and made up my
mind. I would leave secretly for Melbourne.
For Daisy it meant ruin, I knew, but I was. con-
tent to go through life with a troubled conscience
rather than marry her.
She wept, and spoke about her sister Annie. I
caught at the name, and the horror of the story of
the child that I had let drown before my eyes was
like the tightening of a rope around my neck.
They were sisters !
The fatal splash, rung out by every bell, by
every sound that woke me in the night, was back
upon my ears just then. The breaking of the
spray a hundred miles away seemed to flick upon
my cheeks—to blind my sight, and there came a
horrible dream of a ghostly girl who walked the
waters seeking with uplifted hand to point me
out.
“ Spare me ! ” I cried ; “ I will save her.”
The ghost-child smiled, and dropped her hands.
I caught the sister in my arms and kissed her.
“Daisy,” I said, “let us get married.”
And, looking through the mist that dimmed my
eyes, I scarcely knew whether it was Daisy or
Daisy’s sister who smiled radiant upon me.
Whoever it was, she was a very happy girl.

A successful plaintiff in a recent Australian
breach-of-promise case lately conferred her hand
and the amount of the jury-awarded damages on
a fresh sweetheart. That gentleman has now dis-
appeared, leaving the hand but holding tight the
“damages.”
In a Sydney merchant’s office :
A. (the merchant) : Do you know what I think
you are ?
B. : Can’t say, but your expression indicates
that it’s nothing complimentary.
A. : It isn’t. I think you’re a d d rascal.
B. : Then, mj' dear sir, I have the advantage of
you, for I know you are.
In at least one N.S.W. constituency the
ensuing Parliamentary election threatens to de-
velop intense personal antipathies. The wives and
daughters of the voters on the roll of one district
are to be informed by circular of the morals of an
ostensibly religious candidate, at present the
sitting member, in the hope that votes shall
be thereby influenced. The MS. of one of
these circulars was submitted to a city printer in
the course of last week, and 10,000 copies ordered,
but the printer, dreading libel, dropped the job.
The recent hop-beer prosecutions in Mel-
bourne cast a flood of light upon the temperance
curse. Analysis showed that the proportion of
alcohol in the “temperance drinks ” under notice
was larger than in “colonial” beer. During all
these long and weary years this valuable informa-
tion has been confined to a little coterie which has
been having a high old time on ginger-beer and
dandelion ale, and branding as a moral outcast the
man who straightforwardly looked at the sun to
see if it was yet eleven o’clock.
London weeklies of the inane Snappy
Snips order, occasionally do strike something
original. Pearson’s Weekly published diagrams
of the feet of the Queen, Prince of Wales, and
others. Why this particular issue was not seized
by the police is a mystery. Her Gracious is shown
to have “ plates of meat ” shaped for all the world
like the south island of Maoriland, and one of
“ Tummy’s ” feet has a large number of treason-
ably irregular valleys and ridges in its north-west
aspect, so to speak. This latest phase of Cockney
journalism is calculated to do irreparable damage
to the cause of loyalty throughout the Hempire.
Weather!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gold in the Klondyke and Yukon country ? I
should smile ! See my nose?—you chumps think
drink made it that color, but did you ever know a
man’s ears go blue from drink ? Yet my right ear
is the same color as my nose. That’s frost-bite,
and it was got in that same Yukon country. You
Tasmanian West Coasters think you know a lot
about severe weather, but let me give you a bit
of my experience in the Hudson Bay Co. service
collecting skins from the depots from Alaska Bay
(belonged to Russia then) down to the Skeena and
Naas rivers.
We were anchored off Fort Yukon ; the Indian
summer was over, and there had been a heavy
fall of snow during the night, which gave us a bit
of trouble to shift in the morning ; however, when
’twas shifted and things straightened a bit, the
skipper told the purser to serve out an extra gill
of rum. Now it was the ship’s cooper’s work to
get the cask of rum out of the spirit-room onto
the half-deck and pump the rum from the cask
into the grog-tub; and if ever you saw
a man look foolish, ’twas our cooper when he
got the bung out and found the rum frozen into a
solid block. The “skipper” was taken aback
too until he had a look at the “ themomoter,” but
presently he slewed round to the purser and
ordered him to find the cubic measurement of a
gill of rum, and serve it out in the solid. After a
little calculation the purser declared that lg-inch
cube would be the quantity; so the cooper knocked
the cask to pieces and sawed the block of rum
down longways with an ice-saw. Then the two
segments were cut with a tenon-saw into as many
inch-and-seven-eighths cubes as there were men,
and we took our rum as kiddies do their sweets.
This went on for five or six days before the ship’s
fitter remarked to me that the cubes were getting
very small, he thought." I thought the same, so
next day he put his callipers on his cube and
found it only lf-inch. We were being had for a
|-inch of rum, and we determined to watch his
nibs cut up the next cask. Well, there was the
cooper with a half rip-saw with a set on it that
was sending out rum - dust as thick as Yukon
snowflakes, and as he had a tarpaulin spread
under to catch ’em—you see, he scooped all the
saw-dust! No wonder he was drunk every day.
After that, we stood the rum-cask in the galley
for three or four hours, to thaw before serving out.
Yes, it is cold up Yukon way !
YANKEELAND.
The Maoris believe that lawyers, when
they die, turn into pelicans. Yes, they can gobble
up anything, and hold it undigested for a low
time.

A very pleasant “At Home” was given by Miss Lin
Wills, of “ Sunflower Cottage," Beeac, to a few friends i
Friday evening, the 18th March. Amongst those preseni,
were Mr. and Mrs. Harvey, Miss Brennan (“ Juba Villa ”),
Mr. Ainscough, Mr. and Miss Tatchell. The refresh-
ments, which were on a most lavish scale, were specially

the company present. The “At Home” was given in
honor of Miss Wills’ change of residence.

Bile Beans for Biliousness.
STRONG PRAISE FROM A MELBOURNE MAN.
SUFFERED FOR MANY YEARS FROM ACUTE BILIOUS ATTACKS.
UNABLE TO ATTEND TO HIS BUSINESS.
MANY DOCTORS AND VARIOUS MEDICINES UTTERLY FAIL.
BILE BEANS COMPLETELY CURE HIM.
In the person of Mr. F. J. Boustiere, of 67 Toorak-road, Melbourne, one finds a splendid specimen
of the French people who was born in Paris some 45 years ago. Mr. Boustiere is a well-known ladies’
coiffeur, of South Yarra and Toorak, who has patented a very successful article. well known as
“ Lotion Infallible.” This gentleman for a number of years suffered
from severe chronic biliousness from which he was unable to obtain
any permanent relief whatever, although many doctors were con-
sulted and patent medicines taken in endless variety. In Mr.
Boustiere’s case, as in nearly all cases of biliousness, there were
violent headaches, indigestion, bad breath, loss of appetite, and
many other more or less severe symptoms which usually accompany
attacks of this sort. On being asked for details of his case, Mr.
Bostiere said :
“ For years I had scarcely any appetite, and every morning on
rising from bed a dreadful fit of nausea, accompanied by violent
vomiting and headaches, attacked me with unerring regularity.
My throat felt parched, and a yellowish fluid was emitted from the
stomach. This continued for years. Naturally enough, my busi-
ness suffered to some extent, as a feeling of lassitude and drowsiness
prevented my devoting much energy to it. My attention was called
to Bile Beans by seeing in the papers one or two cases of biliousness
which had been cured by them, and I decided to give them a trial.
No sooner had I begun to take them than immediate relief was
experienced. Shortly afterwards the morning vomiting ceased
altogether. Then my appetite regained its former satisfactory state,
and the whole system was toned up. Thanks to this excellent
remedy, I am now able to throw myself with renewed energy into
my business. My wife, who has been a chronic sufferer from various
digestive ailments for over twelve years, has also been cured by this
wonderful remedy. Her experience was almost exactly similar to
mine, only, as her case was of much longer standing, the cure was
not effected quite so quickly. She is, however, quite well now, and rapidly regaining weight which
she lost during her long illness.”
Mr. Boustiere, true to the characteristics of his race, is never tired of singing the praises of the
wonderful remedy that has done so much for himself and his wife. He has the fullest faith in the
efficiency of Bile Beans, and never misses an opportunity of letting others know that there is a remedy
now on the market that will absolutely cure the most obstinate cases of biliousness or other digestive
troubles. - -- - - * ■ • ' ■ .....
